☸️ Kubernetes CKA — Certified Kubernetes Administrator

Complete Learning Guide with anihpj Django Project (jobpost app)

From complete beginner to CKA-certified Kubernetes administrator. Learn cluster architecture, installation, workloads, networking, storage, security, maintenance, and troubleshooting — all through hands-on examples with a real Django application.

🟢 Beginner Friendly ☸️ Kubernetes v1.31+ 🐍 Django / Python 📚 37 Chapters ⏱️ ~50 Hours 🎓 CKA Exam — 100% Coverage 📋 7 Appendices

🚀 Your CKA Learning Journey

🎯 Welcome! This guide takes you from complete beginner to CKA-certified Kubernetes administrator. Every concept is explained with real examples using the anihpj/jobpost Django project — you'll learn by doing, not just reading. Covers all CKA exam domains: Cluster Architecture (25%), Workloads & Scheduling (15%), Services & Networking (20%), Storage (10%), and Troubleshooting (30%).

🟢 Part 1: Foundation Chapters 1-4

Chapter 1: What is Kubernetes? — Why Do Administrators Need It?

Before diving into cluster builds and config files, let's understand why Kubernetes exists, what problems it solves for operations teams, and why the CKA certification focuses on the platform itself rather than just deploying apps on it. We'll use our anihpj/jobpost Django app to make every concept concrete.

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~1.5 hours 📖 Concepts 🎯 Foundation

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ The problems that exist before Kubernetes — manual scaling, 3 AM crashes, single points of failure
  • ✅ What Kubernetes actually is — the simple, clear definition
  • ✅ CKA vs CKAD perspective — why administrators own the cluster layer
  • ✅ Kubernetes core concepts — clusters, control plane, worker nodes, pods, desired state
  • ✅ The Kubernetes ecosystem — CNCF, CNI plugins, Helm, GitOps, service mesh
  • ✅ How companies of different sizes use Kubernetes in real production
🎓 CKA Relevance
Cluster Architecture 25% Foundation ALL DOMAINS
Understanding "Why" Matters on the CKA

The CKA is a performance-based exam (2 hours, 15-20 tasks in a live terminal). While Chapter 1 isn't directly tested, understanding why Kubernetes works the way it does helps you troubleshoot faster and reason through unfamiliar scenarios. Key concepts to internalize:

  • Desired State vs Actual State — K8s controllers constantly reconcile these. This explains why the control plane exists and why etcd is so critical.
  • Self-Healing — If a node goes down, K8s reschedules pods to healthy nodes. The CKA tests your ability to manage this.
  • Control Plane Components — Understanding what the API server, scheduler, controller-manager, and etcd do is directly tested on the CKA (25% of the exam).

1.1 The Problem Before Kubernetes

Imagine you've built anihpj — a Django app with a jobpost module where employers list jobs filtered by Skills, Location, and Author. It works perfectly on your laptop. But how do you run it in production so thousands of users can access it reliably?

📖 Key Term: Container Orchestration

Container orchestration is the automated management of containerized applications across multiple machines — including deployment, scaling, networking, and availability. Kubernetes is the industry-standard container orchestrator, and as a CKA administrator, you are responsible for building and maintaining the orchestration platform itself.

The Evolution of Deployment

Software deployment has evolved through four major eras. Each solved problems of the previous one — but introduced new challenges:

🖥️
2000s
Bare Metal

One app per physical server. No isolation. Massive resource waste. anihpj would need its own dedicated box.

❌ Wasteful & rigid
💻
2010s
Virtual Machines

Multiple VMs per server. Better isolation. Still heavy — each VM runs a full OS. Slow to provision.

⚠️ Heavy overhead
📦
2015+
Containers

Lightweight, fast, shared kernel. But managing hundreds of containers manually across servers? Nightmare.

🟡 Almost there...
☸️
2018+
Kubernetes

Orchestrated containers. Auto-healing, auto-scaling, zero-downtime deploys. The admin builds and maintains this platform.

✅ Production-ready

The Scaling Problem — Before vs After

Without Kubernetes vs With Kubernetes
  ❌ WITHOUT KUBERNETES                    ✅ WITH KUBERNETES
  ┌──────────────────────────┐            ┌──────────────────────────┐
  │                          │            │                          │
  │  ┌──────────────┐        │            │  ┌────┐ ┌────┐ ┌────┐   │
  │  │  🖥️ SERVER    │        │            │  │ 📦 │ │ 📦 │ │ 📦 │   │
  │  │              │        │            │  │app │ │app │ │app │   │
  │  │  anihpj      │  ← All │            │  └────┘ └────┘ └────┘   │
  │  │  postgres    │  on    │            │    ↑ auto-heal & scale  │
  │  │  nginx       │  ONE   │            │                          │
  │  │              │  box   │            │  ┌──────┐ ┌──────┐      │
  │  │  💀 crash    │        │            │  │Node 1│ │Node 2│      │
  │  │  = TOTAL     │        │            │  │ ┌──┐ │ │ ┌──┐ │      │
  │  │    DOWNTIME  │        │            │  │ │📦│ │ │ │📦│ │      │
  │  └──────────────┘        │            │  │ └──┘ │ │ └──┘ │      │
  │                          │            │  └──────┘ └──────┘      │
  │  Manual restart at 3AM 😱│            │  Self-healing 24/7 🎉    │
  └──────────────────────────┘            └──────────────────────────┘

Why Containers Alone Aren't Enough

Docker gives you a container. Kubernetes gives you an army of containers that manage themselves. Here's what you'd have to do manually without an orchestrator:

😰 Managing anihpj WITHOUT Kubernetes
  • 🔴 SSH into the server manually at 3 AM when the process crashes
  • 🔴 Restart the crashed Django process by hand — python manage.py runserver
  • 🔴 No load balancing — one server handles ALL traffic to /jobs/
  • 🔴 Deploying = copy files via SCP + pray nothing breaks
  • 🔴 Scaling = buy a new server, install everything from scratch, configure nginx
  • 🔴 Rolling back a bad deploy = restore from backup (if you remembered to make one)
🎉 Managing anihpj WITH Kubernetes
  • ✅ Pod crashes at 3 AM → K8s restarts it in seconds, you sleep through the night
  • ✅ Traffic spikes → K8s spins up more pods automatically via HPA
  • ✅ Deploy new version → K8s does a rolling update with zero downtime
  • ✅ Bad deploy? → kubectl rollout undo — instant rollback
  • ✅ Node fails → K8s migrates all pods to healthy nodes automatically
  • ✅ Config changes? → Update a ConfigMap, pods reload automatically
💡 CKA Admin Perspective

As a CKA administrator, your job isn't just deploying apps — it's building and maintaining the platform that makes all of the above possible. You install the cluster, manage certificates, upgrade versions, backup etcd, and troubleshoot when things go wrong at the infrastructure level.

1.2 What is Kubernetes? (The Simple Answer)

📖 Official Definition

"An open-source system for automating deployment, scaling, and management of containerized applications."

In plain English: Kubernetes is an operating system for your containers. You tell it what you want ("3 copies of anihpj, always running") — and it figures out how to make that happen across your cluster.

The Kubernetes Elevator Pitch

Declarative Management — You Declare, K8s Delivers
  📝 YOU DECLARE (YAML):             ⚙️ KUBERNETES ENSURES:
  ┌───────────────────────┐         ┌────────────────────────────────┐
  │ replicas: 3           │         │ ✅ 3 containers running        │
  │ image: anihpj:v2      │  ────▶  │ ✅ All using v2 image          │
  │ memory: 256Mi         │         │ ✅ Each limited to 256Mi       │
  └───────────────────────┘         └────────────────────────────────┘

  Pod crashes?     K8s restarts it automatically  🔄
  Node dies?       K8s moves pods to other nodes  🔀
  Traffic spikes?  K8s can auto-scale up          📈
  New version?     K8s rolls out with 0 downtime  🚀

  💡 You never say "start a container on node 2"
     You say "I want 3 copies" — K8s figures out the HOW

Quick Facts

☸️
Name Origin

K8s = K + 8 letters + s. Greek κυβερνήτης means "helmsman" — steering the ship.

🏛️
Born at Google

Evolved from Google's internal Borg system. Open-sourced in 2014, donated to CNCF.

🔤
Written in Go

Built with Google's Go language for high concurrency, compiled performance, and clean APIs.

📜
Apache 2.0 License

Fully open-source. Free to use, modify, and distribute commercially.

Kubernetes Lineage — From Borg to K8s
  Google Borg (2003)           Google Omega (2013)          Kubernetes (2014)
 ┌─────────────────────┐     ┌─────────────────────┐     ┌─────────────────────┐
 │ Internal cluster     │────▶│ Next-gen scheduler  │────▶│ Open-source for     │
 │ manager at Google    │     │ research project    │     │ everyone! ☸️         │
 │ Ran Gmail, Search,   │     │ Flexible queuing,   │     │ CNCF graduated 2018 │
 │ YouTube, Maps        │     │ multi-scheduler     │     │ Largest OSS after   │
 └─────────────────────┘     └─────────────────────┘     │ Linux kernel        │
                                                          └─────────────────────┘

Kubernetes by the Numbers

CNCF Survey — K8s Adoption Rate 96%
Fortune 100 Companies Using K8s 77%
GitHub Stars (Top 10 OSS Projects) 112K+
Active GitHub Contributors 3,500+

1.3 The CKA Perspective: Administrator vs Developer

There are two Kubernetes certifications from the Linux Foundation — and they test fundamentally different skills. Understanding the boundary between them is crucial for knowing what to study.

👨‍💻
CKAD — Developer

"I deploy and configure apps on a cluster someone else built."

  • Create Deployments, Services, ConfigMaps
  • Configure health checks and resource limits
  • Build multi-container pods
  • Manage application lifecycle
🔧
CKA — Administrator ← YOU

"I build, manage, and troubleshoot the cluster itself."

  • Install clusters with kubeadm
  • Upgrade cluster versions safely
  • Backup & restore etcd
  • Manage certificates & RBAC
  • Troubleshoot broken nodes
  • Configure networking (CNI, DNS)
CKA Administrator Responsibilities Map
 ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
 │  THE K8s ADMINISTRATOR OWNS EVERYTHING BELOW THE APP       │
 │                                                            │
 │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
 │  │  👨‍💻 Developer's World (CKAD)                         │  │
 │  │  Pods, Deployments, Services, ConfigMaps, Secrets     │  │
 │  ├──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤  │
 │  │  🔧 Administrator's World (CKA) ← YOU                │  │
 │  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │  │
 │  │  │ ⚙️  Cluster Installation (kubeadm)              │  │  │
 │  │  │ 🔐 Certificate Management (PKI/TLS)            │  │  │
 │  │  │ 💾 etcd Backup & Restore                       │  │  │
 │  │  │ ⬆️  Cluster Upgrades (version management)       │  │  │
 │  │  │ 🖥️  Node Management (drain, cordon, uncordon)   │  │  │
 │  │  │ 🛡️  RBAC & Security Policies                   │  │  │
 │  │  │ 🌐 Networking (CNI, CoreDNS, kube-proxy)       │  │  │
 │  │  │ 💿 Storage (PV, PVC, StorageClasses)            │  │  │
 │  │  │ 🔍 Troubleshooting (nodes, pods, cluster)      │  │  │
 │  │  │ 🏗️  High Availability & Disaster Recovery       │  │  │
 │  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │  │
 │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
 └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

CKA Exam Domain Weights

The CKA exam tests five domains. As a CKA candidate, you need to know exactly where each topic fits:

Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration 25%
Workloads & Scheduling 15%
Services & Networking 20%
Storage 10%
Troubleshooting 30%
⚠️ Troubleshooting is 30% of the CKA!

Unlike the CKAD (which focuses on building apps), the CKA dedicates 30% of the exam to troubleshooting broken clusters, failed nodes, crashed control plane components, and networking issues. You must know how to diagnose and fix problems under time pressure.

Aspect CKAD (Developer) CKA (Administrator) ← YOU
Focus Application deployment & configuration Cluster lifecycle & infrastructure
Duration 2 hours, ~15-20 tasks 2 hours, ~15-20 tasks
Passing Score 66% 66%
etcd Not tested Backup, restore, snapshot
kubeadm Not tested Install, upgrade, configure
Certificates Not tested CSR, TLS, certificate rotation
Node Management Not tested Drain, cordon, troubleshoot
Networking Services, Ingress basics CNI, CoreDNS, kube-proxy, NetworkPolicy

CKA Exam Logistics — Quick Reference

📋
Exam Format
  • Duration: 2 hours
  • Questions: 15-20 performance tasks
  • Passing: 66%
  • Environment: Linux terminal (browser-based)
  • K8s version: Latest stable (check CNCF site)
  • Retake: 1 free retake included
📚
Allowed Resources
  • ✅ kubernetes.io/docs — official docs
  • ✅ kubernetes.io/blog — official blog
  • ✅ github.com/kubernetes — source code
  • ❌ No other websites
  • ❌ No notes, bookmarks, or AI tools
  • 💡 Tip: Practice navigating docs fast!

Kubernetes Release Cycle

K8s releases 3 minor versions per year (~every 4 months). As a CKA admin, you manage cluster upgrades and must understand the support window:

🆕
Month 0
New Release

e.g., v1.32 releases. Includes new features, deprecations, and bug fixes.

✅ Latest
🛡️
0-14 months
Supported

Receives patch releases (security/bug fixes). CKA tests within this window.

✅ Patch support
⚠️
14+ months
End of Life

No more patches. Running EOL versions = security risk. Must upgrade.

❌ Unsupported
💡 CKA Upgrade Rule: One Minor Version at a Time

Kubernetes only supports upgrading one minor version at a time (e.g., 1.30 → 1.31 → 1.32). You cannot skip versions. The CKA tests this: you'll be asked to upgrade a cluster from, say, 1.31 to 1.32 using kubeadm upgrade. Attempting to jump from 1.30 to 1.32 will fail.

1.4 Kubernetes Core Concepts — The Big Picture

Before getting hands-on, you need to understand five fundamental concepts that everything else in Kubernetes builds upon. These form the mental model you'll use for the entire CKA journey.

🏗️
Cluster

A set of machines (nodes) managed by Kubernetes as a single unit.

🧠
Control Plane

The "brain" that makes all scheduling & orchestration decisions.

💪
Worker Nodes

The "muscles" that actually run your containerized applications.

📦
Pod

Smallest deployable unit — one or more containers sharing network & storage.

🎯
Desired State

You declare what you want — K8s continuously ensures it stays that way.

The Kubernetes Architecture — Bird's Eye View

Complete Cluster Architecture
 ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
 │                        CLUSTER                                  │
 │                                                                 │
 │  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
 │  │              🧠 CONTROL PLANE ("The Brain")                │  │
 │  │                                                            │  │
 │  │  ┌─────────────┐ ┌────────┐ ┌────────────┐ ┌───────────┐ │  │
 │  │  │ API Server   │ │  etcd  │ │ Scheduler  │ │ Controller│ │  │
 │  │  │ (Front Door) │ │ (Brain │ │ (Matchmaker│ │  Manager  │ │  │
 │  │  │              │ │  Memory│ │  "Pod→Node"│ │ (Watchdog)│ │  │
 │  │  │ kubectl ──▶  │ │ DB)    │ │            │ │           │ │  │
 │  │  └─────────────┘ └────────┘ └────────────┘ └───────────┘ │  │
 │  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
 │                          │                                      │
 │                   kubelet / kube-proxy                           │
 │                          │                                      │
 │  ┌───────────────────────▼───────────────────────────────────┐  │
 │  │              💪 WORKER NODES ("The Muscles")               │  │
 │  │                                                            │  │
 │  │  ┌──────────────────┐         ┌──────────────────┐        │  │
 │  │  │   Worker Node 1  │         │   Worker Node 2  │        │  │
 │  │  │  ┌──────────┐    │         │  ┌──────────┐    │        │  │
 │  │  │  │📦 Pod     │    │         │  │📦 Pod     │    │        │  │
 │  │  │  │ anihpj   │    │         │  │ anihpj   │    │        │  │
 │  │  │  │ :8000    │    │         │  │ :8000    │    │        │  │
 │  │  │  └──────────┘    │         │  └──────────┘    │        │  │
 │  │  │  ┌──────────┐    │         │  ┌──────────┐    │        │  │
 │  │  │  │📦 Pod     │    │         │  │📦 Pod     │    │        │  │
 │  │  │  │ postgres │    │         │  │ redis    │    │        │  │
 │  │  │  │ :5432    │    │         │  │ :6379    │    │        │  │
 │  │  │  └──────────┘    │         │  └──────────┘    │        │  │
 │  │  └──────────────────┘         └──────────────────┘        │  │
 │  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
 │                                                                 │
 │  You say: "I want 3 anihpj pods" (Desired State)               │
 │  K8s ensures: 3 pods are ALWAYS running (Actual State)         │
 └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

Control Plane Components — The Admin Must Know These

🧠 Control Plane Components (CKA Exam: 25%)

👨‍✈️ kube-apiserver
The front door — ALL communication goes through the API server. kubectl commands, node heartbeats, controller actions. If this goes down, the cluster is blind.
📋 etcd
The cluster's brain memory — a distributed key-value store holding ALL cluster state. CKA tests etcd backup & restore. Lose etcd without a backup = lose your entire cluster.
🎯 kube-scheduler
The matchmaker — watches for new Pods with no node assigned, then picks the best node based on resources, affinity, taints/tolerations.
🔄 kube-controller-manager
The watchdog — runs controller loops that continuously compare desired state vs actual state and take corrective action (e.g., "only 2 of 3 replicas running → create 1 more").
☁️ cloud-controller-manager
Cloud glue (optional) — integrates with AWS/Azure/GCP APIs for load balancers, persistent disks, and node lifecycle. Not present in bare-metal clusters.

Worker Node Components

💪 Worker Node Components

🤖 kubelet
The node agent — receives pod specs from the API server and ensures the containers are running and healthy on that node. Reports node status back.
🔀 kube-proxy
The network plumber — maintains network rules on each node so pods can communicate with each other and with external traffic via Services.
🐋 Container Runtime
The engine — actually runs containers. Kubernetes supports containerd (default), CRI-O, or any CRI-compatible runtime. Docker was removed in v1.24.

Desired State vs Actual State — The Declarative Magic

The Reconciliation Loop
  📝 YOU DECLARE (YAML):           ⚙️ KUBERNETES ENSURES:
  ┌──────────────────────┐        ┌──────────────────────────────┐
  │ replicas: 3          │        │ 📦 📦 📦  running             │
  │ image: anihpj:v2     │ ─────▶ │ all v2 images                │
  │ memory: 256Mi        │        │ each ≤ 256Mi                  │
  └──────────────────────┘        └──────────────────────────────┘
                                            │
                                   Controller Loop 🔄
                                            │
                              ┌─────────────▼──────────────┐
                              │ Pod crashes?  → Restart it  │
                              │ Node dies?    → Reschedule  │
                              │ Too few pods? → Create more │
                              │ Too many?     → Terminate   │
                              └────────────────────────────┘
💡 Why Declarative Matters for CKA

The CKA exam expects you to work declaratively (YAML manifests) rather than imperatively. You declare the desired state, apply it with kubectl apply -f, and trust Kubernetes controllers to make it happen. Understanding this model is essential for troubleshooting — when something breaks, you compare desired state (in etcd) against actual state (on nodes).

1.5 The Kubernetes Ecosystem

Kubernetes doesn't work alone. It sits at the center of a massive ecosystem of tools, plugins, and platforms — all governed by the Cloud Native Computing Foundation (CNCF). As a CKA administrator, you'll work with many of these tools daily.

Kubernetes Ecosystem Orbit
                         Helm 📦 (Package Manager)
                    ╱                               ╲
              ArgoCD 🔄                         Calico 🌐
            (GitOps)   ╲                      ╱  (CNI)
                        ┌─────────────────┐
               ─────────│   KUBERNETES    │─────── Prometheus 📊
                        │     ☸️ Core      │        (Monitoring)
                        └─────────────────┘
            containerd 🐋╱                     ╲  Istio 🕸️
            (Runtime)                           (Service Mesh)
                    ╲                               ╱
                        Grafana 📈            Cilium 🔒
                      (Dashboards)          (eBPF Networking)

              All governed by CNCF (Cloud Native Computing Foundation)

Key Ecosystem Categories for CKA

🐋
Container Runtimes

containerd (default since K8s 1.24), CRI-O (Red Hat). Docker engine was deprecated — Kubernetes uses the Container Runtime Interface (CRI) directly.

🌐
CNI Plugins (Networking)

Calico (network policies), Cilium (eBPF-based), Flannel (simple overlay), Weave. CKA requires you to install a CNI plugin during cluster setup.

📦
Package Management

Helm — the "apt/yum for Kubernetes." Packages K8s manifests into reusable charts. Essential for installing complex applications like Prometheus, cert-manager, ingress controllers.

🔄
GitOps

ArgoCD, FluxCD — automatically sync cluster state from a Git repository. Changes in Git trigger deployments. The source of truth lives in version control.

🕸️
Service Mesh

Istio, Linkerd — adds observability, traffic management, and mTLS encryption between services without changing application code.

📊
Monitoring & Observability

Prometheus (metrics), Grafana (dashboards), Loki (logs), Jaeger (tracing). The CKA expects you to know kubectl top and metrics-server.

📖 CNCF — Who Governs Kubernetes?

The Cloud Native Computing Foundation (part of the Linux Foundation) governs Kubernetes and 170+ related projects. Projects go through three maturity levels: Sandbox → Incubating → Graduated. Kubernetes was the first project to graduate (2018). CNCF also manages the CKA and CKAD certifications.

The CNCF Landscape — Maturity Levels

🎓 Graduated Production-proven, widely adopted
Kubernetes Prometheus containerd CoreDNS etcd Helm
🧪 Incubating Growing adoption, maturing features
ArgoCD Cilium Knative Kyverno Backstage
🌱 Sandbox Early-stage, experimental projects
Headlamp OpenCost Inspektor Gadget

1.6 How Companies Use Kubernetes in Real Life

Kubernetes isn't just for tech giants. Companies of every size use it — the scale of their setup and admin tasks varies dramatically.

Company Size K8s Setup Admin Tasks Team
🏠 Startup (5-20 people)
e.g., anihpj		 1 cluster, 3 nodes. Start with Minikube, graduate to managed cloud (EKS/AKS/GKE). Deploy apps, monitor health, basic troubleshooting, set up CI/CD. 1 admin does it all
🏢 Mid-size (50-200 people) 2-5 clusters. Separate dev/staging/prod. Multi-team RBAC. CI/CD pipelines, RBAC, cluster upgrades, monitoring, cost optimization, security policies. 2-4 admins
🏙️ Enterprise (500+ people) 10-100+ clusters. Multi-region, HA, service mesh, custom networking. HA & disaster recovery, security compliance, GitOps, multi-cluster management, platform engineering. Dedicated platform team (5-20+)

A Day in the Life of a Kubernetes Administrator

1
Morning Check

Review monitoring dashboards (Grafana/Prometheus). Check node health, pod restarts, resource usage. Look for alerts triggered overnight.

2
Troubleshooting

Investigate any failed pods or node issues. kubectl describe node, kubectl logs, check system journals with journalctl -u kubelet.

3
Planned Changes

Apply planned upgrades, RBAC changes, or new namespace configurations. Always test in staging first, then promote to production.

4
Security & Compliance

Review recent audit logs, rotate certificates if due, verify NetworkPolicies, check RBAC for over-permissioned accounts.

5
Capacity Planning

Monitor resource trends. Are nodes nearing capacity? Do we need to add nodes or right-size pod requests/limits?

Real Companies Running Kubernetes at Scale

🎵
Spotify

Migrated 150+ services to K8s. Reduced deployment time from hours to minutes. Custom platform built on top of K8s.

🛒
Shopify

Handles Black Friday traffic (millions of requests/sec) on K8s clusters that auto-scale to handle 10x normal load.

🎮
Pokemon GO

Launched on GKE. Scaled from expected 1x traffic to 50x in days. K8s handled the scaling that would have crashed traditional infrastructure.

🏦
Capital One

Runs critical banking services on K8s with strict compliance requirements. Proves K8s works in highly regulated industries.

💡 Where You Fit In — The anihpj Journey

Throughout this guide, we'll follow the journey of anihpj — our Django job board application — from a single-server deployment to a production-grade Kubernetes cluster. You'll learn to build the infrastructure that makes reliable, scalable deployments possible. By the end, you'll have the skills to pass the CKA exam and administer real-world clusters.

Chapter 2: Docker and Containers — The Building Blocks

Learn containers, Docker basics, and container runtimes — the foundation Kubernetes builds on. As a CKA administrator, you must understand how containers work because your cluster runs them.

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~2 hours 📖 Hands-On 🐳 Docker

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Why containers solve the "works on my machine" problem
  • ✅ Containers vs Virtual Machines — when to use each
  • ✅ Docker basics: Dockerfile, images, containers, registries
  • ✅ Building a Docker image for the anihpj Django app
  • ✅ Essential Docker commands every admin needs
  • ✅ Container runtimes: containerd, CRI-O, and why Docker was removed from K8s
🎓 CKA Relevance
Cluster Architecture 25% Troubleshooting 30%
Container Runtime Knowledge on the CKA

The CKA doesn't test Docker commands directly, but you must understand container runtimes because:

  • Cluster installation — you configure containerd/CRI-O as part of kubeadm setup
  • Troubleshooting — when pods fail, you may need to check runtime logs with crictl
  • Node issues — container runtime failures cause node NotReady status
  • Image management — understanding image layers helps debug ImagePullBackOff errors

2.1 Why Containers? The "Works on My Machine" Problem

Every developer has lived this nightmare: the app works perfectly on your laptop, but crashes in production. Containers solve this by packaging everything the app needs into a single portable unit.

The "Works on My Machine" Problem
  👩‍💻 Developer laptop:  "anihpj works perfectly!"
  🖥️ Production server:  "ModuleNotFoundError: No module named 'django'" 💀

  WHY? Different Python version, missing packages,
  different OS, missing environment variables...

  SOLUTION: Pack EVERYTHING into a container 📦
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────┐
  │ 📦 Docker Container                  │
  │ ┌─────────────────────────────────┐ │
  │ │ Python 3.12                     │ │
  │ │ Django 5.0                      │ │
  │ │ gunicorn, psycopg2              │ │
  │ │ anihpj source code              │ │
  │ │ settings & config files         │ │
  │ └─────────────────────────────────┘ │
  │ Runs IDENTICALLY everywhere         │
  │ Laptop ✅  Staging ✅  Production ✅ │
  └─────────────────────────────────────┘
📖 Key Term: Container

A container is a lightweight, standalone, executable package that includes everything needed to run a piece of software: code, runtime, system tools, libraries, and settings. Containers share the host's OS kernel, making them much lighter than virtual machines.

📦
Portable

Same container runs on Linux, Mac, Windows, cloud, bare metal — identical behavior everywhere.

🔒
Isolated

Each container has its own filesystem, network, and process space. No conflicts between apps.

⚡
Fast

Start in seconds (not minutes like VMs). Share host kernel — no full OS boot needed.

📐
Lightweight

Megabytes (not gigabytes). Run dozens of containers on a single machine.

2.2 Containers vs Virtual Machines

Both containers and VMs provide isolation, but they work at fundamentally different levels. Understanding this helps you troubleshoot as a CKA admin.

VM Architecture vs Container Architecture
  VIRTUAL MACHINES                    CONTAINERS
  ┌─────────────────────┐            ┌─────────────────────┐
  │ ┌─────┐  ┌─────┐    │            │ ┌─────┐  ┌─────┐    │
  │ │App A│  │App B│    │            │ │App A│  │App B│    │
  │ ├─────┤  ├─────┤    │            │ ├─────┤  ├─────┤    │
  │ │Libs │  │Libs │    │            │ │Libs │  │Libs │    │
  │ ├─────┤  ├─────┤    │            │ └──┬──┘  └──┬──┘    │
  │ │Guest│  │Guest│    │            │    │ Shared  │       │
  │ │ OS  │  │ OS  │    │            │ ┌──▼────────▼──┐    │
  │ └─────┘  └─────┘    │            │ │Container Eng.│    │
  │ ┌─────────────────┐  │            │ │ (containerd) │    │
  │ │   Hypervisor    │  │            │ └──────────────┘    │
  │ └─────────────────┘  │            │ ┌──────────────────┐│
  │ ┌─────────────────┐  │            │ │    Host OS       ││
  │ │    Host OS      │  │            │ └──────────────────┘│
  │ └─────────────────┘  │            │ ┌──────────────────┐│
  │ ┌─────────────────┐  │            │ │    Hardware      ││
  │ │    Hardware     │  │            │ └──────────────────┘│
  │ └─────────────────┘  │            │                     │
  │ Heavy: GB, minutes   │            │ Light: MB, seconds  │
  └─────────────────────┘            └─────────────────────┘
Feature Virtual Machine Container
Size Gigabytes (full OS per VM) Megabytes (shared kernel)
Startup Time Minutes (OS boot required) Seconds (process start)
OS Full guest OS per VM Shares host kernel
Isolation Strong (hardware-level) Process-level (namespaces/cgroups)
Performance Near-native (hypervisor overhead) Native (direct kernel access)
Density ~10-20 VMs per server ~100-1000 containers per server
Best For Different OS needed, strong isolation Same OS, many microservices
💡 CKA Admin Note: Kubernetes Nodes ARE VMs (Usually)

In most real-world clusters, Kubernetes worker nodes are virtual machines running in a cloud provider (EC2, Azure VM, GCE). So you actually use both: VMs as the host infrastructure, and containers for the workloads. The Kubernetes admin manages both layers.

2.3 Docker Basics for Kubernetes Admins

Docker established the container ecosystem. Even though Kubernetes no longer uses Docker as a runtime, Docker's concepts and tooling remain fundamental to the workflow.

📝Dockerfile
→
🔨docker build
→
📀Image
→
🚀docker run
→
📦Container
📝
Dockerfile

A text recipe defining how to build an image. Lists the base OS, dependencies, code, and startup command.

📀
Image

The built, read-only template. Like a class in OOP — it defines the container but isn't running yet.

📦
Container

A running instance of an image. Like an object created from a class — it's live and executing.

🏪
Registry

Image storage & distribution. Docker Hub (public), ECR (AWS), GCR (GCP), ACR (Azure).

Image Layers — How Docker Images Work

Docker Image Layer Architecture
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  Container Layer (read-write)         │ ← Runtime changes
  ├──────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  Layer 5: CMD ["gunicorn", "..."]    │ ← Startup command
  ├──────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  Layer 4: COPY . /app               │ ← anihpj source code
  ├──────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  Layer 3: RUN pip install -r req.txt │ ← Python packages
  ├──────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  Layer 2: COPY requirements.txt .    │ ← Dependencies file
  ├──────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  Layer 1: FROM python:3.12-slim      │ ← Base OS + Python
  └──────────────────────────────────────┘
  
  💡 Each layer is cached. Change Layer 4 → only rebuild 4 & 5.
     This is why you copy requirements.txt BEFORE source code!
📖 Why Layer Order Matters for CKA

When troubleshooting ImagePullBackOff errors, understanding image layers helps you diagnose whether the issue is a missing base image, a build failure, or a registry authentication problem. As an admin, you configure imagePullSecrets for private registries.

2.4 Building an anihpj Docker Image

Let's build a production-ready Docker image for our anihpj Django application. This is the image Kubernetes will pull and run as pods.

Dockerfile 
# Stage 1: Base image with Python
FROM python:3.12-slim

# Set environment variables
ENV PYTHONDONTWRITEBYTECODE=1 \
    PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1

# Set working directory
WORKDIR /app

# Install system dependencies
RUN apt-get update && \
    apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends gcc libpq-dev && \
    rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

# Copy requirements first (for layer caching)
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install --no-cache-dir -r requirements.txt

# Copy project source code
COPY . .

# Collect static files
RUN python manage.py collectstatic --noinput

# Create non-root user (security best practice)
RUN addgroup --system appgroup && \
    adduser --system --ingroup appgroup appuser
USER appuser

# Expose port
EXPOSE 8000

# Run with gunicorn (production WSGI server)
CMD ["gunicorn", "anihpj.wsgi:application", \
     "--bind", "0.0.0.0:8000", \
     "--workers", "3"]
.dockerignore 
# Don't copy these into the image
__pycache__/
*.pyc
*.pyo
.git/
.gitignore
.env
db.sqlite3
venv/
env/
node_modules/
*.md
.dockerignore
Dockerfile
⚠️ Security: Always Run as Non-Root

Notice the USER appuser directive. Running containers as root is a security risk — if an attacker breaks out of the container, they'd have root access to the host. CKA-level admins enforce this via PodSecurityStandards and security contexts.

Build & Test Workflow

terminal — build & run 
# Build the image
docker build -t anihpj:v1 .

# Run the container
docker run -d -p 8000:8000 --name anihpj-app anihpj:v1

# Test it
curl http://localhost:8000/jobs/

# View logs
docker logs anihpj-app

# Push to registry (for Kubernetes to pull later)
docker tag anihpj:v1 myregistry/anihpj:v1
docker push myregistry/anihpj:v1

2.5 Essential Docker Commands for Admins

While the CKA doesn't test Docker directly, these commands help you build images and troubleshoot container issues during daily admin work.

Command What It Does CKA Relevance
docker build -t anihpj:v1 . Build image from Dockerfile Prep images for K8s
docker run -p 8000:8000 anihpj Run container, expose port Test before deploying
docker ps List running containers Debug outside K8s
docker logs <id> View container logs Similar to kubectl logs
docker exec -it <id> bash Shell into container Similar to kubectl exec
docker images List local images Check cached images
docker push <registry>/anihpj Push image to registry Required for K8s to pull
docker pull <image> Pull image from registry Pre-cache on nodes
docker stop <id> Stop a running container Graceful shutdown
docker system prune Clean up unused resources Disk space on nodes
💡 On the CKA Exam: Use crictl, Not Docker

On CKA exam nodes, Docker may not be installed. Use crictl (CRI CLI) instead:

  • crictl ps — list containers (like docker ps)
  • crictl logs <id> — view logs (like docker logs)
  • crictl images — list images (like docker images)
  • crictl inspect <id> — container details (like docker inspect)

2.6 Container Runtimes: Beyond Docker

Since Kubernetes v1.24, Docker is no longer a supported container runtime. Kubernetes uses the Container Runtime Interface (CRI) to communicate with runtimes directly. As a CKA admin, you install and configure the runtime on every node.

Container Runtime Evolution in Kubernetes
  Before v1.24:                   After v1.24:
  
  kubelet                         kubelet
     │                               │
     ▼                               ▼
  dockershim (REMOVED ❌)         CRI interface
     │                            ┌──┴────┐
     ▼                            ▼       ▼
  Docker daemon               containerd   CRI-O
     │                            │         │
     ▼                            ▼         ▼
  containerd                  containers  containers
     │
     ▼                        (direct — no Docker overhead!)
  containers
                              
  💡 Docker images still work! Only the runtime changed.
     You still USE Docker to BUILD images on your laptop.
🐋
containerd

The industry standard. Extracted from Docker itself. Used by GKE, EKS, AKS, and most managed K8s providers. Lightweight, stable, CNCF graduated project.

  • Default runtime for kubeadm clusters
  • CLI tool: ctr (low-level), crictl (K8s-native)
  • Config: /etc/containerd/config.toml
🔴
CRI-O

Purpose-built for Kubernetes — nothing more, nothing less. Preferred by Red Hat/OpenShift. Minimal footprint, K8s version-aligned releases.

  • Used by OpenShift and Fedora CoreOS
  • Version-locked to Kubernetes versions
  • Config: /etc/crio/crio.conf
⚠️ CKA Exam: You May Need to Configure the Container Runtime

If a CKA task involves setting up a new node or troubleshooting a node that's NotReady, check the container runtime first:

terminal — runtime troubleshooting 
# Check if containerd is running
systemctl status containerd

# Check kubelet logs for CRI errors
journalctl -u kubelet | grep -i "container runtime"

# List containers via CRI
crictl ps

# Check runtime endpoint
crictl info

# Restart containerd if stuck
sudo systemctl restart containerd

🔑 Chapter 2 Key Takeaways for CKA

Containers are the workloads your cluster runs. As a CKA admin, you don't write Dockerfiles daily — but you must understand container images, runtimes, and how Kubernetes interacts with them via CRI. When a pod fails to start, your troubleshooting starts at the runtime level.

Multi-Stage Build — Production Pattern

Multi-stage builds produce smaller, more secure images by separating the build environment from the final runtime. Here's how to apply this to anihpj:

Dockerfile.production — multi-stage build 
# Stage 1: Builder — install dependencies
FROM python:3.12-slim AS builder
WORKDIR /app
COPY requirements.txt .
RUN pip install --no-cache-dir --prefix=/install -r requirements.txt

# Stage 2: Runtime — final slim image
FROM python:3.12-slim
WORKDIR /app
COPY --from=builder /install /usr/local
COPY . .
RUN addgroup --system app && adduser --system --ingroup app app
USER app
EXPOSE 8000
CMD ["gunicorn", "anihpj.wsgi:application", "--bind", "0.0.0.0:8000"]
Multi-Stage Build: Size Reduction
  Single-stage:    python:3.12   + gcc + build tools + app
                   ~900 MB 😬

  Multi-stage:     python:3.12-slim + app (no build tools!)
                   ~150 MB ✅

  ┌────────────────────┐     ┌────────────────────┐
  │ Stage 1: Builder   │     │ Stage 2: Runtime   │
  │ gcc, build-deps    │────▶│ ONLY runtime deps  │
  │ pip install        │copy │ + app code         │
  │ (discarded after!) │     │ FINAL IMAGE        │
  └────────────────────┘     └────────────────────┘

Container Image Security Checklist

🛡️
Do These ✅
  • Use slim/distroless base images
  • Run as non-root (USER appuser)
  • Pin image versions (python:3.12.3 not :latest)
  • Scan images with Trivy or Snyk
  • Use .dockerignore to exclude secrets
  • Set readOnlyRootFilesystem: true in K8s
🚫
Avoid These ❌
  • Running as root in containers
  • Using :latest tag (unpredictable)
  • Embedding secrets in images
  • Using full OS base images (Ubuntu 1GB+)
  • Leaving build tools in production images
  • Skipping image vulnerability scans

Chapter 3: Kubernetes Architecture — The Admin's Blueprint

Deep dive into control plane, worker nodes, and how pods get created — the administrator's perspective. This chapter maps directly to the CKA exam's "Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration" domain (25%).

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~2 hours 📖 Architecture 🎯 CKA Core

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Complete cluster architecture — control plane + worker nodes
  • ✅ Every control plane component: API server, etcd, scheduler, controller-manager
  • ✅ Every worker node component: kubelet, kube-proxy, container runtime
  • ✅ How a Pod gets created — the full 8-step journey
  • ✅ Cluster networking overview — CNI, pod network, service network
  • ✅ Static Pods — admin-only concept used by kubeadm
🎓 CKA Relevance
Cluster Architecture 25% Troubleshooting 30%
Architecture Knowledge is CKA Gold

Every CKA troubleshooting question requires you to understand how components interact. When a node is NotReady, you check kubelet. When pods aren't scheduling, you check the scheduler. When state is lost, you restore etcd. This chapter is the foundation for 55% of the exam.

  • API Server — diagnosing connectivity and authentication issues
  • etcd — backup, restore, and snapshot operations (tested directly)
  • Scheduler — understanding why pods are Pending
  • Static Pods — managing control plane components via kubelet

3.1 Cluster Architecture Overview for Admins

A Kubernetes cluster consists of two layers: the Control Plane (brain) that makes decisions, and Worker Nodes (muscles) that run workloads. As a CKA administrator, you install, configure, and maintain both layers.

Complete Kubernetes Cluster Architecture
 ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
 │  KUBERNETES CLUSTER                                              │
 │                                                                  │
 │  ┌──────────────── CONTROL PLANE ────────────────────────┐      │
 │  │                                                        │      │
 │  │  ┌──────────┐  ┌───────────┐  ┌──────────────────┐   │      │
 │  │  │ API      │  │ etcd      │  │ kube-scheduler   │   │      │
 │  │  │ Server   │  │ (database)│  │ (pod placement)  │   │      │
 │  │  │ 🚪 Front │  │ 🧠 Brain  │  │ 📋 "Pod → Node"  │   │      │
 │  │  │ door for │  │ stores    │  │ picks best node  │   │      │
 │  │  │ ALL cmds │  │ all state │  │ for each pod     │   │      │
 │  │  └──────────┘  └───────────┘  └──────────────────┘   │      │
 │  │                                                        │      │
 │  │  ┌────────────────────┐  ┌───────────────────────┐   │      │
 │  │  │ controller-manager │  │ cloud-controller-mgr  │   │      │
 │  │  │ 🔄 Watches state   │  │ ☁️ Cloud integration   │   │      │
 │  │  │ & corrects drift   │  │ LB, routes, disks     │   │      │
 │  │  └────────────────────┘  └───────────────────────┘   │      │
 │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘      │
 │                          │ API                                    │
 │  ┌───────────────────────▼───────────────────────────────┐      │
 │  │                   WORKER NODES                         │      │
 │  │                                                        │      │
 │  │  ┌───────────────────┐    ┌───────────────────┐       │      │
 │  │  │ Worker Node 1     │    │ Worker Node 2     │       │      │
 │  │  │                   │    │                   │       │      │
 │  │  │ 🤖 kubelet        │    │ 🤖 kubelet        │       │      │
 │  │  │ 🌐 kube-proxy     │    │ 🌐 kube-proxy     │       │      │
 │  │  │ 🐋 containerd     │    │ 🐋 containerd     │       │      │
 │  │  │                   │    │                   │       │      │
 │  │  │ ┌────┐ ┌────┐    │    │ ┌────┐ ┌────┐    │       │      │
 │  │  │ │ 📦 │ │ 📦 │    │    │ │ 📦 │ │ 📦 │    │       │      │
 │  │  │ │pod │ │pod │    │    │ │pod │ │pod │    │       │      │
 │  │  │ └────┘ └────┘    │    │ └────┘ └────┘    │       │      │
 │  │  └───────────────────┘    └───────────────────┘       │      │
 │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘      │
 └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
🧠 Control Plane Makes all orchestration decisions
kube-apiserver etcd kube-scheduler controller-manager
💪 Worker Nodes Run the actual workloads
kubelet kube-proxy containerd/CRI-O Pods
🌐 Networking Layer Connects everything together
CNI Plugin CoreDNS Service Network Pod Network

3.2 Control Plane Components — Deep Dive for Admins

These are the components you maintain, troubleshoot, and restore as a CKA administrator. Each one is critical — if any fails, the cluster is impacted.

a) kube-apiserver — The Front Door

👨‍✈️ kube-apiserver

🎯 Role
The ONLY entry point to the cluster. ALL communication (kubectl, kubelet, controllers) goes through the API server.
🔐 Security
Handles authentication (certificates, tokens), authorization (RBAC), and admission control (validation, mutation).
📂 Config
Static pod manifest: /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml
🔌 Default Port
6443 (secure HTTPS)
💀 If It Fails
No kubectl commands work, no new pods are created, nodes can't report status. Existing pods keep running (kubelet is independent).

b) etcd — The Cluster Database

📋 etcd — The Brain's Memory

🎯 Role
Distributed key-value store holding ALL cluster state: pods, services, secrets, config, RBAC rules — everything.
🔄 Consensus
Uses Raft protocol. Requires a quorum (majority) of nodes to agree on writes. That's why HA clusters use 3 or 5 etcd instances.
🔌 Ports
2379 (client), 2380 (peer communication)
📝 CKA Exam
Backup & restore is directly tested. You must know etcdctl snapshot save and etcdctl snapshot restore.
🚨 WARNING: etcd Lost = Cluster Lost

If etcd data is lost without a backup, your entire cluster configuration is gone. No pods, no services, no secrets — nothing. Always maintain regular etcd backups. This is why the CKA tests this skill.

terminal — etcd backup (CKA exam skill) 
# Backup etcd
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot save /opt/etcd-backup.db \
  --endpoints=https://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --cacert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt \
  --cert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt \
  --key=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key

# Verify backup
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot status /opt/etcd-backup.db --write-table

c) kube-scheduler — The Matchmaker

🎯 kube-scheduler

🎯 Role
Watches for new Pods with no node assigned, evaluates all available nodes, and assigns the Pod to the best fit.
🔍 Process
Filter (eliminate unfit nodes) → Score (rank remaining) → Bind (assign to top-scored node).
📊 Considers
CPU/memory availability, nodeSelector, affinity/anti-affinity, taints/tolerations, PV requirements, topology spread.
💀 If It Fails
New pods stay in Pending state. Existing pods keep running. Check with: kubectl get pods -A | grep Pending
1
Watch

Scheduler watches API server for new Pods with nodeName: ""

2
Filter

Eliminate nodes that can't run the Pod (insufficient resources, taints, affinity rules)

3
Score

Rank remaining nodes by fit (spread, resource balance, affinity preferences)

4
Bind

Assign Pod to top-scored node → update etcd → kubelet takes over

d) kube-controller-manager — The Watchdog

🔄 kube-controller-manager

🎯 Role
Runs multiple controller loops in a single process. Each controller watches one resource type and reconciles desired state vs actual state.
🔍 Node Controller
Monitors node heartbeats. Marks nodes as NotReady after 40s of no heartbeat. Evicts pods after 5 minutes.
📦 ReplicaSet Ctrl
Maintains correct pod count. Spec says 3 replicas but only 2 running? Creates 1 more.
🔗 Endpoint Ctrl
Connects Services to Pods by maintaining the Endpoints object (lists of pod IPs matching the service selector).
👤 SA Controller
Creates default ServiceAccount for each new namespace.
The Controller Loop — Heart of Kubernetes
  CONTROLLER LOOP (runs continuously)
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                          │
  │  ┌──────────┐                            │
  │  │ OBSERVE  │ ← Watch actual state       │
  │  │          │   "3 pods running"          │
  │  └────┬─────┘                            │
  │       │                                  │
  │       ▼                                  │
  │  ┌──────────┐                            │
  │  │ COMPARE  │ ← Compare to desired state │
  │  │ (diff)   │   "spec says 5 replicas"   │
  │  └────┬─────┘                            │
  │       │                                  │
  │       ▼                                  │
  │  ┌──────────┐                            │
  │  │   ACT    │ ← Corrective action        │
  │  │(reconcile)│  "create 2 more pods"     │
  │  └────┬─────┘                            │
  │       │                                  │
  │       └──────────── loop forever ────────┘
  └──────────────────────────────────────────┘

3.3 Worker Node Components — Deep Dive for Admins

Worker nodes run the actual application containers. Each node has three critical components you must maintain and troubleshoot.

a) kubelet — The Node Agent

🤖 kubelet

🎯 Role
Agent on every node. Receives PodSpecs from API server, ensures containers are running and healthy, reports status back.
📂 Config
/var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml
🔌 Port
10250 (HTTPS API)
⚡ Key Fact
kubelet is NOT a pod — it runs as a systemd service on the node. If it crashes, check with systemctl status kubelet.
🔍 Troubleshoot
journalctl -u kubelet -f — watch kubelet logs in real-time. Most common issue: certificate expired or runtime not responding.

b) kube-proxy — The Network Plumber

🌐 kube-proxy

🎯 Role
Maintains network rules on each node. Enables Service-based access to Pods — routes traffic to the right Pod endpoint.
⚙️ Modes
iptables (default), IPVS (high performance, 1000+ services), nftables (newer kernel-based).
📦 Runs As
DaemonSet pod (one per node) in kube-system namespace. Check with kubectl get ds -n kube-system

c) Container Runtime

🐋
containerd

Default for most K8s installations. CNCF graduated. Used by EKS, AKS, GKE, and kubeadm clusters.

🔴
CRI-O

Kubernetes-native, minimal runtime. Used by OpenShift. Versioned in lockstep with K8s releases.

⚠️ CKA Troubleshooting: Node NotReady?

When a node shows NotReady, check these in order:

  • systemctl status kubelet — is the node agent running?
  • journalctl -u kubelet — any error messages?
  • systemctl status containerd — is the container runtime running?
  • crictl ps — can the runtime list containers?

3.4 How a Pod Gets Created — The Full Journey

Understanding every step of pod creation is essential for CKA troubleshooting. When a pod fails, you need to know which step broke to fix it.

1
kubectl → API Server

kubectl apply -f anihpj-deployment.yaml sends HTTPS request to API server (port 6443) with TLS certificate authentication.

2
Authentication → Authorization → Admission

API server validates: Who are you? (certs/tokens) → Can you do this? (RBAC) → Is the YAML valid? (admission controllers)

3
API Server → etcd

Validated Deployment object is persisted to etcd. The desired state is now recorded.

4
Controller Manager Creates Pods

DeploymentController sees new Deployment → creates ReplicaSet → ReplicaSetController creates Pod objects (still without a node).

5
Scheduler Assigns Nodes

Scheduler watches for Pods with nodeName: "" → filters → scores → binds Pod to best node.

6
kubelet Starts Container

kubelet on the assigned node pulls the container image → starts container via containerd → sets up networking.

7
kube-proxy Updates Network

Network rules are updated so Services can route traffic to the new Pod's IP address.

8
Pod is Running ✅

kubelet reports Pod status → API server → etcd. The reconciliation loop continues monitoring.

Pod Creation Flow — Visual Summary
  kubectl apply ──▶ API Server ──▶ etcd (store)
                        │
                        ▼
                 Controller Manager
                   (create Pods)
                        │
                        ▼
                    Scheduler
                  (assign nodes)
                        │
                        ▼
                 kubelet (on node)
                   (pull image)
                   (start container)
                        │
                        ▼
                   kube-proxy
                (update network rules)
                        │
                        ▼
                   Pod Running ✅
💡 CKA Troubleshooting Map

Match the symptom to the failed step:

  • Connection refused → Step 1 (API server down)
  • Forbidden/Unauthorized → Step 2 (RBAC/certs)
  • Pod Pending → Step 5 (scheduler can't find a node)
  • ImagePullBackOff → Step 6 (kubelet can't pull image)
  • CrashLoopBackOff → Step 6 (container starts but crashes)
  • Service unreachable → Step 7 (kube-proxy/network issue)

3.5 Cluster Networking Overview (Admin Perspective)

Kubernetes networking has one fundamental rule: every Pod gets its own unique IP address, and all Pods can communicate with all other Pods without NAT. CNI plugins make this happen.

Kubernetes Networking — Three Layers
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  EXTERNAL TRAFFIC (Users, Internet)                  │
  │  Access via: NodePort / LoadBalancer / Ingress       │
  ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  SERVICE NETWORK (ClusterIP range)                   │
  │  Virtual IPs managed by kube-proxy                   │
  │  e.g., 10.96.0.0/12                                 │
  │  Services → route traffic to Pod endpoints           │
  ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  POD NETWORK (Pod CIDR)                              │
  │  Real IPs assigned by CNI plugin                     │
  │  e.g., 10.244.0.0/16                                │
  │  Every Pod gets a unique IP — flat network           │
  ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │  NODE NETWORK (Physical/VM IPs)                      │
  │  Infrastructure network                              │
  │  e.g., 192.168.1.0/24                               │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

CNI Plugins — Your Choice as Admin

🐯
Calico

Most popular. Full NetworkPolicy support. BGP routing. Used in the CKA exam environment.

🐝
Cilium

eBPF-based. Advanced observability. High performance. Growing rapidly in adoption.

🟦
Flannel

Simple VXLAN overlay. Easy setup. No NetworkPolicy support. Good for learning.

🕸️
Weave

Encrypted mesh. Easy install. Auto-discovers. NetworkPolicy support.

📖 CKA Exam: CNI Installation

After running kubeadm init, your cluster's Pods won't start until you install a CNI plugin. This is a common CKA task: kubectl apply -f https://docs.projectcalico.org/manifests/calico.yaml. Without a CNI, all pods (including CoreDNS) will be in Pending state.

3.6 Static Pods — Admin-Only Concept

Static Pods are managed directly by the kubelet on a specific node — not by the API server. They're how kubeadm deploys control plane components, and they're directly tested on the CKA exam.

📦
Regular Pods

Created via API server (kubectl). Stored in etcd. Scheduled by the scheduler. Managed by controllers.

📌
Static Pods

Created by kubelet directly. YAML files in /etc/kubernetes/manifests/. kubelet watches this directory and auto-manages pod lifecycle.

💡 Control Plane = Static Pods!

On kubeadm clusters, the control plane components (API server, scheduler, controller-manager, etcd) are all static pods. This means you can edit their configuration by modifying the YAML files in /etc/kubernetes/manifests/:

terminal — control plane static pods 
# List control plane static pod manifests
ls /etc/kubernetes/manifests/
# Output:
#   etcd.yaml
#   kube-apiserver.yaml
#   kube-controller-manager.yaml
#   kube-scheduler.yaml

# Static pods have nodename suffix
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep controlplane
# Output:
#   etcd-controlplane                  1/1   Running
#   kube-apiserver-controlplane        1/1   Running
#   kube-controller-manager-controlplane  1/1   Running
#   kube-scheduler-controlplane        1/1   Running

Creating a Custom Static Pod

static-nginx.yaml — /etc/kubernetes/manifests/ 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: static-nginx
  namespace: kube-system
spec:
  containers:
  - name: nginx
    image: nginx:1.25
    ports:
    - containerPort: 80
⚠️ CKA Static Pod Tips

Key facts for the exam:

  • Static pods can ONLY be created/deleted by adding/removing YAML from the manifest directory
  • You cannot delete a static pod with kubectl delete — kubelet recreates it immediately
  • Check the staticPodPath in /var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml to find the manifest directory
  • Static pod names always end with -<nodename> (e.g., static-nginx-worker01)

🔑 Chapter 3 Key Takeaways for CKA

This is the most architecturally important chapter. Every troubleshooting scenario on the CKA requires you to know which component does what, where its logs are, and how to restart it. Memorize: API server (port 6443), etcd (2379/2380), kubelet (10250), kube-proxy (DaemonSet), static pods (/etc/kubernetes/manifests/).

etcd Restore — Complete CKA Procedure

Restoring etcd is one of the most commonly tested CKA tasks. Here's the full step-by-step procedure:

terminal — etcd restore (CKA exam skill) 
# Step 1: Restore snapshot to a new data directory
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot restore /opt/etcd-backup.db \
  --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restored

# Step 2: Update etcd static pod to use the new data directory
# Edit /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml:
#   - Change hostPath volume from /var/lib/etcd → /var/lib/etcd-restored

# Step 3: Wait for etcd to restart automatically (kubelet watches manifests)
#   kubelet detects the manifest change and recreates the pod

# Step 4: Verify the restore
kubectl get pods -n kube-system
kubectl get nodes
🚨 CKA etcd Restore Gotcha

The --data-dir flag must point to a new directory, not the existing one. Then you update the etcd Pod manifest to point to this new path. If you restore to the same directory, etcd may fail to start. Always create a new path like /var/lib/etcd-restored.

Component Quick Reference — Ports, Configs & Logs

Component Port Config / Manifest Logs Command
kube-apiserver 6443 /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml kubectl logs kube-apiserver-* -n kube-system
etcd 2379 / 2380 /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml kubectl logs etcd-* -n kube-system
kube-scheduler 10259 /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-scheduler.yaml kubectl logs kube-scheduler-* -n kube-system
controller-manager 10257 /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-controller-manager.yaml kubectl logs kube-controller-manager-* -n kube-system
kubelet 10250 /var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml journalctl -u kubelet -f
kube-proxy 10256 ConfigMap kube-proxy in kube-system kubectl logs kube-proxy-* -n kube-system
containerd — /etc/containerd/config.toml journalctl -u containerd
CoreDNS 53 ConfigMap coredns in kube-system kubectl logs coredns-* -n kube-system
💡 CKA Pro Tip: Key File Paths to Memorize
  • /etc/kubernetes/manifests/ — static pod manifests (control plane)
  • /etc/kubernetes/pki/ — all cluster certificates (CA, server, client)
  • /etc/kubernetes/admin.conf — kubeconfig for the admin user
  • /var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml — kubelet configuration
  • /var/lib/etcd/ — etcd data directory
  • /etc/cni/net.d/ — CNI plugin configuration
  • /opt/cni/bin/ — CNI plugin binaries

Chapter 4: Core Kubernetes Objects — The Admin's Toolkit

Pods, ReplicaSets, Deployments, Services, Namespaces — the objects you'll manage daily as a CKA administrator. These are the building blocks of every Kubernetes workload.

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~2 hours 📖 Core Objects 🎯 Foundation

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Pods — the smallest deployable unit in Kubernetes
  • ✅ ReplicaSets — self-healing pod management
  • ✅ Deployments — rolling updates, rollbacks, scaling
  • ✅ Services — stable networking for ephemeral pods
  • ✅ Namespaces — logical cluster isolation
  • ✅ Labels, Selectors & Annotations — the glue connecting everything
  • ✅ Full object relationship map — how all objects interact
🎓 CKA Relevance
Workloads & Scheduling 15% Services & Networking 20%
Object Mastery = Exam Speed

Every CKA task involves core objects. You'll create Deployments, expose them with Services, troubleshoot pod issues, and manage namespaces. Master these objects imperatively (fast kubectl commands) and declaratively (YAML manifests).

4.1 Pods — The Smallest Unit

A Pod is the smallest deployable unit in Kubernetes — one or more containers sharing the same network namespace (IP) and storage volumes. You rarely create pods directly; you use Deployments. But you debug pods constantly.

Pod Anatomy — Single vs Multi-Container
  SINGLE-CONTAINER POD              MULTI-CONTAINER POD
  (most common)                     (sidecar pattern)

  ┌──────────────────────┐         ┌──────────────────────────────┐
  │  POD                  │         │  POD (shared network)         │
  │  IP: 10.244.1.5       │         │  IP: 10.244.1.8               │
  │                       │         │                                │
  │  ┌──────────────┐    │         │  ┌──────────┐ ┌──────────┐   │
  │  │ 📦 Container  │    │         │  │ 📦 Django │ │ 📦 Nginx  │   │
  │  │ anihpj:v1    │    │         │  │ port:8000│ │ port:80  │   │
  │  │ port: 8000   │    │         │  └─────┬────┘ └─────┬────┘   │
  │  └──────────────┘    │         │        │  localhost  │         │
  │                       │         │        └──────┬─────┘         │
  │                       │         │           ┌───▼────┐          │
  │                       │         │           │ Volume │          │
  │                       │         │           │(shared)│          │
  │                       │         │           └────────┘          │
  └──────────────────────┘         └──────────────────────────────┘
💡
Key Pod Facts
  • Every container in a pod shares the same IP
  • Containers communicate via localhost
  • Pods are ephemeral — they die and get replaced, never repaired
  • As admin: you debug pods, not create them directly
🔧
Essential Pod Commands
  • kubectl get pods -o wide
  • kubectl describe pod <name>
  • kubectl logs <pod> -c <container>
  • kubectl exec -it <pod> -- /bin/sh
anihpj-pod.yaml — Pod manifest 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj-pod
  labels:
    app: anihpj
    tier: backend
spec:
  containers:
  - name: anihpj
    image: anihpj:v1
    ports:
    - containerPort: 8000
    resources:
      requests:
        memory: "128Mi"
        cpu: "250m"
      limits:
        memory: "256Mi"
        cpu: "500m"

4.2 ReplicaSets — Maintaining Pod Count

A ReplicaSet ensures a specified number of identical pods are running at all times. If a pod dies, the ReplicaSet controller creates a replacement. You rarely create ReplicaSets directly — Deployments manage them for you.

ReplicaSet Self-Healing — Maintaining 3 Replicas
  ReplicaSet: anihpj-rs (replicas: 3, selector: app=anihpj)

  HEALTHY STATE:
  ┌──────┐  ┌──────┐  ┌──────┐
  │ 📦 1  │  │ 📦 2  │  │ 📦 3  │   3/3 Running ✅
  │anihpj│  │anihpj│  │anihpj│
  └──────┘  └──────┘  └──────┘

  POD 2 CRASHES:
  ┌──────┐  ┌──────┐  ┌──────┐
  │ 📦 1  │  │ 💀 2  │  │ 📦 3  │   2/3 Running ⚠️
  │anihpj│  │ DEAD │  │anihpj│
  └──────┘  └──────┘  └──────┘
                │
                │  ReplicaSet: "I need 3, only 2 running!"
                ▼
  SELF-HEALED:
  ┌──────┐  ┌──────┐  ┌──────┐
  │ 📦 1  │  │ 📦 4  │  │ 📦 3  │   3/3 Running ✅
  │anihpj│  │ NEW! │  │anihpj│   (new pod created)
  └──────┘  └──────┘  └──────┘
📖 ReplicaSet vs Deployment

A ReplicaSet only ensures pod count. A Deployment adds rolling updates, rollback history, and version tracking on top. In practice, you always use Deployments — they create and manage ReplicaSets for you.

4.3 Deployments — Managing Application Lifecycle

Deployments are the standard way to run stateless applications. You declare the desired state (image, replicas, resources), and the Deployment controller makes it happen — including rolling updates, rollbacks, and scaling.

Deployment Ownership Chain
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  Deployment: anihpj                                    │
  │  ├── manages ──▶ ReplicaSet (current: v3)             │
  │  │              ├── owns ──▶ Pod anihpj-7f8b4-abc1    │
  │  │              ├── owns ──▶ Pod anihpj-7f8b4-abc2    │
  │  │              └── owns ──▶ Pod anihpj-7f8b4-abc3    │
  │  │                                                     │
  │  ├── keeps ──▶ ReplicaSet (old: v2, 0 replicas)       │
  │  └── keeps ──▶ ReplicaSet (old: v1, 0 replicas)       │
  │                                                        │
  │  Old ReplicaSets kept for rollback history!            │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
anihpj-deployment.yaml 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
  labels:
    app: anihpj
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj
  strategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxSurge: 1
      maxUnavailable: 0
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj
        version: v3
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: anihpj
        image: anihpj:v3
        ports:
        - containerPort: 8000
        resources:
          requests:
            memory: "128Mi"
            cpu: "250m"
          limits:
            memory: "256Mi"
            cpu: "500m"

Pod Lifecycle — States You Must Know for CKA

Pod Phase Transitions
  ┌──────────┐    ┌──────────┐    ┌──────────┐
  │ Pending  │───▶│ Running  │───▶│Succeeded │
  │          │    │          │    │ (exit 0) │
  └──────────┘    └────┬─────┘    └──────────┘
       │               │
       │               ▼
       │          ┌──────────┐
       └─────────▶│  Failed  │
                  │(exit ≠ 0)│
                  └──────────┘

  STATUS COLUMN (what you see in kubectl get pods):
  ┌───────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────┐
  │ ContainerCreating │ Image pulling, volumes mounting   │
  │ Running           │ At least one container running    │
  │ Completed         │ All containers exited with 0      │
  │ Error             │ Container exited with error       │
  │ CrashLoopBackOff  │ Container keeps crashing, backoff │
  │ ImagePullBackOff  │ Can't pull container image        │
  │ Pending           │ Not yet scheduled to a node       │
  │ Terminating       │ Pod is being gracefully deleted   │
  │ Init:N/M          │ Init containers running           │
  └───────────────────┴──────────────────────────────────┘
⚠️ CKA Troubleshooting: Common Pod Statuses
  • Pending → check: scheduler (no node fits), ResourceQuota exceeded, PVC not bound
  • ImagePullBackOff → check: image name typo, private registry auth (imagePullSecrets), network
  • CrashLoopBackOff → check: kubectl logs <pod> --previous for the crash reason
  • Init:0/1 → check: init container stuck — kubectl logs <pod> -c <init-container>

Deployment Operations (CKA Speed)

terminal — deployment commands 
# Create deployment imperatively (CKA speed!)
kubectl create deployment anihpj --image=anihpj:v1 --replicas=3

# Scale up/down
kubectl scale deployment anihpj --replicas=5

# Update image (triggers rolling update)
kubectl set image deployment/anihpj anihpj=anihpj:v4

# Check rollout status
kubectl rollout status deployment/anihpj

# View rollout history
kubectl rollout history deployment/anihpj

# Rollback to previous version
kubectl rollout undo deployment/anihpj

# Rollback to specific revision
kubectl rollout undo deployment/anihpj --to-revision=2
💡 Rolling Update Strategy

maxSurge: 1 = allow 1 extra pod during update. maxUnavailable: 0 = never have fewer than desired replicas. This ensures zero-downtime deployments. The exam tests your ability to configure and troubleshoot rolling updates.

4.4 Services — Stable Network Endpoints

Pods get random IPs that change every time they restart. Services provide a stable IP and DNS name that routes traffic to healthy pods matching a label selector.

Service Type Access From Use Case Port Range
ClusterIP Inside cluster only Internal services (default) Any
NodePort External via node IP Dev/testing 30000-32767
LoadBalancer External via cloud LB Production (cloud) Any
ExternalName DNS alias (CNAME) External services N/A
Service Type Comparison — Visual
  ClusterIP (default)         NodePort                 LoadBalancer
  ┌─────────────────┐        ┌─────────────────┐      ┌─────────────────┐
  │  Inside cluster  │        │  Node:30080      │      │  Cloud LB       │
  │  only            │        │       │          │      │  203.0.113.50   │
  │                  │        │       ▼          │      │       │         │
  │  svc:10.96.0.10 │        │  svc:10.96.0.11 │      │       ▼         │
  │       │          │        │       │          │      │  svc:10.96.0.12│
  │       ▼          │        │       ▼          │      │       │         │
  │  ┌────┐ ┌────┐  │        │  ┌────┐ ┌────┐  │      │       ▼         │
  │  │pod │ │pod │  │        │  │pod │ │pod │  │      │  ┌────┐ ┌────┐ │
  │  └────┘ └────┘  │        │  └────┘ └────┘  │      │  │pod │ │pod │ │
  └─────────────────┘        └─────────────────┘      │  └────┘ └────┘ │
                                                       └─────────────────┘
anihpj-service.yaml — ClusterIP + NodePort 
# ClusterIP (internal)
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: anihpj-svc
spec:
  selector:
    app: anihpj          # matches pods with label app=anihpj
  ports:
  - port: 80             # service port
    targetPort: 8000     # pod/container port
  type: ClusterIP
---
# NodePort (external dev access)
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: anihpj-nodeport
spec:
  selector:
    app: anihpj
  ports:
  - port: 80
    targetPort: 8000
    nodePort: 30080      # accessible at NodeIP:30080
  type: NodePort
terminal — CKA imperative service creation 
# Expose deployment as ClusterIP (fast!)
kubectl expose deployment anihpj --port=80 --target-port=8000

# Expose as NodePort
kubectl expose deployment anihpj --port=80 --target-port=8000 \
  --type=NodePort --name=anihpj-nodeport

# Check service endpoints
kubectl get endpoints anihpj-svc

Kubernetes DNS — How Services Get Discovered

Every Service automatically gets a DNS name inside the cluster. CoreDNS resolves these names so pods can find each other without hard-coding IPs:

Kubernetes Service DNS Resolution
  Pod in anihpj-dev namespace:
    curl http://anihpj-svc                    ← same namespace
    curl http://anihpj-svc.anihpj-dev         ← explicit namespace
    curl http://anihpj-svc.anihpj-dev.svc     ← full service path
    curl http://anihpj-svc.anihpj-dev.svc.cluster.local  ← FQDN

  DNS Pattern:
  <service-name>.<namespace>.svc.cluster.local
  
  Cross-namespace access:
  ┌─────────────────────┐         ┌─────────────────────┐
  │ NS: anihpj-dev       │         │ NS: database         │
  │                      │  DNS    │                      │
  │ Pod: frontend        │────────▶│ Service: postgres-svc│
  │ curl postgres-svc    │         │ (ClusterIP)          │
  │   .database.svc      │         │                      │
  │   .cluster.local     │         │ ┌──────┐             │
  │                      │         │ │ 📦 pg │             │
  └─────────────────────┘         │ └──────┘             │
                                   └─────────────────────┘
💡 CKA Tip: Debugging DNS Issues

If services aren't reachable by name, check CoreDNS: kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns. Test DNS from inside a pod: kubectl exec -it <pod> -- nslookup anihpj-svc. CoreDNS logs: kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns.

4.5 Namespaces — Virtual Clusters

Namespaces provide logical isolation within a cluster. As a CKA admin, you create namespaces, apply ResourceQuotas, set RBAC policies, and manage resources across them.

Namespace Isolation — anihpj Example
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  CLUSTER                                                  │
  │                                                           │
  │  ┌──────────────────┐      ┌──────────────────┐          │
  │  │ NS: anihpj-dev    │      │ NS: anihpj-prod   │          │
  │  │ 🟢 1 replica      │      │ 🔴 3 replicas     │          │
  │  │ DEBUG=True        │      │ DEBUG=False       │          │
  │  │ limits: low       │      │ limits: high      │          │
  │  └──────────────────┘      └──────────────────┘          │
  │                                                           │
  │  ┌──────────────────┐      ┌──────────────────┐          │
  │  │ NS: kube-system   │      │ NS: monitoring    │          │
  │  │ 🔧 CoreDNS        │      │ 📊 Prometheus     │          │
  │  │    kube-proxy     │      │    Grafana        │          │
  │  └──────────────────┘      └──────────────────┘          │
  │                                                           │
  │  Each namespace can have its own:                          │
  │  • ResourceQuotas  • RBAC Roles  • NetworkPolicies        │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

Default Namespaces

📦
default

Where objects go if no namespace is specified. Don't use in production.

🔧
kube-system

System components: CoreDNS, kube-proxy, metrics-server. Don't deploy apps here.

🌍
kube-public

Readable by all (even unauthenticated). Contains cluster-info ConfigMap.

💓
kube-node-lease

Node heartbeat Lease objects. Used for node health detection.

terminal — namespace commands 
# Create namespace
kubectl create namespace anihpj-dev

# List all namespaces
kubectl get namespaces

# Run commands in a specific namespace
kubectl get pods -n anihpj-dev

# Set default namespace for current context
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=anihpj-dev

# List ALL pods across ALL namespaces
kubectl get pods -A
⚠️ CKA Exam Namespace Traps

The CKA exam gives tasks in different namespaces. Always read the namespace instruction at the top of each question. If you create a resource in the wrong namespace, you get zero marks. Use -n <namespace> or switch context with kubectl config set-context.

ResourceQuota — Limiting Namespace Resources

As a CKA admin, you enforce resource limits per namespace to prevent one team from consuming the entire cluster:

anihpj-quota.yaml — ResourceQuota 
apiVersion: v1
kind: ResourceQuota
metadata:
  name: anihpj-quota
  namespace: anihpj-prod
spec:
  hard:
    requests.cpu: "4"
    requests.memory: 8Gi
    limits.cpu: "8"
    limits.memory: 16Gi
    pods: "20"
    services: "10"
    persistentvolumeclaims: "5"
💡 LimitRange vs ResourceQuota

ResourceQuota limits the total resources for an entire namespace. LimitRange sets default and max limits for individual pods/containers. Use both together: ResourceQuota caps the namespace, LimitRange ensures no single pod is too greedy.

4.6 Labels, Selectors, and Annotations

Labels are key-value pairs attached to objects for organizing and selecting. They're the glue that connects Services to Pods, ReplicaSets to Pods, and NetworkPolicies to targets.

🏷️
Labels

For selection and grouping. Used by selectors to match objects. Keep them meaningful.

  • app: anihpj
  • tier: backend
  • env: production
  • version: v3
📝
Annotations

For metadata and tools. Not used for selection. Store non-identifying info.

  • description: "Job portal backend"
  • owner: "team-alpha"
  • last-deployed: "2024-01-15"
  • prometheus.io/scrape: "true"
How Labels Connect Objects
  Service (selector: app=anihpj)
       │
       │ finds pods with matching labels
       ▼
  ┌──────────────┐  ┌──────────────┐  ┌──────────────┐
  │ Pod           │  │ Pod           │  │ Pod           │
  │ app: anihpj  │  │ app: anihpj  │  │ app: other   │
  │ tier: backend│  │ tier: backend│  │ tier: frontend│
  │ ✅ MATCHED    │  │ ✅ MATCHED    │  │ ❌ NOT matched│
  └──────────────┘  └──────────────┘  └──────────────┘
terminal — label commands 
# Add labels to existing pods
kubectl label pod anihpj-pod tier=backend

# Show labels
kubectl get pods --show-labels

# Filter by label selector
kubectl get pods -l app=anihpj
kubectl get pods -l "app=anihpj,tier=backend"
kubectl get pods -l "app in (anihpj, other-app)"

# Remove a label (trailing dash)
kubectl label pod anihpj-pod tier-

4.7 Kubernetes Object Relationship Map

This is the big picture — how every core object connects to form a working application deployment in Kubernetes.

How All Kubernetes Objects Connect
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                             │
  │  Deployment ──manages──▶ ReplicaSet ──owns──▶ Pods         │
  │       │                                        │            │
  │       │ references                    labels   │            │
  │       ▼                                        ▼            │
  │  ConfigMap ◀──────── envFrom ──────── Pod spec │            │
  │  Secret    ◀──────── envFrom ──────── Pod spec │            │
  │                                        │       │            │
  │  Service ────selector────matches───────┘       │            │
  │       │                                                     │
  │       ▼                                                     │
  │  Ingress ──routes traffic──▶ Service                        │
  │                                                             │
  │  PVC ◀──────── volumeMount ──────── Pod spec                │
  │   │                                                         │
  │   ▼                                                         │
  │  PV / StorageClass                                          │
  │                                                             │
  │  Namespace ──contains all above──                           │
  │  RBAC ──controls access to all above──                      │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

🔑 Part 1 Key Takeaways for CKA

  • ✅ Kubernetes — container orchestration: deployment, scaling, management
  • ✅ Architecture — Control Plane (API Server, etcd, Scheduler, Controller Manager) + Worker Nodes (kubelet, kube-proxy)
  • ✅ API Server is the ONLY component that talks to etcd
  • ✅ Core objects — Pod (smallest), Deployment (manages ReplicaSets), Service (networking), Namespace (isolation)
  • ✅ Labels connect Services to Pods, ReplicaSets to Pods, and NetworkPolicies to targets
  • ✅ Everything is declarative — tell K8s WHAT you want, not HOW
terminal — must-remember CKA commands 
# Cluster overview
kubectl cluster-info
kubectl get nodes -o wide
kubectl api-resources

# Object inspection
kubectl get all -A
kubectl describe <resource> <name>
kubectl get <resource> -o yaml

# Speed commands (CKA exam)
kubectl run nginx --image=nginx                  # create pod
kubectl create deployment web --image=nginx      # create deployment
kubectl expose deployment web --port=80          # create service

🟢 Part 2: Getting Started — Hands-On Basics Chapters 5-8

Chapter 5: Setting Up Your Environment

Before you can manage a Kubernetes cluster, you need one to practice on. This chapter walks you through installing the tools, connecting to a cluster, managing multiple contexts, and getting your environment CKA-exam ready. Everything uses our anihpj/jobpost Django project as the target workload.

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~1 hour 🛠️ Setup 💻 Hands-On

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Choose the right local Kubernetes tool — Minikube, Kind, k3s, kubeadm, Docker Desktop
  • ✅ Install and start Minikube with the optimal settings for CKA practice
  • ✅ Install kubectl and verify it connects to your cluster
  • ✅ Understand kubeconfig — the file that controls all cluster connections
  • ✅ Switch between multiple clusters and contexts (directly tested on CKA)
  • ✅ Verify cluster health and enable essential addons
