🎓 CKA Relevance
Cluster Architecture 25% Troubleshooting 30%
The CKA Exam Environment

On the CKA exam you'll be given multiple pre-built clusters and asked to switch between them. You must know kubectl config use-context and how kubeconfig works — it's tested in the very first instruction of most questions. Practice context switching until it's muscle memory.

  • Context switching — Every question starts with: "Use context: k8s-cluster-X". You must switch before answering.
  • kubectl is pre-installed — But knowing aliases and auto-completion saves critical exam time.
  • Bookmark kubernetes.io/docs — The only allowed reference during the exam.

5.1 Choosing a Local Kubernetes Tool

There are several tools to run Kubernetes locally. Each has trade-offs — the right choice depends on what you're practicing for:

Tool Best For Nodes Resources Install CKA Prep?
Minikube Learning, interactive 1 → multi Medium Easy ✅ Start here
Kind CI/CD, testing Multi Low Easy ✅ Good
k3s Edge, lightweight Multi Very Low Easy ⚠️ Differs from full K8s
kubeadm Real clusters Multi High Manual ⚠️ ✅ Must learn
Docker Desktop Windows/Mac dev 1 Medium Very Easy ⚠️ Limited
💡 CKA Learning Path Recommendation

Start with Minikube for Chapters 5-8 (instant setup, great for learning). Then switch to kubeadm in Part 3 (Chapter 9) to practice building clusters from scratch — this is directly tested on the CKA exam.

5.2 Installing Minikube (Quick Start)

Minikube creates a single-node Kubernetes cluster inside a VM or container on your laptop. One command and you have a fully functional cluster.

Installation by OS

🪟
Windows

choco install minikube
or
winget install minikube

🍎
macOS

brew install minikube

🐧
Linux (x86)

curl -LO https://storage.googleapis.com/minikube/releases/latest/minikube-linux-amd64
sudo install minikube-linux-amd64 /usr/local/bin/minikube

🐳
Driver

Requires Docker, VirtualBox, or Hyper-V. Docker is recommended.

Starting Your First Cluster

terminal 
# Start a cluster with optimal CKA practice settings
minikube start --driver=docker --cpus=2 --memory=4096

# Expected output:
# 😄  minikube v1.33.0 on Darwin 14.0
# ✨  Using the docker driver based on user configuration
# 📦  Preparing Kubernetes v1.30.0 on Docker 26.0.1...
# 🔗  Configuring bridge networking...
# 🏄  Done! kubectl is now configured to use "minikube" cluster

# Check status
minikube status
# minikube
# type: Control Plane
# host: Running
# kubelet: Running
# apiserver: Running
# kubeconfig: Configured

# For multi-node practice (CKA-relevant):
minikube start --nodes=3 --driver=docker --cpus=2 --memory=4096
📖 Multi-Node Practice

Use --nodes=3 when practicing node operations like kubectl drain, kubectl cordon, and node troubleshooting — these are heavily tested on the CKA. Single-node is fine for basic learning.

Minikube Architecture — What's Running Inside
  Your Machine (Host OS)
  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                │
  │   Docker Engine / Hypervisor                                   │
  │   ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │   │  Minikube Node (VM or Container)                       │   │
  │   │                                                        │   │
  │   │  ┌──────────── Control Plane ──────────────────────┐   │   │
  │   │  │  kube-apiserver    ← All kubectl commands go here│  │   │
  │   │  │  etcd              ← Cluster state database      │  │   │
  │   │  │  kube-scheduler    ← Assigns pods to node        │  │   │
  │   │  │  controller-manager ← Manages deployments, RS    │  │   │
  │   │  │  CoreDNS           ← Internal DNS for Services   │  │   │
  │   │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │   │
  │   │                                                        │   │
  │   │  ┌──────────── Worker Components ──────────────────┐   │   │
  │   │  │  kubelet           ← Runs pods on this node      │  │   │
  │   │  │  kube-proxy        ← Network rules (Services)    │  │   │
  │   │  │  containerd        ← Container runtime           │  │   │
  │   │  │  ┌─────┐ ┌─────┐ ┌─────┐                       │  │   │
  │   │  │  │Pod 1│ │Pod 2│ │Pod 3│  ← Your workloads      │  │   │
  │   │  │  └─────┘ └─────┘ └─────┘                       │  │   │
  │   │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │   │
  │   └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                                                                │
  │   kubectl (your laptop) ──HTTPS──▶ API Server :8443            │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  Key insight: Minikube runs BOTH control plane AND worker on the SAME node.
  Real clusters separate these onto different machines.

🔧 Troubleshooting Common Minikube Issues

If Minikube doesn't start cleanly, try these fixes:

Error Likely Cause Fix
Exiting due to DRV_NOT_DETECTED Docker/Hyper-V not running Start Docker Desktop, then retry
insufficient memory Not enough RAM allocated minikube start --memory=2048 (lower)
Unable to connect to the server Cluster crashed or IP changed minikube delete && minikube start
apiserver not responding Stale cluster state minikube stop && minikube start
Slow startup (> 5 min) First-time image pull Normal — K8s images are large (~1 GB)
minikube-lifecycle.sh 
# Minikube lifecycle commands (know these)
minikube start         # Start cluster
minikube stop          # Stop cluster (preserves state)
minikube delete        # Destroy cluster (fresh start)
minikube pause         # Pause — free CPU but keep state
minikube unpause       # Resume paused cluster
minikube ssh           # SSH into the Minikube node
minikube ip            # Get the Minikube node IP address
minikube logs          # View Minikube system logs (for debugging)

5.3 Installing kubectl

kubectl is the CLI tool you'll use to interact with any Kubernetes cluster — local or remote, Minikube or production. Think of it as the "remote control" for your cluster.

installation-per-os.sh 
# Windows
choco install kubernetes-cli

# macOS
brew install kubectl

# Linux
curl -LO "https://dl.k8s.io/release/$(curl -L -s https://dl.k8s.io/release/stable.txt)/bin/linux/amd64/kubectl"
sudo install kubectl /usr/local/bin/kubectl

# Verify installation
kubectl version --client
# Client Version: v1.30.0
# Kustomize Version: v5.0.4
How kubectl Connects to Your Cluster
  ┌─────────────────┐         HTTPS (TLS)          ┌─────────────────────┐
  │  YOU             │                               │  KUBERNETES CLUSTER │
  │                  │    ~/.kube/config tells        │                     │
  │  $ kubectl get   │    kubectl WHERE and WHO       │  ┌───────────────┐ │
  │    pods          │──────────────────────────────▶ │  │  API Server   │ │
  │                  │    1. Read kubeconfig           │  │  :6443        │ │
  │  ┌────────────┐  │    2. Find server URL           │  └───────┬───────┘ │
  │  │  kubectl   │  │    3. Authenticate (cert/token) │          │         │
  │  └────────────┘  │    4. Send REST request         │          ▼         │
  │                  │                                 │  ┌───────────────┐ │
  │  ┌────────────┐  │    5. Receive JSON response      │  │    etcd       │ │
  │  │ kubeconfig │  │◀ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─ ─  │  │  (data store) │ │
  │  └────────────┘  │    6. Format & display           │  └───────────────┘ │
  └─────────────────┘                                 └─────────────────────┘
💡 CKA Speed Boost: Shell Aliases & Autocomplete

Set these up immediately — they save massive time on the exam:

~/.bashrc 
# Essential CKA aliases — add to ~/.bashrc
alias k='kubectl'
alias kg='kubectl get'
alias kd='kubectl describe'
alias kaf='kubectl apply -f'
alias kdel='kubectl delete'
alias kl='kubectl logs'
alias ke='kubectl exec -it'
alias kns='kubectl config set-context --current --namespace'

# Enable auto-completion (provided on CKA exam)
source <(kubectl completion bash)
complete -o default -F __start_kubectl k

# Quick dry-run alias
alias kdr='kubectl --dry-run=client -o yaml'

# Verify aliases work
k get nodes

🔌 kubectl Plugins Worth Knowing

While not allowed on the CKA exam, these plugins accelerate daily work. Install via krew (the kubectl plugin manager):

Plugin What It Does Install Example
kubectx Fast context switching (interactive) kubectl krew install ctx kubectl ctx dev
kubens Fast namespace switching kubectl krew install ns kubectl ns anihpj-dev
neat Clean up -o yaml output (remove clutter) kubectl krew install neat kubectl get pod nginx -o yaml | kubectl neat
tree Show resource ownership hierarchy kubectl krew install tree kubectl tree deployment nginx

5.4 Understanding kubeconfig

The ~/.kube/config file is the single most important configuration file for kubectl. It tells kubectl where to connect, who you are, and which namespace to use by default. The CKA exam relies heavily on this.

kubeconfig — The 3 Building Blocks
  ~/.kube/config
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                          │
  │  clusters:       ← WHERE to connect                     │
  │  ├── minikube    (https://192.168.49.2:8443)            │
  │  ├── staging     (https://staging.k8s.example.com)      │
  │  └── prod        (https://prod.k8s.example.com)         │
  │                                                          │
  │  users:          ← WHO you are                           │
  │  ├── minikube-user   (client certificate)               │
  │  ├── dev-user        (token)                             │
  │  └── admin-user      (client certificate)               │
  │                                                          │
  │  contexts:       ← cluster + user + namespace            │
  │  ├── minikube    (minikube + minikube-user)              │
  │  ├── dev         (staging + dev-user + anihpj-dev)      │
  │  └── prod        (prod + admin-user + anihpj-prod)      │
  │                                                          │
  │  current-context: minikube   ← active connection         │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  Context = Cluster + User + Namespace
  ┌────────────┐   ┌────────────┐   ┌──────────────┐
  │  🖥️ Cluster  │ + │  👤 User    │ + │  📂 Namespace │
  │  WHERE      │   │  WHO       │   │  SCOPE        │
  └────────────┘   └────────────┘   └──────────────┘
             \\          │          /
              ▼          ▼        ▼
         ┌──────────────────────────┐
         │  📋 Context = all three   │
         │  "dev" = staging +        │
         │  dev-user + anihpj-dev    │
         └──────────────────────────┘
~/.kube/config 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Config
current-context: minikube        # ← active context

clusters:
- cluster:
    certificate-authority: /home/user/.minikube/ca.crt
    server: https://192.168.49.2:8443
  name: minikube

users:
- name: minikube-user
  user:
    client-certificate: /home/user/.minikube/profiles/minikube/client.crt
    client-key: /home/user/.minikube/profiles/minikube/client.key

contexts:
- context:
    cluster: minikube
    user: minikube-user
    namespace: default           # ← default namespace for this context
  name: minikube
⚠️ CKA Exam Warning: Always Check Your Context

Every CKA question specifies which context to use. Running commands in the wrong context = wrong cluster = wrong answer = zero points. Always run kubectl config current-context before answering.

5.5 Managing Multiple Clusters and Contexts

In real environments (and on the CKA exam), you work with multiple clusters. Context management commands are your lifeline.

context-management.sh 
# List all contexts
kubectl config get-contexts
# CURRENT   NAME       CLUSTER    AUTHINFO        NAMESPACE
# *         minikube   minikube   minikube-user   default
#           dev        staging    dev-user        anihpj-dev
#           prod       prod       admin-user      anihpj-prod

# Show current context
kubectl config current-context
# minikube

# Switch to a different context
kubectl config use-context dev
# Switched to context "dev".

# Set default namespace for current context
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=anihpj-dev
# Context "dev" modified.

# Create a new context
kubectl config set-context anihpj-staging \
  --cluster=staging \
  --user=dev-user \
  --namespace=anihpj-staging

# Merge multiple kubeconfig files
export KUBECONFIG=~/.kube/config:~/.kube/config-staging:~/.kube/config-prod
kubectl config get-contexts    # shows all merged contexts
📋Read Question
→
🔄Switch Context
→
✅Verify Context
→
⌨️Answer Question
💡 Practice Exercise: Simulate CKA Context Switching

Create multiple contexts on Minikube to practice the exact workflow used on the CKA exam:

cka-context-practice.sh 
# Setup: Create 3 simulated contexts (all point to minikube)
kubectl config set-context cka-cluster1 \
  --cluster=minikube --user=minikube --namespace=default
kubectl config set-context cka-cluster2 \
  --cluster=minikube --user=minikube --namespace=kube-system
kubectl config set-context cka-cluster3 \
  --cluster=minikube --user=minikube --namespace=anihpj-dev

# ── Simulate CKA Question 1 ──
# "Task: Use context: cka-cluster1. List all pods in default namespace."
kubectl config use-context cka-cluster1
kubectl config current-context   # Verify: cka-cluster1
kubectl get pods

# ── Simulate CKA Question 2 ──
# "Task: Use context: cka-cluster2. Find kube-proxy pods."
kubectl config use-context cka-cluster2
kubectl config current-context   # Verify: cka-cluster2
kubectl get pods | grep kube-proxy

# ── Simulate CKA Question 3 ──
# "Task: Use context: cka-cluster3. Scale deployment anihpj to 3 replicas."
kubectl config use-context cka-cluster3
kubectl config current-context   # Verify: cka-cluster3
kubectl scale deployment anihpj --replicas=3

# Cleanup practice contexts
kubectl config delete-context cka-cluster1
kubectl config delete-context cka-cluster2
kubectl config delete-context cka-cluster3
kubectl config use-context minikube

5.6 Verifying Your Cluster

After setting up your environment, verify everything works. These are the same commands you'll use on the CKA to orient yourself in unfamiliar clusters.

cluster-verification.sh 
# 1. Cluster info — where is the API server?
kubectl cluster-info

# 2. Node status — are all nodes Ready?
kubectl get nodes -o wide

kubectl get pods -n kube-system

cluster-info

Confirm API server URL is reachable

2
get nodes

All nodes should show STATUS: Ready

3
get pods -n kube-system

All control plane pods should be Running

4
healthz endpoint

API should return "ok"

🚨 CKA Troubleshooting: Node Not Ready?

If kubectl get nodes shows NotReady, use these commands to diagnose:

Symptom Check Command Common Cause
Node STATUS: NotReady kubectl describe node <name> kubelet stopped, network plugin down
Pods stuck in Pending kubectl describe pod <name> No schedulable nodes, resource limits
kube-system pods CrashLoop kubectl logs -n kube-system <pod> etcd disk full, config errors
API server unreachable systemctl status kubelet (on node) kubelet service not running

5.7 Enabling Essential Addons

Minikube addons extend your cluster with features that are standard in production. Enable these for a realistic practice environment:

Addon What It Enables CKA Relevant? Command
metrics-server CPU/memory metrics, kubectl top ✅ Yes minikube addons enable metrics-server
ingress NGINX Ingress controller for HTTP routing ✅ Yes minikube addons enable ingress
dashboard Web UI for visual cluster management ⚠️ Not tested minikube addons enable dashboard
storage-provisioner Dynamic PV provisioning ✅ Yes Enabled by default
enable-addons.sh 
# Enable essential addons for CKA practice
minikube addons enable metrics-server
minikube addons enable ingress

# Verify addons are running
minikube addons list | grep enabled
# |  ingress          | minikube | enabled ✅ |
# |  metrics-server   | minikube | enabled ✅ |
# |  storage-provisioner | minikube | enabled ✅ |

# Verify metrics-server is working (wait ~60 seconds after enabling)
kubectl top nodes
# NAME       CPU(cores)   CPU%   MEMORY(bytes)   MEMORY%
# minikube   250m         12%    1024Mi          25%

📋 Complete Environment Readiness Checklist

Run this one-shot script to verify your entire environment is ready for CKA practice:

environment-readiness-check.sh 
#!/bin/bash
echo "══════════════════════════════════════════════"
echo "  CKA Environment Readiness Check"
echo "══════════════════════════════════════════════"

# 1. kubectl installed?
echo -n "✓ kubectl: "; kubectl version --client --short 2>/dev/null || echo "❌ NOT INSTALLED"

# 2. Cluster reachable?
echo -n "✓ Cluster: "; kubectl cluster-info 2>/dev/null | head -1 || echo "❌ NOT REACHABLE"

# 3. Nodes ready?
echo "✓ Nodes:"
kubectl get nodes -o wide 2>/dev/null || echo "  ❌ Cannot list nodes"

# 4. Control plane pods running?
echo "✓ Control Plane Components:"
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -o custom-columns=\
"NAME:.metadata.name,STATUS:.status.phase,RESTARTS:.status.containerStatuses[0].restartCount" \
2>/dev/null | grep -E "etcd|apiserver|scheduler|controller"

# 5. DNS working?
echo -n "✓ CoreDNS: "
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns --no-headers 2>/dev/null | \
awk '{print $3}' || echo "❌"

# 6. Metrics server?
echo -n "✓ Metrics: "
kubectl top nodes 2>/dev/null | head -1 || echo "⚠️ Not enabled (run: minikube addons enable metrics-server)"

# 7. Alias configured?
echo -n "✓ Alias 'k': "
type k 2>/dev/null && echo "configured" || echo "⚠️ Add: alias k='kubectl'"

# 8. Bash completion?
echo -n "✓ Completion: "
complete -p kubectl 2>/dev/null && echo "active" || echo "⚠️ Run: source <(kubectl completion bash)"

echo "══════════════════════════════════════════════"
echo "  All checks complete!"
echo "══════════════════════════════════════════════"
📖 Chapter 5 Complete — Your Environment is Ready!

You now have a working Kubernetes cluster with Minikube, kubectl configured with auto-completion, and essential addons enabled. You understand kubeconfig and can switch between contexts. Next: mastering the kubectl commands you'll use every day.

Chapter 6: kubectl — The Administrator's Command Center

kubectl is the single tool you'll use for every CKA exam question and every day as a Kubernetes administrator. This chapter breaks down the syntax, the essential commands, output formatting, and the critical difference between imperative and declarative approaches.

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~1.5 hours ⌨️ CLI 🎯 CKA Essential

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ The kubectl command anatomy — verb + resource + name + flags
  • ✅ Essential admin commands organized by category (get, describe, create, debug)
  • ✅ kubectl explain — your built-in API documentation during the exam
  • ✅ Imperative vs Declarative — when to use each and the dry-run trick
  • ✅ Output formatting with JSONPath, custom-columns, and label selectors
🎓 CKA Relevance
All Domains 100%
kubectl Speed = CKA Pass/Fail

The CKA is a timed, hands-on exam. You have 2 hours for 15-20 tasks. Every second counts. Administrators who type full resource names lose time. Administrators who know --dry-run=client -o yaml finish early. This chapter is the most important for exam speed.

  • Use aliases — k instead of kubectl saves ~8 characters per command
  • Use kubectl explain — faster than searching docs.kubernetes.io
  • Use imperative + dry-run — generate YAML skeletons in seconds

6.1 kubectl Syntax Pattern

Every kubectl command follows the same anatomy. Learning this pattern once lets you construct any command from memory:

kubectl Command Anatomy
  kubectl     get       pods      anihpj-xyz     -n anihpj-dev
  ───────    ─────    ───────    ───────────    ──────────────
  command    VERB     RESOURCE     NAME            FLAGS

  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  VERB (what to do):                                        │
  │  get, describe, create, apply, delete, edit, logs, exec,   │
  │  top, patch, label, annotate, scale, rollout, drain,       │
  │  cordon, uncordon, taint, auth, explain, port-forward      │
  │                                                            │
  │  RESOURCE (what to act on):                                │
  │  pods (po), deployments (deploy), services (svc),          │
  │  nodes (no), namespaces (ns), configmaps (cm),             │
  │  secrets, ingress (ing), pv, pvc, sa, roles, clusterroles  │
  │                                                            │
  │  FLAGS (modifiers):                                        │
  │  -n namespace, -o output, -l labels, --all-namespaces (-A),│
  │  --sort-by, --field-selector, -w (watch), -f (file/follow) │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
💡 Resource Short Names Save Time

Use abbreviations on the CKA: po=pods, deploy=deployments, svc=services, no=nodes, ns=namespaces, cm=configmaps, ing=ingress, pv=persistentvolumes, pvc=persistentvolumeclaims, sa=serviceaccounts. Find all with kubectl api-resources.

6.2 Essential Admin Commands — Daily Use

These are the commands you'll use every single day as a Kubernetes administrator. They're organized by what you're trying to do:

📋 Get Information

get-commands.sh 
# List resources
kubectl get nodes -o wide              # nodes with IP, OS, runtime
kubectl get pods -A                     # ALL pods in ALL namespaces
kubectl get pods -o yaml                # full YAML definition
kubectl get pods --sort-by=.status.startTime  # sorted by start time
kubectl get all -n anihpj-dev           # everything in namespace

# Resource usage (requires metrics-server)
kubectl top nodes                       # node CPU/memory usage
kubectl top pods -n anihpj-dev          # pod CPU/memory usage

🔍 Inspect Details

describe-commands.sh 
# Detailed info (events, conditions, volumes, etc.)
kubectl describe pod anihpj-xyz -n anihpj-dev
kubectl describe node worker-1
kubectl get events --sort-by=.lastTimestamp -n anihpj-dev

✏️ Create and Modify

create-modify-commands.sh 
# Apply manifests
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml
kubectl create namespace anihpj-dev

# Edit live objects
kubectl edit deployment anihpj -n anihpj-dev
kubectl patch deployment anihpj -p '{"spec":{"replicas":5}}' -n anihpj-dev

# Labels and taints (CKA-important)
kubectl label node worker-1 disk=ssd
kubectl taint node worker-1 key=value:NoSchedule

🗑️ Delete and Clean Up

delete-commands.sh 
kubectl delete pod anihpj-xyz -n anihpj-dev
kubectl delete -f deployment.yaml
kubectl delete pods --field-selector status.phase=Failed -n anihpj-dev

🐛 Debug and Troubleshoot

debug-commands.sh 
# Logs
kubectl logs anihpj-xyz -n anihpj-dev
kubectl logs anihpj-xyz --previous -n anihpj-dev   # crashed container logs

# Interactive shell
kubectl exec -it anihpj-xyz -n anihpj-dev -- /bin/sh

# Port forwarding
kubectl port-forward pod/anihpj-xyz 8000:8000 -n anihpj-dev

# Debug a node (creates ephemeral debug pod)
kubectl debug node/worker-1 --image=busybox
📋
get

List resources. Add -o wide for extra detail.

🔍
describe

Deep dive. Shows events, conditions, volumes.

📝
logs

Container output. Add --previous for crashes.

🐚
exec -it

Shell into a running container for debugging.

🏷️ Labels & Selectors — The Glue of Kubernetes

Labels are key-value pairs attached to objects. Selectors use them to find and group resources. Services find pods by labels. Deployments manage pods by labels. Understanding this linkage is critical:

labels-and-selectors.sh 
# Add a label to a pod
kubectl label pod anihpj-xyz app=anihpj -n anihpj-dev

# Show labels
kubectl get pods --show-labels -n anihpj-dev
# NAME           READY   STATUS    LABELS
# anihpj-abc12   1/1     Running   app=anihpj,version=v1

# Filter by label (equality)
kubectl get pods -l app=anihpj

# Filter by label (set-based)
kubectl get pods -l 'app in (anihpj, nginx)'
kubectl get pods -l 'app notin (redis)'
kubectl get pods -l 'environment'              # has label
kubectl get pods -l '!environment'             # doesn't have label

# Remove a label (trailing minus sign)
kubectl label pod anihpj-xyz version- -n anihpj-dev

# Overwrite a label
kubectl label pod anihpj-xyz version=v2 --overwrite -n anihpj-dev
How Labels Connect Everything
  Deployment                  Service                    Ingress
  selector:                   selector:                  backend:
    matchLabels:                app: anihpj                service:
      app: anihpj                                           name: anihpj-svc
       │                          │
       │   Labels are the          │
       └───────────────┬──────────┘
                   │
                   ▼
       ┌──────────────────────┐
       │  Pod                      │
       │  labels:                  │
       │    app: anihpj  ◀── match  │
       │    version: v1            │
       └──────────────────────┘

  If labels don't match → Service has 0 endpoints → App unreachable!

⚡ Top 15 CKA Must-Know Commands — Quick Reference

These commands appear in nearly every CKA question. Memorize them:

# Task Command
1 Switch context kubectl config use-context <name>
2 Set default namespace kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=<ns>
3 Generate YAML from command kubectl run nginx --image=nginx --dry-run=client -o yaml
4 Create deployment kubectl create deploy <name> --image=<img> --replicas=<n>
5 Expose as service kubectl expose deploy <name> --port=80 --target-port=8000
6 Scale deployment kubectl scale deploy <name> --replicas=5
7 View pod logs kubectl logs <pod> [-c container] [--previous]
8 Shell into pod kubectl exec -it <pod> -- /bin/sh
9 Get all in namespace kubectl get all -n <namespace>
10 Describe resource kubectl describe <resource> <name> -n <ns>
11 Find field paths kubectl explain <resource>.spec --recursive
12 Drain a node kubectl drain <node> --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data
13 Cordon/uncordon node kubectl cordon <node> / kubectl uncordon <node>
14 Rollout status/undo kubectl rollout status deploy/<name> / kubectl rollout undo
15 Apply a manifest kubectl apply -f <file.yaml>

6.3 kubectl explain — Built-in API Documentation

kubectl explain is your best friend during the CKA exam. It shows you the exact fields available for any resource — no internet search needed. The exam allows access to kubernetes.io/docs, but explain is often faster.

kubectl-explain.sh 
# Top-level fields for a resource
kubectl explain pod
# KIND:     Pod
# VERSION:  v1
# FIELDS:   apiVersion, kind, metadata, spec, status

# Drill into nested fields
kubectl explain pod.spec
kubectl explain pod.spec.containers
kubectl explain pod.spec.containers.resources
kubectl explain pod.spec.containers.resources.limits

# See ALL fields recursively (great for finding field paths)
kubectl explain deployment.spec.strategy --recursive
# strategy     <Object>
#    rollingUpdate     <Object>
#       maxSurge       <string>
#       maxUnavailable <string>
#    type              <string>

# Find specific field names
kubectl explain pod.spec.containers.livenessProbe --recursive
kubectl explain — Drill-Down Navigation
  kubectl explain pod
       │
       ▼
  ┌─────────────────┐
  │ apiVersion       │
  │ kind             │
  │ metadata ────────┼──▶ kubectl explain pod.metadata
  │ spec ────────────┼──▶ kubectl explain pod.spec
  │ status           │         │
  └─────────────────┘         ▼
                         ┌─────────────────────┐
                         │ containers ──────────┼──▶ kubectl explain pod.spec.containers
                         │ volumes              │         │
                         │ nodeName             │         ▼
                         │ serviceAccountName   │    ┌──────────────────┐
                         │ tolerations          │    │ image             │
                         │ nodeSelector         │    │ ports             │
                         └─────────────────────┘    │ env               │
                                                     │ resources ────────┼──▶ .resources.limits
                                                     │ volumeMounts      │
                                                     │ livenessProbe     │
                                                     │ readinessProbe    │
                                                     └──────────────────┘
💡 CKA Pro Tip: Explain + Dry-Run = Speed

Forgot the field name for resource limits? kubectl explain pod.spec.containers.resources --recursive in 2 seconds. Then use kubectl run nginx --image=nginx --dry-run=client -o yaml to generate the skeleton and add the field. This combo is faster than searching docs.

6.4 Imperative vs Declarative — When to Use Each

Kubernetes supports two approaches to managing resources. Understanding when to use each is critical for both the CKA exam and production work.

Imperative vs Declarative — Two Approaches
  🏃 IMPERATIVE: "Do this NOW"               📝 DECLARATIVE: "Make it like THIS"
  ┌──────────────────────────────────┐       ┌──────────────────────────────────┐
  │ $ kubectl run anihpj \            │       │ $ kubectl apply -f deploy.yaml   │
  │     --image=anihpj:v1             │       │                                  │
  │ $ kubectl scale deploy anihpj \   │       │ deploy.yaml defines desired state│
  │     --replicas=5                  │       │ K8s figures out what to change   │
  │ $ kubectl expose deploy anihpj \  │       │                                  │
  │     --port=80                     │       │ Like ordering from a menu:       │
  │                                    │       │ "I want pasta" — kitchen handles │
  │ Like giving step-by-step cooking  │       │ the recipe                       │
  │ instructions to a chef            │       │                                  │
  │                                    │       │                                  │
  │ ✅ Fast for one-off tasks         │       │ ✅ Repeatable, version-controlled │
  │ ✅ Great for CKA exam speed       │       │ ✅ Production standard            │
  │ ❌ No record of what you did      │       │ ✅ GitOps friendly                │
  │ ❌ Hard to reproduce              │       │ ❌ Slower for quick tests         │
  └──────────────────────────────────┘       └──────────────────────────────────┘
Approach When to Use CKA Exam Production
Imperative Quick tasks, generate YAML ✅ Use for speed ❌ Avoid
Declarative Real environments, repeatable ✅ Required for complex tasks ✅ Always

The CKA Power Move: Imperative + Dry-Run = YAML Generator

dry-run-yaml-generators.sh 
# Generate Pod YAML (don't create, just output)
kubectl run anihpj --image=anihpj:v1 --port=8000 \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > pod.yaml

# Generate Deployment YAML
kubectl create deployment anihpj --image=anihpj:v1 --replicas=3 \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > deployment.yaml

# Generate Service YAML
kubectl expose deployment anihpj --port=80 --target-port=8000 \
  --type=ClusterIP --dry-run=client -o yaml > service.yaml

# Generate Namespace YAML
kubectl create namespace anihpj-dev \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > namespace.yaml

# Generate ConfigMap YAML
kubectl create configmap anihpj-config \
  --from-literal=DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE=anihpj.settings \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > configmap.yaml

# Then edit the generated YAML and apply:
vim deployment.yaml   # add resource limits, env vars, etc.
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml
⚠️ CKA Exam Critical: --dry-run=client vs --dry-run=server

--dry-run=client validates locally (no API call), always use this for YAML generation. --dry-run=server validates against the API server (checks if resource already exists, validates references). Use server for pre-apply validation.

🎯 Real CKA Scenario: "Create a Deployment with Specific Requirements"

The exam gives you constraints: name, image, replicas, port, namespace, labels. Here's the fastest workflow:

cka-scenario-workflow.sh 
# STEP 1: Generate the skeleton (5 sec)
kubectl create deployment web-app \
  --image=nginx:1.25 \
  --replicas=3 \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > web-app.yaml

# STEP 2: Edit to add requirements (30 sec)
# vim web-app.yaml
# → Add resource limits, nodeSelector, labels, etc.

# STEP 3: Validate before applying (5 sec)
kubectl apply -f web-app.yaml --dry-run=server
# deployment.apps/web-app created (server dry run)

# STEP 4: Apply for real (2 sec)
kubectl apply -f web-app.yaml

# STEP 5: Verify (5 sec)
kubectl get deployment web-app -o wide
kubectl get pods -l app=web-app

# Total: ~47 seconds → vs 3+ minutes writing YAML from scratch!
⚡Generate
→
✏️Edit
→
✅Validate
→
🚀Apply
→
🔍Verify

6.5 Output Formatting and Filtering

The CKA frequently asks you to extract specific information — pod names, node IPs, container images. Mastering output formatting separates fast solvers from slow ones.

Flag Purpose Example
-o wide Extra columns (IP, node, etc.) kubectl get pods -o wide
-o yaml Full object as YAML kubectl get pod anihpj -o yaml
-o json Full object as JSON kubectl get pod anihpj -o json
-o jsonpath Extract specific fields kubectl get pods -o jsonpath='{.items[*].metadata.name}'
-o custom-columns Custom table output kubectl get pods -o custom-columns=NAME:.metadata.name,STATUS:.status.phase
--sort-by Sort output by field kubectl get pods --sort-by=.metadata.creationTimestamp
-l key=value Filter by label kubectl get pods -l app=anihpj
--field-selector Filter by field value kubectl get pods --field-selector status.phase=Running

JSONPath Examples (CKA Favorite)

jsonpath-examples.sh 
# Get all pod names
kubectl get pods -o jsonpath='{.items[*].metadata.name}'

# Get pod names + their node assignments
kubectl get pods -o jsonpath='{range .items[*]}{.metadata.name}{"\t"}{.spec.nodeName}{"\n"}{end}'

# Get node internal IPs
kubectl get nodes -o jsonpath='{.items[*].status.addresses[?(@.type=="InternalIP")].address}'

# Get container images across all pods
kubectl get pods -o jsonpath='{.items[*].spec.containers[*].image}' -A

# Custom columns — clean table output
kubectl get pods -o custom-columns=\
NAME:.metadata.name,\
STATUS:.status.phase,\
NODE:.spec.nodeName,\
IP:.status.podIP
💡 JSONPath Cheat Sheet for CKA

{.items[*]} = all items, {.metadata.name} = specific field, {range .items[*]}...{end} = loop, {"\n"} = newline, {"\t"} = tab, [?(@.type=="InternalIP")] = filter. Practice these — JSONPath extraction is tested on every CKA exam.

🎯 Common CKA Output Extraction Patterns

These exact patterns appear on CKA exams. Save as muscle memory:

cka-extraction-patterns.sh 
# ── Pattern 1: "List pod names sorted by creation time" ──
kubectl get pods --sort-by=.metadata.creationTimestamp \
  -o jsonpath='{range .items[*]}{.metadata.name}{"\n"}{end}'

# ── Pattern 2: "Find which node each pod is running on" ──
kubectl get pods -o custom-columns=\
POD:.metadata.name,\
NODE:.spec.nodeName

# ── Pattern 3: "Get the IP address of all nodes" ──
kubectl get nodes -o jsonpath=\
'{.items[*].status.addresses[?(@.type=="InternalIP")].address}'

# ── Pattern 4: "List all container images in the cluster" ──
kubectl get pods -A -o jsonpath=\
'{range .items[*]}{.spec.containers[*].image}{"\n"}{end}' | sort -u

# ── Pattern 5: "Find pods not in Running state" ──
kubectl get pods -A --field-selector=status.phase!=Running

# ── Pattern 6: "Count pods per namespace" ──
kubectl get pods -A --no-headers | awk '{print $1}' | sort | uniq -c | sort -rn

# ── Pattern 7: "Save output to file" (CKA often requires this) ──
kubectl get pods -o jsonpath='{.items[*].metadata.name}' > /opt/answers/pod-names.txt
📤
Output to File

Many CKA questions say "save result to /opt/answers/file.txt". Always use > redirect. Double-check with cat after writing.

🔗
Pipe Power

Combine kubectl with grep, awk, sort, wc -l. Example: kubectl get pods -A | grep -c Running counts running pods.

Chapter 7: YAML and Kubernetes Manifests

Every Kubernetes object is defined in YAML. You can't pass the CKA without being able to read, write, and debug YAML manifests quickly. This chapter covers YAML syntax, the 4 required fields every manifest must have, finding the right apiVersion, multi-resource files, and validation.

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~1 hour 📝 YAML 🎯 CKA Essential

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ YAML syntax — key-value pairs, lists, nested objects, and the rules that trip people up
  • ✅ The 4 required fields every Kubernetes manifest must have
  • ✅ How to find the right apiVersion for any resource
  • ✅ Combining multiple resources in one YAML file with --- separator
  • ✅ Validating manifests with --dry-run and kubectl diff before applying
🎓 CKA Relevance
All Domains 100%
YAML Mistakes = Failed Tasks

The #1 reason candidates fail CKA tasks isn't lack of knowledge — it's YAML indentation errors. A single wrong indent breaks the entire manifest. Practice writing YAML by hand until it's automatic. On the exam, always use --dry-run=server before applying to catch errors.

  • Spaces only — tabs will break your YAML silently
  • 2-space indentation — Kubernetes convention
  • Colon + space — key: value not key:value

7.1 YAML Basics for Kubernetes

YAML ("YAML Ain't Markup Language") is a human-readable data format. Kubernetes uses it to describe desired state. Here are the three building blocks you need:

YAML Building Blocks
  1️⃣ KEY-VALUE PAIR (scalar):
  ┌─────────────────────────────┐
  │ name: anihpj                │  string
  │ replicas: 3                 │  number
  │ debug: true                 │  boolean
  │ description: "Job platform" │  quoted string
  └─────────────────────────────┘

  2️⃣ LIST (sequence) — each item starts with "- ":
  ┌─────────────────────────────┐
  │ ports:                      │
  │ - 8000          ◀── item 1  │
  │ - 8080          ◀── item 2  │
  │ - 443           ◀── item 3  │
  └─────────────────────────────┘

  3️⃣ NESTED OBJECT (mapping) — indent = nesting:
  ┌─────────────────────────────┐
  │ metadata:       ◀── parent  │  2 spaces per level
  │   name: anihpj  ◀── child   │
  │   labels:       ◀── child   │
  │     app: anihpj ◀── grandchild
  └─────────────────────────────┘

  ⚠️ CRITICAL RULES:
  ✅ Spaces only      (2 per indent level)
  ❌ NO tabs          (YAML breaks silently!)
  ✅ Colon + space    key: value
  ❌ NOT              key:value  (missing space!)
  ✅ Dash + space     - item
  ❌ NOT              -item  (missing space!)
  ✅ Use ---          to separate multiple documents
yaml-syntax-examples.yaml 
# Key-value pairs
name: anihpj
replicas: 3
enabled: true

# List of strings
skills:
  - Python
  - Django
  - Kubernetes

# List of objects
containers:
  - name: django
    image: anihpj:v1
    ports:
      - containerPort: 8000
  - name: nginx
    image: nginx:alpine
    ports:
      - containerPort: 80

# Inline list (shorthand)
args: ["runserver", "0.0.0.0:8000"]

# Multi-line string (pipe = preserve newlines)
description: |
  anihpj is a Django job posting platform.
  It supports filtering by Skills, Location, and Author.

# Folded string (> = join lines)
summary: >
  This is a single paragraph
  even though it spans multiple lines.
🚨 Common YAML Gotcha: Indentation

These look similar but mean completely different things:

Mistake What You Wrote What K8s Sees Fix
Tab character [TAB]name: anihpj Parse error — YAML fails entirely Use spaces only
No space after colon name:anihpj Treats name:anihpj as a single string name: anihpj
Wrong indent level labels: at root level Top-level field instead of nested under metadata Indent 2 spaces under parent
String vs number replicas: "3" Error: string instead of integer replicas: 3 (no quotes)
Missing dash for list containers:\n name: django Error: expected list, got mapping containers:\n- name: django
Indentation Matters — Same Words, Different Meaning
  ✅ CORRECT — ports is a field of the container:
  containers:
  - name: django
    ports:                    ← indented under the list item
    - containerPort: 8000

  ❌ WRONG — ports is a sibling, not a child:
  containers:
  - name: django
  ports:                      ← NOT indented = separate field!
  - containerPort: 8000

  ✅ CORRECT — labels nested under metadata:
  metadata:
    name: anihpj
    labels:
      app: anihpj

  ❌ WRONG — labels at same level as metadata:
  metadata:
    name: anihpj
  labels:                     ← NOT nested = top-level field!
    app: anihpj

🔄 YAML vs JSON — Why Kubernetes Uses YAML

Kubernetes actually stores everything as JSON internally. YAML is a superset of JSON — easier to write by hand, with comments support:

📝
YAML (What You Write)
# Pod definition
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj
spec:
  containers:
  - name: django
    image: anihpj:v1

✅ Comments • ✅ Readable • ✅ Less typing

{ }
JSON (What K8s Stores)
{
  "apiVersion": "v1",
  "kind": "Pod",
  "metadata": {"name": "anihpj"},
  "spec": {
    "containers": [{
      "name": "django",
      "image": "anihpj:v1"
    }]}}

❌ No comments • ❌ Verbose • ✅ Precise

📖 Both are Valid for kubectl

kubectl apply -f pod.yaml and kubectl apply -f pod.json both work. However, always use YAML for writing manifests (readable, supports comments). Use JSON with -o json for extracting data programmatically.

7.2 The 4 Required Fields — Every Manifest

Every single Kubernetes object — Pod, Deployment, Service, ConfigMap, Secret — requires exactly 4 top-level fields. Memorize these:

🔌
apiVersion

Which API group? v1, apps/v1, networking.k8s.io/v1

📦
kind

What type of object? Pod, Deployment, Service

🏷️
metadata

Identity: name, namespace, labels, annotations

⚙️
spec

Desired state — THE MEAT. Different for every resource type.

anihpj-pod.yaml 
apiVersion: v1                    # ← 1. API version
kind: Pod                         # ← 2. Object type
metadata:                         # ← 3. Identity
  name: anihpj-django
  namespace: anihpj-dev
  labels:
    app: anihpj
    component: web
    environment: dev
spec:                             # ← 4. Desired state
  containers:
  - name: django
    image: anihpj:v1
    ports:
    - containerPort: 8000
    env:
    - name: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
      value: "anihpj.settings"
    resources:
      requests:
        cpu: "100m"
        memory: "128Mi"
      limits:
        cpu: "500m"
        memory: "256Mi"
Manifest Structure — Annotated
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  apiVersion: apps/v1     ← Which API group?          │
  │  kind: Deployment        ← What type of object?      │
  │  metadata:               ← Identity (name, labels)   │
  │    name: anihpj                                      │
  │    namespace: anihpj-dev                             │
  │    labels:                                           │
  │      app: anihpj                                     │
  │  spec:                   ← Desired state (THE MEAT)  │
  │    replicas: 3              ← How many pods?          │
  │    selector:                ← Which pods to manage?   │
  │      matchLabels:                                     │
  │        app: anihpj                                    │
  │    template:                ← Pod template            │
  │      metadata:                                        │
  │        labels:                                        │
  │          app: anihpj        ← MUST match selector!    │
  │      spec:                  ← Pod spec (containers)   │
  │        containers:                                    │
  │        - name: django                                 │
  │          image: anihpj:v1                             │
  │          ports:                                       │
  │          - containerPort: 8000                        │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

7.3 Finding the Right apiVersion

Wrong apiVersion = manifest rejected by the API server. Here's the complete reference and how to look it up:

Resource apiVersion Kind Short Name
Podv1Podpo
Servicev1Servicesvc
ConfigMapv1ConfigMapcm
Secretv1Secret—
Namespacev1Namespacens
PersistentVolumev1PersistentVolumepv
PersistentVolumeClaimv1PersistentVolumeClaimpvc
ServiceAccountv1ServiceAccountsa
Deploymentapps/v1Deploymentdeploy
ReplicaSetapps/v1ReplicaSetrs
DaemonSetapps/v1DaemonSetds
StatefulSetapps/v1StatefulSetsts
Ingressnetworking.k8s.io/v1Ingressing
NetworkPolicynetworking.k8s.io/v1NetworkPolicynetpol
ClusterRolerbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1ClusterRole—
ClusterRoleBindingrbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1ClusterRoleBinding—
find-apiversion.sh 
# List ALL resources with their apiVersion and short names
kubectl api-resources
# NAME               SHORTNAMES   APIVERSION                     NAMESPACED   KIND
# pods               po           v1                             true         Pod
# services           svc          v1                             true         Service
# deployments        deploy       apps/v1                        true         Deployment
# ingresses          ing          networking.k8s.io/v1           true         Ingress

# List all available API versions
kubectl api-versions
# apps/v1
# networking.k8s.io/v1
# rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
# v1

# Quick check: what apiVersion does Ingress use?
kubectl api-resources | grep -i ingress
# ingresses   ing   networking.k8s.io/v1   true   Ingress
💡 CKA Memory Trick

Core resources = v1 (Pod, Service, ConfigMap, Secret, Namespace, PV, PVC, SA). Workloads = apps/v1 (Deployment, ReplicaSet, DaemonSet, StatefulSet). Networking = networking.k8s.io/v1 (Ingress, NetworkPolicy). RBAC = rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1 (Role, ClusterRole, RoleBinding, ClusterRoleBinding).

API Group Hierarchy — Visual Map
  Kubernetes API Groups
  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                            │
  │  Core API (v1)  ← No group prefix                         │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ Pod • Service • ConfigMap • Secret • Namespace       │  │
  │  │ PersistentVolume • PersistentVolumeClaim • SA • Node │  │
  │  │ Endpoints • Event • ReplicationController            │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │                                                            │
  │  apps/v1  ← Workload controllers                           │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ Deployment • ReplicaSet • DaemonSet • StatefulSet    │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │                                                            │
  │  networking.k8s.io/v1  ← Network resources                 │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ Ingress • NetworkPolicy • IngressClass               │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │                                                            │
  │  rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1  ← Access control            │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ Role • RoleBinding • ClusterRole • ClusterRoleBinding│  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │                                                            │
  │  batch/v1  ← Job workloads                                 │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ Job • CronJob                                        │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │                                                            │
  │  storage.k8s.io/v1  ← Storage                              │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ StorageClass • CSIDriver • CSINode                   │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │                                                            │
  │  policy/v1  ← Pod disruption                               │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ PodDisruptionBudget                                  │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

7.4 Multi-Resource YAML Files

You can define multiple Kubernetes objects in a single YAML file by separating them with ---. This is common for deploying an app with its service together:

anihpj-complete.yaml 
# Namespace
apiVersion: v1
kind: Namespace
metadata:
  name: anihpj-dev
---
# ConfigMap
apiVersion: v1
kind: ConfigMap
metadata:
  name: anihpj-config
  namespace: anihpj-dev
data:
  DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE: "anihpj.settings"
  ALLOWED_HOSTS: "*"
---
# Deployment
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
  namespace: anihpj-dev
spec:
  replicas: 2
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
        ports:
        - containerPort: 8000
        envFrom:
        - configMapRef:
            name: anihpj-config
---
# Service
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: anihpj-svc
  namespace: anihpj-dev
spec:
  selector:
    app: anihpj
  ports:
  - port: 80
    targetPort: 8000
apply-strategies.sh 
# Apply a single multi-resource file
kubectl apply -f anihpj-complete.yaml

# Apply all YAML files in a directory
kubectl apply -f k8s/

# Apply with Kustomize
kubectl apply -k overlays/prod/

# Delete everything from the file
kubectl delete -f anihpj-complete.yaml
File Organization — Two Approaches
  Approach A: Single File              Approach B: Directory
  ┌─────────────────────┐             ┌─────────────────────┐
  │ anihpj-complete.yaml │             │ k8s/                 │
  │ ├── Namespace        │             │ ├── namespace.yaml   │
  │ ├── ---              │             │ ├── configmap.yaml   │
  │ ├── ConfigMap        │             │ ├── secret.yaml      │
  │ ├── ---              │             │ ├── deployment.yaml  │
  │ ├── Deployment       │             │ └── service.yaml     │
  │ ├── ---              │             │                      │
  │ └── Service          │             │ $ kubectl apply -f k8s/
  │                      │             │ (applies all files)  │
  │ $ kubectl apply -f   │             └─────────────────────┘
  │ anihpj-complete.yaml │
  └─────────────────────┘
  ✅ Simple, one command              ✅ Better organization
  ❌ Hard to manage large apps        ✅ Each file = one concern
⚠️ Resource Ordering Matters!

When using kubectl apply -f dir/, Kubernetes processes files in alphabetical order. If your Deployment references a ConfigMap that hasn't been created yet, it'll fail. Use numbered filenames to control order: 01-namespace.yaml, 02-configmap.yaml, 03-secret.yaml, 04-deployment.yaml, 05-service.yaml. Or use a single multi-resource file with --- separators — resources are created top-to-bottom.

7.5 YAML Validation and Dry Run

Never apply a manifest blindly. Always validate first — especially on the CKA exam where a bad apply can cost you time and points.

validation-workflow.sh 
# Step 1: Client-side validation (no API call)
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml --dry-run=client
# deployment.apps/anihpj created (dry run)   ← YAML is valid

# Step 2: Server-side validation (checks against API)
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml --dry-run=server
# deployment.apps/anihpj created (dry run)   ← API accepted it

# Step 3: Preview changes before applying
kubectl diff -f deployment.yaml
# Shows what will change (like git diff)

# Step 4: Apply for real
kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml
# deployment.apps/anihpj created
1
--dry-run=client

Validates YAML syntax locally. No cluster needed.

2
--dry-run=server

Validates against API server. Catches invalid refs.

3
kubectl diff

Preview what will change. Like git diff for K8s.

4
kubectl apply

Apply for real. K8s reconciles to desired state.

⚠️ CKA Exam Workflow

On the exam, always use this order: generate YAML with --dry-run=client -o yaml > file.yaml → edit the YAML to add required fields → validate with --dry-run=server → apply. This prevents wasted time debugging apply errors.

🚨 Decoding Common Error Messages

When validation fails, K8s gives you error messages. Learn to read them fast:

common-errors.sh 
# ERROR: Wrong apiVersion
error: resource mapping not found for name: "anihpj"
  kind: Deployment  apiVersion: v1
# FIX: Change apiVersion to apps/v1

# ERROR: Missing required field
error: error validating: ValidationError(Deployment.spec):
  missing required field "selector"
# FIX: Add spec.selector.matchLabels

# ERROR: Label mismatch
The Deployment "anihpj" is invalid:
  spec.selector does not match template.metadata.labels
# FIX: Make selector.matchLabels == template.metadata.labels

# ERROR: Invalid YAML syntax
error: error parsing deployment.yaml: yaml: line 12:
  did not find expected key
# FIX: Check indentation at line 12 (probably tabs or wrong indent)

# ERROR: Namespace doesn't exist
Error: namespaces "anihpj-dev" not found
# FIX: Create namespace first: kubectl create ns anihpj-dev

🛠️ Vim Tips for YAML Editing (CKA Exam Editor)

The CKA exam provides vim as the default editor. These settings prevent YAML indent disasters:

~/.vimrc 
# Add to ~/.vimrc FIRST THING on the exam:
set tabstop=2       " Tab = 2 spaces
set shiftwidth=2    " Indent = 2 spaces
set expandtab       " Convert tabs to spaces
set autoindent      " Keep indent level on new line
set smartindent     " Smart indent for code
set number          " Show line numbers (helps find errors)
set paste           " Prevents auto-indent issues when pasting

# Quick setup (one command):
echo 'set ts=2 sw=2 et ai si nu paste' >> ~/.vimrc
Vim Command What It Does When to Use
:set paste Disable auto-indent for pasting YAML Before pasting from docs
:set nopaste Re-enable auto-indent After pasting, for manual editing
gg=G Re-indent entire file When indentation is messed up
Shift+V then > Visual select + indent right Indent a YAML block deeper
Shift+V then < Visual select + indent left Un-indent a YAML block
/searchterm Search for text Find a specific field in large YAML
dd / yy / p Delete/copy/paste line Move or duplicate YAML lines
u Undo last change Revert a mistake instantly

Chapter 8: Deploying anihpj — Your First Kubernetes Application

Time to put everything together. In this chapter you'll deploy the anihpj/jobpost Django application step by step — creating a Namespace, ConfigMap, Secret, Deployment, and Service. By the end, anihpj will be running on Kubernetes and you'll understand every piece of the deployment flow.

🟢 Beginner ⏱️ ~2 hours 🚀 Deploy 🐍 Django

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Plan a Kubernetes deployment — what resources you need and in what order
  • ✅ Create a Namespace to isolate your application
  • ✅ Use ConfigMap for non-sensitive configuration and Secret for credentials
  • ✅ Write a complete Deployment manifest with environment variables and resource limits
  • ✅ Expose your app with a Service and access it via port-forward
  • ✅ Verify and troubleshoot your deployment end-to-end
  • ✅ Understand the full flow: kubectl apply → API server → etcd → scheduler → kubelet → container
🎓 CKA Relevance
Workloads & Scheduling 15% Services & Networking 20%
This Is the Pattern for Every CKA Deployment Task

The CKA will ask you to create Deployments, Services, ConfigMaps, and Secrets multiple times. The step-by-step workflow in this chapter is exactly the workflow you'll follow for every deployment question on the exam. Practice this until it's muscle memory.

  • Namespace → ConfigMap → Secret → Deployment → Service — this order avoids reference errors
  • Use imperative + dry-run to generate skeletons, then edit for specific requirements
  • Always verify with kubectl get all -n <namespace> after deploying

8.1 The anihpj Application Overview

Let's review what we're deploying — the anihpj Django project with its jobpost app. Understanding the application architecture helps you make the right Kubernetes decisions.

🐍
Django Project: anihpj

Job posting platform. Models: Skills, User, Author, Location, JobPost. Uses SQLite for dev, PostgreSQL for prod.

📦
Docker Image: anihpj:v1

Containerized Django app. Runs on port 8000. Needs environment vars for settings and secret key.

Target Architecture — anihpj in Kubernetes
  NAMESPACE: anihpj-dev
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐

  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐            │

8.2 Step 1: Create the Namespace

Always isolate your application in its own namespace. This keeps resources organized and makes cleanup easy.

01-namespace.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Namespace
metadata:
  name: anihpj-dev
  labels:
    project: anihpj
    environment: dev
terminal 
# Imperative (faster for CKA)
kubectl create namespace anihpj-dev

# Or declarative
kubectl apply -f 01-namespace.yaml

# Set as default namespace
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=anihpj-dev

# Verify
kubectl get namespaces
# NAME              STATUS   AGE
# anihpj-dev        Active   5s
# default           Active   1h
# kube-system       Active   1h

8.3 Step 2: Create ConfigMap

ConfigMaps hold non-sensitive configuration data. Our Django app needs settings module path, allowed hosts, and debug mode.

02-configmap.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: ConfigMap
metadata:
  name: anihpj-config
  namespace: anihpj-dev
  labels:
    app: anihpj
data:
  DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE: "anihpj.settings"
  ALLOWED_HOSTS: "*"
  DEBUG: "True"
  DATABASE_ENGINE: "django.db.backends.sqlite3"
  DATABASE_NAME: "/app/db.sqlite3"
terminal 
# Apply
kubectl apply -f 02-configmap.yaml

# Verify
kubectl get configmap anihpj-config -n anihpj-dev -o yaml
# data:
#   ALLOWED_HOSTS: "*"
#   DATABASE_ENGINE: django.db.backends.sqlite3
#   DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE: anihpj.settings

# Imperative alternative (CKA speed)
kubectl create configmap anihpj-config \
  --from-literal=DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE=anihpj.settings \
  --from-literal=ALLOWED_HOSTS="*" \
  --from-literal=DEBUG=True \
  -n anihpj-dev
ConfigMap Secret
Purpose Non-sensitive config (URLs, feature flags) Sensitive data (passwords, tokens, keys)
Data format Plain text Base64-encoded
Size limit 1 MiB 1 MiB
Mount as Env vars or volume files Env vars or volume files (tmpfs)
RBAC Less restricted by default Should be RBAC-restricted
Create command kubectl create configmap kubectl create secret generic

8.4 Step 3: Create Secret

Secrets hold sensitive data — passwords, API keys, certificates. Values are base64-encoded (NOT encrypted by default).

03-secret.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Secret
metadata:
  name: anihpj-secret
  namespace: anihpj-dev
  labels:
    app: anihpj
type: Opaque
data:
  # echo -n 'django-insecure-cka-practice-key-12345' | base64
  SECRET_KEY: ZGphbmdvLWluc2VjdXJlLWNrYS1wcmFjdGljZS1rZXktMTIzNDU=
  # echo -n 'sqlite:///app/db.sqlite3' | base64
  DATABASE_URL: c3FsaXRlOi8vL2FwcC9kYi5zcWxpdGUz
🚨 Security Warning: Secrets Are Base64, NOT Encrypted

By default, Kubernetes Secrets are only base64-encoded — anyone with kubectl get secret -o yaml access can decode them. In production, enable encryption at rest (EncryptionConfiguration) and use RBAC to restrict access. Never commit Secret YAML to Git.

terminal 
# Apply
kubectl apply -f 03-secret.yaml

# Imperative alternative (CKA speed — auto-encodes to base64)
kubectl create secret generic anihpj-secret \
  --from-literal=SECRET_KEY='django-insecure-cka-practice-key-12345' \
  --from-literal=DATABASE_URL='sqlite:///app/db.sqlite3' \
  -n anihpj-dev

# Verify (values are base64)
kubectl get secret anihpj-secret -n anihpj-dev -o yaml

# Decode a value
kubectl get secret anihpj-secret -n anihpj-dev \
  -o jsonpath='{.data.SECRET_KEY}' | base64 -d
# django-insecure-cka-practice-key-12345

📚 Kubernetes Secret Types Reference

Kubernetes has several built-in Secret types. Know which to use for the CKA:

Type Value for type: Purpose CKA?
Opaque Opaque Generic key-value (default) ✅ Most common
Docker Registry kubernetes.io/dockerconfigjson Image pull authentication ✅ Tested
TLS kubernetes.io/tls TLS certificate + private key ✅ Tested
Service Account kubernetes.io/service-account-token Auto-generated SA token ⚠️ Managed by K8s
Basic Auth kubernetes.io/basic-auth Username + password ❌ Rare
secret-types-examples.sh 
# Create docker-registry secret (for private images)
kubectl create secret docker-registry my-registry \
  --docker-server=registry.example.com \
  --docker-username=admin \
  --docker-password=pass123 \
  --docker-email=admin@example.com -n anihpj-dev

# Create TLS secret (for Ingress HTTPS)
kubectl create secret tls my-tls \
  --cert=path/to/tls.crt \
  --key=path/to/tls.key -n anihpj-dev

8.5 Step 4: Create the Deployment

The Deployment is the core of your application — it manages pods, rolling updates, and self-healing. This is the most complete manifest in our deployment.

04-deployment.yaml 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
  namespace: anihpj-dev
  labels:
    app: anihpj
    version: v1
spec:
  replicas: 2
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj                       # ← MUST match template labels
  strategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxSurge: 1
      maxUnavailable: 0
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj                     # ← MUST match selector
        version: v1
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
        ports:
        - containerPort: 8000
          name: http
        envFrom:
        - configMapRef:
            name: anihpj-config          # ← all ConfigMap keys as env vars
        - secretRef:
            name: anihpj-secret          # ← all Secret keys as env vars
        resources:
          requests:
            cpu: "100m"                  # ← minimum CPU (0.1 core)
            memory: "128Mi"              # ← minimum memory
          limits:
            cpu: "500m"                  # ← maximum CPU (0.5 core)
            memory: "256Mi"              # ← maximum memory
        readinessProbe:
          httpGet:
            path: /
            port: 8000
          initialDelaySeconds: 10
          periodSeconds: 5
        livenessProbe:
          httpGet:
            path: /
            port: 8000
          initialDelaySeconds: 30
          periodSeconds: 10

Deployment Manifest — Field by Field

🔢 replicas: 2
Run 2 identical pods. K8s maintains this count via the ReplicaSet.
🏷️ selector.matchLabels
Links the Deployment to its pods. MUST match template.metadata.labels.
🔄 strategy: RollingUpdate
Zero-downtime updates. maxSurge=1 adds 1 new pod before removing old.
📦 envFrom
Injects ALL keys from ConfigMap/Secret as environment variables at once.
📊 resources
requests = guaranteed minimum. limits = maximum allowed. Prevents resource starvation.
💚 readinessProbe
Is the pod ready to receive traffic? Fails = removed from Service endpoints.
❤️ livenessProbe
Is the pod still alive? Fails = pod gets restarted by kubelet.
terminal 
# Apply the deployment
kubectl apply -f 04-deployment.yaml

# Watch pods being created
kubectl get pods -n anihpj-dev -w
# NAME                      READY   STATUS    RESTARTS   AGE
# anihpj-6d7f8c9b5d-abc12   1/1     Running   0          15s
# anihpj-6d7f8c9b5d-def34   1/1     Running   0          15s
💡 envFrom vs env — Two Ways to Inject Variables

envFrom injects ALL keys from a ConfigMap/Secret as env vars (what we used above — simplest approach). env lets you pick individual keys and optionally rename them. Use env when you need selective injection or renaming:

env-vs-envfrom.yaml 
# envFrom: inject ALL keys from ConfigMap/Secret
envFrom:
- configMapRef:
    name: anihpj-config
- secretRef:
    name: anihpj-secret

# env: inject INDIVIDUAL keys (can rename)
env:
- name: DJANGO_SECRET       # ← name in container
  valueFrom:
    secretKeyRef:
      name: anihpj-secret
      key: SECRET_KEY        # ← name in Secret
- name: SETTINGS_MODULE      # ← renamed!
  valueFrom:
    configMapKeyRef:
      name: anihpj-config
      key: DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE
Rolling Update — Zero-Downtime Deployment
  Initial State (replicas: 2)
  ┌──────────┐  ┌──────────┐
  │ Pod v1 ✅ │  │ Pod v1 ✅ │   Both running, serving traffic
  └──────────┘  └──────────┘

  Step 1: maxSurge=1 → Create new pod with v2
  ┌──────────┐  ┌──────────┐  ┌──────────┐
  │ Pod v1 ✅ │  │ Pod v1 ✅ │  │ Pod v2 🔄 │  New pod starting
  └──────────┘  └──────────┘  └──────────┘

  Step 2: v2 passes readinessProbe → remove one v1
  ┌──────────┐  ╔══════════╗  ┌──────────┐
  │ Pod v1 ✅ │  ║ Pod v1 ❌ ║  │ Pod v2 ✅ │  v2 ready, old v1 terminating
  └──────────┘  ╚══════════╝  └──────────┘

  Step 3: Start another v2 → remove last v1
  ╔══════════╗  ┌──────────┐  ┌──────────┐
  ║ Pod v1 ❌ ║  │ Pod v2 ✅ │  │ Pod v2 🔄 │  Last v1 terminating
  ╚══════════╝  └──────────┘  └──────────┘

  Final State: All v2, zero downtime
  ┌──────────┐  ┌──────────┐
  │ Pod v2 ✅ │  │ Pod v2 ✅ │   Update complete!
  └──────────┘  └──────────┘

  Key: maxSurge=1 means at most 3 pods during update (2+1)
       maxUnavailable=0 means traffic always has 2 healthy pods
rolling-update-commands.sh 
# Update the image (triggers rolling update)
kubectl set image deployment/anihpj django=anihpj:v2 -n anihpj-dev

# Watch the rollout in real time
kubectl rollout status deployment/anihpj -n anihpj-dev
# Waiting for deployment "anihpj" rollout to finish: 1 of 2 updated...
# deployment "anihpj" successfully rolled out

# View rollout history
kubectl rollout history deployment/anihpj -n anihpj-dev
# REVISION  CHANGE-CAUSE
# 1         <none>
# 2         <none>

# Rollback to previous version (CKA important!)
kubectl rollout undo deployment/anihpj -n anihpj-dev

# Rollback to specific revision
kubectl rollout undo deployment/anihpj --to-revision=1 -n anihpj-dev

8.6 Step 5: Create the Service

The Service gives your pods a stable IP and DNS name. Without it, you'd need to track individual pod IPs that change on every restart.

05-service.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: anihpj-svc
  namespace: anihpj-dev
  labels:
    app: anihpj
spec:
  type: ClusterIP
  selector:
    app: anihpj                      # ← selects pods with this label
  ports:
  - name: http
    port: 80                         # ← service port (what clients use)
    targetPort: 8000                 # ← container port (where Django listens)
    protocol: TCP
Service → Pod Traffic Flow
  Client Request
       │
       ▼
  ┌──────────────────────────┐
  │  Service: anihpj-svc     │
  │  ClusterIP: 10.96.45.123 │
  │  Port: 80                │
  └──────────┬───────────────┘
             │ selector: app=anihpj
             │ (finds all matching pods)
       ┌─────┴──────┐
       ▼            ▼
  ┌─────────┐  ┌─────────┐
  │ Pod 1   │  │ Pod 2   │
  │ :8000   │  │ :8000   │
  │ app:    │  │ app:    │
  │ anihpj  │  │ anihpj  │
  └─────────┘  └─────────┘
  Round-robin load balancing
terminal 
# Apply
kubectl apply -f 05-service.yaml

# Verify service
kubectl get svc -n anihpj-dev
# NAME         TYPE        CLUSTER-IP     EXTERNAL-IP   PORT(S)   AGE
# anihpj-svc   ClusterIP   10.96.45.123   <none>        80/TCP    5s

# Check endpoints (should list pod IPs)
kubectl get endpoints anihpj-svc -n anihpj-dev
# NAME         ENDPOINTS                         AGE
# anihpj-svc   172.17.0.3:8000,172.17.0.4:8000   5s

📊 All Kubernetes Service Types — Comparison

We used ClusterIP above. Know all 4 types for the CKA:

Type Accessible From Use Case External Access
ClusterIP Inside cluster only Internal microservice communication ❌ No (use port-forward for dev)
NodePort Node IP + static port Dev/testing, direct node access ✅ Via <NodeIP>:<30000-32767>
LoadBalancer Cloud LB → cluster Production on cloud (AWS, GKE, AKS) ✅ Via cloud load balancer IP
ExternalName DNS CNAME redirect Alias to external services N/A (just a DNS alias)
Service Types — Visibility Layers
  External Traffic (Internet)
  ───────────────────────────────────────────────
       │
       ▼
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  LoadBalancer     ← Cloud provider LB     │
  │  (AWS ELB, GCP LB, Azure LB)             │
  └─────────────────────┬────────────────────┘
                        ▼
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  NodePort           ← All nodes listen    │
  │  (Port 30000-32767 on every node)         │
  └─────────────────────┬────────────────────┘
                        ▼
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  ClusterIP          ← Internal only       │
  │  (Virtual IP, accessible within cluster)  │
  │  Service: 10.96.45.123:80                 │
  └─────────────────────┬────────────────────┘
                        ▼
              ┌─────────┴──────────┐
              ▼                    ▼
         ┌─────────┐         ┌─────────┐
         │  Pod 1   │         │  Pod 2   │
         │  :8000   │         │  :8000   │
         └─────────┘         └─────────┘

  Each type INCLUDES the ones below it:
  LoadBalancer = NodePort + ClusterIP + external LB
  NodePort = ClusterIP + exposed port on all nodes

8.7 Step 6: Access Your Application

ClusterIP services are only accessible from inside the cluster. To access from your laptop, use port-forward:

terminal 
# Port-forward: laptop:8000 → service:80 → pod:8000
kubectl port-forward svc/anihpj-svc 8000:80 -n anihpj-dev
# Forwarding from 127.0.0.1:8000 -> 8000

# Now open your browser:
# http://localhost:8000          ← anihpj homepage
# http://localhost:8000/admin    ← Django admin

# Alternative: port-forward directly to a pod
kubectl port-forward pod/anihpj-6d7f8c9b5d-abc12 8000:8000 -n anihpj-dev

# Alternative: Minikube service (opens browser automatically)
minikube service anihpj-svc -n anihpj-dev
Port-Forward — How It Works
  Your Laptop                    Kubernetes Cluster
  ┌────────────────┐            ┌────────────────────────────┐
  │ Browser        │            │                            │
  │ localhost:8000 │───kubectl──│──▶ Service:80 ──▶ Pod:8000 │
  │                │  tunnel    │     anihpj-svc    django   │
  └────────────────┘            └────────────────────────────┘
  
  kubectl creates a secure tunnel from your machine to the cluster.
  Traffic: browser → kubectl → API server → pod

8.8 Step 7: Verify Everything

After deploying, run a comprehensive health check. This is the same verification workflow you'll use on the CKA exam:

verification-commands.sh 
# 1. See EVERYTHING in the namespace
kubectl get all -n anihpj-dev
# NAME                          READY   STATUS    RESTARTS   AGE
# pod/anihpj-6d7f8c9b5d-abc12   1/1     Running   0          2m
# pod/anihpj-6d7f8c9b5d-def34   1/1     Running   0          2m
#
# NAME                 TYPE        CLUSTER-IP     PORT(S)   AGE
# service/anihpj-svc   ClusterIP   10.96.45.123   80/TCP    1m
#
# NAME                     READY   UP-TO-DATE   AVAILABLE   AGE
# deployment.apps/anihpj   2/2     2            2           2m
#
# NAME                                DESIRED   CURRENT   READY   AGE
# replicaset.apps/anihpj-6d7f8c9b5d   2         2         2       2m

# 2. Detailed deployment info
kubectl describe deployment anihpj -n anihpj-dev

# 3. Pod logs (check for errors)
kubectl logs deployment/anihpj -n anihpj-dev
# Watching for Django startup messages...

# 4. Check environment variables are injected
kubectl exec deployment/anihpj -n anihpj-dev -- env | grep DJANGO
# DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE=anihpj.settings

# 5. Check ConfigMap and Secret are mounted
kubectl get configmap,secret -n anihpj-dev
# NAME                         DATA   AGE
# configmap/anihpj-config      5      3m
# NAME                         TYPE     DATA   AGE
# secret/anihpj-secret         Opaque   2      3m
✅
Healthy Deployment Checklist

All pods STATUS=Running, READY=1/1. Deployment AVAILABLE matches replicas. Service has ENDPOINTS listed. No error events.

❌
Common Problems

ImagePullBackOff = wrong image name. CrashLoopBackOff = app crashes on start. Pending = no node has enough resources. 0 endpoints = label mismatch.

Troubleshooting Decision Tree — Pod Not Running?
  kubectl get pods -n anihpj-dev
       │
       ├── STATUS: Pending
       │       └── kubectl describe pod <name>
       │           ├── "Insufficient cpu/memory" → Lower resource requests or add nodes
       │           ├── "no nodes match" → Check nodeSelector, taints, tolerations
       │           └── "Unschedulable" → kubectl uncordon <node>
       │
       ├── STATUS: ImagePullBackOff / ErrImagePull
       │       └── Wrong image name, tag doesn't exist, or registry auth required
       │           └── Fix: check image:tag spelling, create imagePullSecret
       │
       ├── STATUS: CrashLoopBackOff
       │       └── kubectl logs <pod> --previous
       │           ├── App error → Fix application code/config
       │           ├── Missing env var → Check ConfigMap/Secret refs
       │           └── Permission denied → Check securityContext
       │
       ├── STATUS: Running but READY: 0/1
       │       └── Readiness probe failing
       │           └── Check readinessProbe path/port, app might need more startup time
       │
       └── STATUS: Running, READY: 1/1 → ✅ Healthy!

🧹 Cleanup — Tear Down the Deployment

When you're done practicing, clean up all resources with one command:

cleanup.sh 
# Option 1: Delete the namespace (removes EVERYTHING inside)
kubectl delete namespace anihpj-dev
# namespace "anihpj-dev" deleted
# → All pods, services, configmaps, secrets in anihpj-dev are gone

# Option 2: Delete individual resources
kubectl delete -f 05-service.yaml
kubectl delete -f 04-deployment.yaml
kubectl delete -f 03-secret.yaml
kubectl delete -f 02-configmap.yaml
kubectl delete -f 01-namespace.yaml

# Option 3: Delete all from a directory
kubectl delete -f k8s/

# Reset your context namespace
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=default

🔗 Resource Dependency Chain — What Depends on What

Understanding the dependency order prevents "resource not found" errors:

Deployment Resource Dependencies
  Create Order (top → bottom)         Delete Order (bottom → top)
  ═══════════════════════════         ═════════════════════════════

  1. Namespace                         5. Namespace (deletes ALL inside!)
     └── creates boundary                 ↑
                                          │
  2. ConfigMap + Secret                4. ConfigMap + Secret
     └── config data ready                ↑ (deployment needs these)
                                          │
  3. Deployment                        3. Deployment
     └── refs ConfigMap/Secret            ↑ (service targets its pods)
     └── creates ReplicaSet               │
         └── creates Pods              2. Service
                                          ↑
  4. Service                           1. Ingress (if exists)
     └── selector finds pods
                                     ⚡ Shortcut: 
  5. Ingress (optional)              kubectl delete namespace anihpj-dev
     └── routes to service           → removes EVERYTHING at once!
⚠️
Common Dependency Mistakes
  • • Creating Deployment before ConfigMap → CreateContainerError
  • • Wrong Secret name in Deployment → Pod stuck in Pending
  • • Service selector doesn't match pod labels → 0 endpoints
  • • Deployment in wrong namespace → resource not found
✅
Verification at Each Step
  • • After Namespace: kubectl get ns
  • • After ConfigMap: kubectl get cm -n <ns>
  • • After Deployment: kubectl get pods -n <ns>
  • • After Service: kubectl get ep -n <ns>

8.9 What Just Happened? — Full Walkthrough

When you ran kubectl apply, a complex chain of events happened inside the cluster. Understanding this flow is essential for CKA troubleshooting:

End-to-End Deployment Flow — What K8s Did With Your YAML
  YOU: $ kubectl apply -f 04-deployment.yaml
       $ kubectl apply -f 05-service.yaml
            │
  ┌─────────▼──────────────┐
  │  1. API SERVER           │  Receives YAML, validates schema,
  │     validates & stores   │  authenticates you, authorizes action,
  │     in etcd              │  stores object in etcd
  └─────────┬──────────────┘
            ▼
  ┌────────────────────────┐
  │  2. CONTROLLER MANAGER   │  deployment-controller sees new Deployment →
  │     creates child objects │  creates ReplicaSet → RS creates Pod objects
  └─────────┬──────────────┘
            ▼
  ┌────────────────────────┐
  │  3. SCHEDULER            │  Sees unassigned Pods → evaluates nodes
  │     assigns pods to      │  (resources, taints, affinity) →
  │     worker nodes         │  assigns best worker node
  └─────────┬──────────────┘
            ▼
  ┌────────────────────────┐
  │  4. KUBELET              │  On assigned node: sees pod spec →
  │     (on worker node)     │  pulls anihpj:v1 image →
  │     starts containers    │  starts container via containerd
  └─────────┬──────────────┘
            ▼
  ┌────────────────────────┐
  │  5. KUBE-PROXY           │  Updates iptables/IPVS rules →
  │     (all nodes)          │  Service ClusterIP 10.96.45.123
  │     creates routing      │  now routes to pod IPs
  └─────────┬──────────────┘
            ▼
  ┌────────────────────────┐
  │  6. YOU: port-forward    │  kubectl creates tunnel →
  │     localhost:8000       │  browser → API server → pod
  │     anihpj is LIVE! 🎉  │  Django responds with job listings
  └────────────────────────┘
1
API Server

Validates, authenticates, stores in etcd

2
Controller Manager

Creates ReplicaSet → creates Pod objects

3
Scheduler

Assigns pods to worker nodes

4
Kubelet

Pulls image, starts container

5
Kube-Proxy

Updates networking rules for Service

6
Port-Forward

You access anihpj at localhost:8000 🎉

🎯 Part 2 Complete — Key Takeaways

You've set up an environment, mastered kubectl, learned YAML, and deployed a real application. Here are the commands you must remember:

must-remember-commands.sh 
# Generate YAML (CKA speed trick)
kubectl run nginx --image=nginx --dry-run=client -o yaml > pod.yaml
kubectl create deploy web --image=nginx --replicas=3 --dry-run=client -o yaml
kubectl expose deploy web --port=80 --type=ClusterIP --dry-run=client -o yaml

# Context management (every CKA question)
kubectl config use-context k8s-cluster-1
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=anihpj-dev

# API documentation (your exam cheat sheet)
kubectl explain pod.spec.containers
kubectl explain deployment.spec.strategy --recursive

# Deployment verification
kubectl get all -n anihpj-dev
kubectl describe deployment anihpj -n anihpj-dev
kubectl logs deployment/anihpj -n anihpj-dev
kubectl exec -it deployment/anihpj -n anihpj-dev -- /bin/sh
