🟡 Part 3: Cluster Installation & Configuration Chapters 9-12

Chapter 9: Installing Kubernetes with kubeadm

This is the chapter that separates "I know Kubernetes concepts" from "I can build a Kubernetes cluster from scratch." kubeadm is the official tool for creating production-grade clusters, and the CKA exam heavily tests this skill. You'll walk through every step — from preparing nodes to joining workers — using our anihpj/jobpost project as the target workload.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~2.5 hours 🔧 kubeadm 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Understand what kubeadm does (and what it doesn't do)
  • ✅ Prepare all nodes — disable swap, load kernel modules, install containerd
  • ✅ Initialize the control plane with kubeadm init
  • ✅ Install a CNI plugin to enable pod-to-pod networking
  • ✅ Join worker nodes to the cluster with kubeadm join
  • ✅ Verify the cluster is fully operational
  • ✅ Practice the complete cluster build workflow under CKA time pressure
🎓 CKA Relevance
Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration 25%
kubeadm = 25% of Your CKA Score

The "Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration" domain is worth 25% of the CKA exam. You will almost certainly get a question asking you to bootstrap a cluster or join a node. The exact steps in this chapter are what the exam tests.

  • Know the prerequisites — swap off, containerd, kernel modules — the exam may ask you to fix a broken node
  • Know kubeadm init flags — especially --pod-network-cidr and --apiserver-advertise-address
  • Know how to recover the join command — kubeadm token create --print-join-command

9.1 What is kubeadm?

kubeadm is the official Kubernetes tool for bootstrapping clusters. It handles the complex orchestration of generating certificates, starting control plane components, and configuring networking — but it does NOT provision the machines themselves.

kubeadm: What It Does vs What It Doesn't
  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                │
  │  YOU provide:                    kubeadm handles:              │
  │                                                                │
  │  ┌──────────────────────┐       ┌───────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │ 🖥️ VMs / Bare Metal   │       │ ✅ Bootstrap control plane │ │
  │  │ OS installed (Ubuntu) │       │ ✅ Generate TLS certs      │ │
  │  │ Network connectivity  │       │ ✅ Create etcd cluster     │ │
  │  │ Swap disabled         │       │ ✅ Configure kubelet       │ │
  │  │ Container runtime     │       │ ✅ Join worker nodes       │ │
  │  │ (containerd)          │       │ ✅ Set up kube-proxy       │ │
  │  └──────────────────────┘       └───────────────────────────┘ │
  │                                                                │
  │  ❌ NOT kubeadm's job:                                        │
  │     • VM provisioning (use Vagrant, Terraform, cloud console) │
  │     • OS installation / updates                                │
  │     • CNI plugin install (you choose: Calico, Flannel, etc.)  │
  │     • Load balancer setup (for HA control plane)              │
  │     • Application deployment (that's kubectl's job)           │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
🔧
kubeadm init

Run on the control plane node. Generates certificates, starts API server, etcd, scheduler, controller manager.

🔗
kubeadm join

Run on worker nodes. Connects the node to an existing cluster using a token + CA cert hash.

🖥️Provision VMs
→
⚙️Prerequisites
→
🔧kubeadm init
→
🌐Install CNI
→
🔗kubeadm join
→
✅Cluster Ready

9.2 Pre-requisites for All Nodes

Before running kubeadm, every node (control plane AND workers) must meet these requirements. On the CKA exam, you may be asked to fix a node that's missing one of these steps.

🖥️
Hardware

2+ CPUs, 2 GB+ RAM, unique hostname/MAC per node, full network connectivity

🚫
Swap Off

Kubernetes requires swap disabled. kubelet won't start with swap on.

🐳
Container Runtime

containerd (recommended), CRI-O, or Docker + cri-dockerd shim.

📦
K8s Packages

kubeadm, kubelet, kubectl — all pinned to the same version.

Step A: Disable Swap

all-nodes.sh 
# Disable swap immediately
sudo swapoff -a

# Disable swap permanently (survives reboot)
sudo sed -i '/swap/d' /etc/fstab

# Verify swap is off
free -h | grep Swap
# Swap:            0B          0B          0B

Step B: Load Kernel Modules

/etc/modules-load.d/k8s.conf 
# Create module config
cat <<EOF | sudo tee /etc/modules-load.d/k8s.conf
overlay
br_netfilter
EOF

# Load modules now
sudo modprobe overlay
sudo modprobe br_netfilter

# Verify
lsmod | grep -E "overlay|br_netfilter"
# br_netfilter           32768  0
# overlay               151552  0

Step C: Configure Sysctl Parameters

/etc/sysctl.d/k8s.conf 
# Enable bridge networking and IP forwarding
cat <<EOF | sudo tee /etc/sysctl.d/k8s.conf
net.bridge.bridge-nf-call-iptables  = 1
net.bridge.bridge-nf-call-ip6tables = 1
net.ipv4.ip_forward                 = 1
EOF

# Apply without reboot
sudo sysctl --system

# Verify
sysctl net.bridge.bridge-nf-call-iptables
# net.bridge.bridge-nf-call-iptables = 1

Step D: Install containerd

install-containerd.sh 
# Install containerd (Ubuntu/Debian)
sudo apt-get update
sudo apt-get install -y containerd

# Generate default config
sudo mkdir -p /etc/containerd
containerd config default | sudo tee /etc/containerd/config.toml

# CRITICAL: Enable SystemdCgroup (required for K8s)
sudo sed -i 's/SystemdCgroup = false/SystemdCgroup = true/' /etc/containerd/config.toml

# Restart and enable
sudo systemctl restart containerd
sudo systemctl enable containerd

# Verify
sudo systemctl status containerd
# ● containerd.service - containerd container runtime
#      Active: active (running)
🚨 Critical: SystemdCgroup Must Be true

If SystemdCgroup is false in containerd's config, kubelet will fail to start pods correctly. This is one of the most common "broken node" scenarios on the CKA exam. Always verify this setting after installing containerd.

Step E: Install kubeadm, kubelet, kubectl

install-k8s-packages.sh 
# Add Kubernetes apt repo (Ubuntu/Debian)
sudo apt-get install -y apt-transport-https ca-certificates curl
curl -fsSL https://pkgs.k8s.io/core:/stable:/v1.30/deb/Release.key \
  | sudo gpg --dearmor -o /etc/apt/keyrings/kubernetes-apt-keyring.gpg
echo 'deb [signed-by=/etc/apt/keyrings/kubernetes-apt-keyring.gpg] \
  https://pkgs.k8s.io/core:/stable:/v1.30/deb/ /' \
  | sudo tee /etc/apt/sources.list.d/kubernetes.list

# Install packages
sudo apt-get update
sudo apt-get install -y kubelet kubeadm kubectl

# CRITICAL: Hold versions (prevent auto-upgrade breaking cluster)
sudo apt-mark hold kubelet kubeadm kubectl

# Verify
kubeadm version
# kubeadm version: &version.Info{Major:"1", Minor:"30", ...}
⚠️ CKA Exam Note: Version Pinning

apt-mark hold prevents package upgrades from accidentally breaking your cluster. On the CKA, you may need to upgrade a cluster — in that case you explicitly unhold, upgrade, then hold again. We cover this in Chapter 23.

A
Disable Swap

swapoff -a + remove from fstab

B
Kernel Modules

overlay + br_netfilter

C
Sysctl Params

IP forwarding + bridge-nf-call

D
Container Runtime

containerd + SystemdCgroup=true

E
K8s Packages

kubeadm + kubelet + kubectl (held)

9.3 Initialize the Control Plane Node

With prerequisites done on all nodes, run kubeadm init on the control plane node only. This is the single most important command in cluster creation.

control-plane-init.sh 
# Initialize the control plane
sudo kubeadm init \
  --pod-network-cidr=10.244.0.0/16 \
  --apiserver-advertise-address=192.168.56.10 \
  --kubernetes-version=v1.30.0

# Expected output (abbreviated):
# [init] Using Kubernetes version: v1.30.0
# [certs] Generating certificates...
# [certs]  etcd/ca
# [certs]  etcd/server
# [certs]  apiserver
# [certs]  apiserver-kubelet-client
# [kubeconfig] Writing kubeconfig files...
# [kubelet-start] Starting the kubelet
# [control-plane] Creating static Pod manifests...
# [etcd] Creating static Pod manifest for local etcd
# [upload-config] Storing configuration in ConfigMap
# [mark-control-plane] Marking the node as control-plane
#
# Your Kubernetes control-plane has initialized successfully!
#
# To start using your cluster, run:
#   mkdir -p $HOME/.kube
#   sudo cp -i /etc/kubernetes/admin.conf $HOME/.kube/config
#   sudo chown $(id -u):$(id -g) $HOME/.kube/config
#
# You can now join worker nodes by running:
#   kubeadm join 192.168.56.10:6443 --token abcdef.0123456789abcdef \
#     --discovery-token-ca-cert-hash sha256:abc123...

kubeadm init Flags — What Each One Does

🌐 --pod-network-cidr
IP address range for pods. Must match your CNI plugin. Calico: 192.168.0.0/16, Flannel: 10.244.0.0/16
📡 --apiserver-advertise-address
IP address the API server advertises to other nodes. Use the control plane node's IP.
📌 --kubernetes-version
Pin to an exact version. Prevents version mismatches between components.
🔒 --control-plane-endpoint
For HA clusters: the load balancer address shared by all control plane nodes.

Post-Init: Configure kubectl Access

post-init-kubeconfig.sh 
# MUST run these 3 commands as regular user (NOT root)
mkdir -p $HOME/.kube
sudo cp -i /etc/kubernetes/admin.conf $HOME/.kube/config
sudo chown $(id -u):$(id -g) $HOME/.kube/config

# Verify kubectl works
kubectl get nodes
# NAME           STATUS     ROLES           AGE   VERSION
# control-plane  NotReady   control-plane   30s   v1.30.0
# (NotReady because CNI is not installed yet!)
⚠️ SAVE THE JOIN COMMAND!

The kubeadm join command shown at the end of kubeadm init contains a token that expires in 24 hours. Copy it now! If you lose it, regenerate with kubeadm token create --print-join-command.

What kubeadm init Creates
  $ sudo kubeadm init
       │
       ├── 🔐 Generates Certificates (/etc/kubernetes/pki/)
       │   ├── ca.crt, ca.key                    ← Cluster CA
       │   ├── apiserver.crt, apiserver.key       ← API Server TLS
       │   ├── apiserver-kubelet-client.crt       ← API→Kubelet auth
       │   ├── front-proxy-ca.crt                 ← Aggregation layer
       │   ├── etcd/ca.crt, etcd/server.crt       ← etcd TLS
       │   └── sa.key, sa.pub                     ← ServiceAccount signing
       │
       ├── 📋 Creates kubeconfig Files (/etc/kubernetes/)
       │   ├── admin.conf           ← cluster-admin kubeconfig
       │   ├── kubelet.conf         ← kubelet kubeconfig
       │   ├── controller-manager.conf
       │   └── scheduler.conf
       │
       ├── 📦 Creates Static Pod Manifests (/etc/kubernetes/manifests/)
       │   ├── etcd.yaml
       │   ├── kube-apiserver.yaml
       │   ├── kube-controller-manager.yaml
       │   └── kube-scheduler.yaml
       │
       └── 🚀 Starts Components
           ├── kubelet (systemd service) watches /etc/kubernetes/manifests/
           ├── etcd, API server, scheduler, controller manager start as static pods
           └── kube-proxy deployed as DaemonSet

9.4 Install a CNI Plugin (Pod Networking)

Without a CNI (Container Network Interface) plugin, pods cannot communicate across nodes. The control plane will stay NotReady until you install one. This is a required step the CKA expects you to know.

CNI Plugin Features Performance Complexity CKA Choice?
Calico Network policies, BGP, IPIP 🟢 High 🟡 Medium ✅ Most common
Flannel Simple VXLAN overlay 🟡 Medium 🟢 Low ✅ Good for learning
Cilium eBPF-based, advanced security 🟢 Very High 🔴 High ⚠️ Advanced
Weave Net Simple, encrypted mesh 🟡 Medium 🟢 Low ⚠️ End of life
install-cni.sh 
# Option 1: Calico (recommended for CKA — supports NetworkPolicy)
kubectl apply -f https://raw.githubusercontent.com/projectcalico/calico/v3.27.0/manifests/calico.yaml

# Option 2: Flannel (simpler, use --pod-network-cidr=10.244.0.0/16)
kubectl apply -f https://github.com/flannel-io/flannel/releases/latest/download/kube-flannel.yml

# Wait for CNI pods to be Ready
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -w
# NAME                                       READY   STATUS    AGE
# calico-kube-controllers-xxxx               1/1     Running   30s
# calico-node-xxxxx                          1/1     Running   30s
# coredns-7db6d8ff4d-xxxxx                   1/1     Running   60s  ← was Pending!

# NOW the control plane node becomes Ready
kubectl get nodes
# NAME           STATUS   ROLES           AGE    VERSION
# control-plane  Ready    control-plane   5m     v1.30.0
Why CNI is Required — Before vs After
  BEFORE CNI:                         AFTER CNI (Calico):
  ┌──────────────────────┐           ┌──────────────────────┐
  │ Node 1               │           │ Node 1               │
  │ ┌──────┐ ┌──────┐   │           │ ┌──────┐ ┌──────┐   │
  │ │Pod A │ │Pod B │   │           │ │Pod A │ │Pod B │   │
  │ │10.1  │ │10.2  │   │           │ │10.1  │ │10.2  │   │
  │ └──┬───┘ └──┬───┘   │           │ └──┬───┘ └──┬───┘   │
  │    │   ❌    │       │           │    │   ✅    │       │
  │    └────────┘       │           │    └───┬────┘       │
  │                      │           │        │ Calico     │
  │        ❌ No route    │           │        │ creates    │
  │                      │           │        │ routes     │
  │ ┌──────┐             │           │        │            │
  │ │Pod C │ (Node 2)    │           │        ▼            │
  │ │10.3  │ UNREACHABLE │           │  ┌──────┐ (Node 2)  │
  │ └──────┘             │           │  │Pod C │ REACHABLE │
  └──────────────────────┘           │  │10.3  │           │
                                      └──────────────────────┘
💡 CKA Tip: pod-network-cidr Must Match

Flannel requires --pod-network-cidr=10.244.0.0/16. Calico defaults to 192.168.0.0/16 but can be configured. If you use the wrong CIDR in kubeadm init, pods will get IPs but routing won't work. On the exam, use whatever CIDR the question specifies.

9.5 Join Worker Nodes

After the control plane is ready and CNI is installed, join each worker node. Each worker must have completed all prerequisites from Section 9.2.

worker-nodes.sh 
# Run on EACH worker node (use the command from kubeadm init output)
sudo kubeadm join 192.168.56.10:6443 \
  --token abcdef.0123456789abcdef \
  --discovery-token-ca-cert-hash sha256:abc123def456...

# Expected output:
# [preflight] Running pre-flight checks
# [preflight] Reading configuration from the cluster...
# [kubelet-start] Starting the kubelet
# [kubelet-start] Waiting for kubelet to perform TLS Bootstrap...
#
# This node has joined the cluster:
# * Certificate signing request was sent and approved
# * Run 'kubectl get nodes' on the control-plane to see this node

# If token expired (after 24 hours), regenerate on control plane:
sudo kubeadm token create --print-join-command
# kubeadm join 192.168.56.10:6443 --token xyz789... \
#   --discovery-token-ca-cert-hash sha256:newHash...
Complete Cluster Build — Step by Step
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  KUBEADM CLUSTER CREATION                                       │
  │                                                                 │
  │  Step 1: Prerequisites (ALL nodes)                               │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │ swap off │ kernel modules │ sysctl │ containerd │ kubeadm │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                              ▼                                   │
  │  Step 2: kubeadm init (CONTROL PLANE only)                      │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │ $ sudo kubeadm init --pod-network-cidr=10.244.0.0/16     │   │
  │  │ → Generates certs → Starts API server → Creates configs   │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                              ▼                                   │
  │  Step 3: Setup kubeconfig (CONTROL PLANE)                       │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │ $ mkdir -p ~/.kube                                        │   │
  │  │ $ sudo cp /etc/kubernetes/admin.conf ~/.kube/config       │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                              ▼                                   │
  │  Step 4: Install CNI (CONTROL PLANE)                            │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │ $ kubectl apply -f calico.yaml                            │   │
  │  │ → Control plane becomes Ready, CoreDNS pods start         │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                              ▼                                   │
  │  Step 5: kubeadm join (EACH WORKER)                             │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │ $ sudo kubeadm join <IP>:6443 --token X --discovery...    │   │
  │  │ → Worker registers with API server → becomes Ready        │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                              ▼                                   │
  │  Step 6: Verify ✅                                              │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │ $ kubectl get nodes                                       │   │
  │  │ control-plane  Ready   control-plane   5m    v1.30.0      │   │
  │  │ worker-1       Ready   <none>          2m    v1.30.0      │   │
  │  │ worker-2       Ready   <none>          1m    v1.30.0      │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
📖 Worker Node ROLES Show <none>

By default, worker nodes show <none> for ROLES. You can label them: kubectl label node worker-1 node-role.kubernetes.io/worker=. This is cosmetic only — it doesn't change any behavior.

9.6 Verifying the Cluster

After all nodes have joined, run these checks to confirm the cluster is fully operational:

verify-cluster.sh 
# 1. All nodes Ready?
kubectl get nodes -o wide
# NAME           STATUS   ROLES           AGE   VERSION   INTERNAL-IP
# control-plane  Ready    control-plane   10m   v1.30.0   192.168.56.10
# worker-1       Ready    <none>          5m    v1.30.0   192.168.56.11
# worker-2       Ready    <none>          3m    v1.30.0   192.168.56.12

# 2. All system pods Running?
kubectl get pods -n kube-system
# NAME                                       READY   STATUS    RESTARTS
# calico-kube-controllers-xxxx               1/1     Running   0
# calico-node-xxxxx (one per node)           1/1     Running   0
# coredns-7db6d8ff4d-xxxx                    1/1     Running   0
# coredns-7db6d8ff4d-yyyy                    1/1     Running   0
# etcd-control-plane                         1/1     Running   0
# kube-apiserver-control-plane               1/1     Running   0
# kube-controller-manager-control-plane      1/1     Running   0
# kube-proxy-xxxxx (one per node)            1/1     Running   0
# kube-scheduler-control-plane               1/1     Running   0

# 3. Cluster info
kubectl cluster-info
# Kubernetes control plane is running at https://192.168.56.10:6443
# CoreDNS is running at https://192.168.56.10:6443/api/v1/.../kube-dns

# 4. Deploy a test pod to confirm everything works
kubectl run test-nginx --image=nginx --port=80
kubectl get pod test-nginx -o wide
# NAME         READY   STATUS    NODE
# test-nginx   1/1     Running   worker-1     ← scheduled to a worker!

# Clean up test
kubectl delete pod test-nginx
✅
Healthy Cluster Checklist

All nodes STATUS=Ready. All kube-system pods Running. CoreDNS pods Running (not Pending). Test pod schedules to a worker node.

❌
Common Issues

Node NotReady = kubelet not running or CNI missing. CoreDNS Pending = CNI not installed. Pod stuck on control plane = no workers joined.

9.7 Practice Exercises

Building clusters from scratch is a muscle memory skill. Practice until you can complete Exercise 9.1 in under 30 minutes.

🏋️
Exercise 9.1: Build a 3-Node Cluster

Create 3 VMs (Vagrant or cloud). Install prerequisites on all. Run kubeadm init, install Calico, join 2 workers. Deploy anihpj and verify. ⏱️ Target: 30 min.

🔑
Exercise 9.2: Token Recovery

Pretend the join token expired. Generate a new one with kubeadm token create --print-join-command. Join a new worker using the fresh token.

🔧
Exercise 9.3: Fix a Broken Node

Intentionally break prerequisites on a worker (enable swap, stop containerd). Troubleshoot and fix it until the node shows Ready.

🎯
Exercise 9.4: Add Control Plane Node

Use kubeadm join --control-plane to add a second control plane. Verify etcd member list shows 2 members. (HA setup)

💡 Vagrant Quick Setup for Practice

Use a Vagrantfile to spin up 3 Ubuntu VMs with one command: vagrant up. Each VM gets a static IP and 2 GB RAM. This is the fastest way to practice kubeadm clusters repeatedly.

🔄 Bonus: kubeadm Cluster Upgrade (CKA Critical Skill)

Upgrading a Kubernetes cluster is a guaranteed CKA topic. The process is sequential: upgrade the control plane first, then each worker node one at a time. Never skip minor versions (e.g., 1.28 → 1.30 is not allowed).

1
Upgrade kubeadm

Update the kubeadm package to the target version on the control plane node.

2
Plan the Upgrade

Run kubeadm upgrade plan to verify compatibility and see available versions.

3
Apply the Upgrade

Run kubeadm upgrade apply v1.XX.Y to upgrade control plane components.

4
Drain & Upgrade kubelet

Drain the node, upgrade kubelet and kubectl packages, restart kubelet.

5
Uncordon & Repeat

Uncordon the node, then repeat steps 1 + 4 on each worker node.

upgrade-control-plane.sh 
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
#  CONTROL PLANE UPGRADE (e.g., 1.29.0 → 1.30.0)
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

# Step 1: Update kubeadm to target version
sudo apt-mark unhold kubeadm
sudo apt-get update
sudo apt-get install -y kubeadm=1.30.0-1.1
sudo apt-mark hold kubeadm

# Step 2: Verify the upgrade plan
sudo kubeadm upgrade plan
# [upgrade] Found 1 control plane instance...
# Components that will be upgraded:
#   kube-apiserver     v1.29.0 → v1.30.0
#   kube-controller-manager  v1.29.0 → v1.30.0
#   kube-scheduler     v1.29.0 → v1.30.0
#   etcd               3.5.10 → 3.5.12

# Step 3: Apply the upgrade
sudo kubeadm upgrade apply v1.30.0
# [upgrade] Successfully upgraded to v1.30.0!

# Step 4: Drain the control plane node
kubectl drain control-plane --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data

# Step 5: Upgrade kubelet and kubectl
sudo apt-mark unhold kubelet kubectl
sudo apt-get install -y kubelet=1.30.0-1.1 kubectl=1.30.0-1.1
sudo apt-mark hold kubelet kubectl

# Step 6: Restart kubelet
sudo systemctl daemon-reload
sudo systemctl restart kubelet

# Step 7: Uncordon the node
kubectl uncordon control-plane
# node/control-plane uncordoned
upgrade-worker-node.sh 
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
#  WORKER NODE UPGRADE (repeat for each worker)
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

# On the CONTROL PLANE — drain the worker first
kubectl drain worker-1 --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data

# SSH to worker-1, then:

# Step 1: Upgrade kubeadm
sudo apt-mark unhold kubeadm
sudo apt-get update
sudo apt-get install -y kubeadm=1.30.0-1.1
sudo apt-mark hold kubeadm

# Step 2: Upgrade node config (NOT "apply" — use "node")
sudo kubeadm upgrade node

# Step 3: Upgrade kubelet and kubectl
sudo apt-mark unhold kubelet kubectl
sudo apt-get install -y kubelet=1.30.0-1.1 kubectl=1.30.0-1.1
sudo apt-mark hold kubelet kubectl

# Step 4: Restart kubelet
sudo systemctl daemon-reload
sudo systemctl restart kubelet

# Back on CONTROL PLANE — uncordon the worker
kubectl uncordon worker-1
# node/worker-1 uncordoned

# Verify all nodes are at the new version
kubectl get nodes
# NAME            STATUS   ROLES           AGE   VERSION
# control-plane   Ready    control-plane   90d   v1.30.0
# worker-1        Ready    <none>          90d   v1.30.0
# worker-2        Ready    <none>          90d   v1.29.0  ← still needs upgrade
🚨 CKA Upgrade Rules — Memorize These

1) Always upgrade control plane FIRST, then workers. 2) Never skip minor versions (1.28 → 1.29 → 1.30, not 1.28 → 1.30). 3) Control plane uses kubeadm upgrade apply, workers use kubeadm upgrade node. 4) Always drain before upgrading kubelet, uncordon after.

🔧 Bonus: Troubleshooting Common kubeadm Issues

When things go wrong during cluster setup, these are the errors you'll encounter most. Knowing the fix saves crucial exam time.

Symptom Likely Cause Fix
node NotReady CNI not installed or container runtime stopped Install CNI plugin; systemctl start containerd
kubeadm init hangs Swap enabled or port 6443 already in use swapoff -a; check ss -tlnp | grep 6443
connection refused :6443 API server not running (bad manifest edit) Check /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml; restore backup
kubelet not starting cgroupDriver mismatch or containerd down Match cgroupDriver: systemd in kubelet config AND containerd config
token expired Join tokens expire after 24 hours kubeadm token create --print-join-command
etcd unhealthy etcd cert expired or data corruption Check certs: openssl x509 -in ca.crt -noout -dates; restore from backup
Pods stuck Pending No worker nodes or all nodes tainted Join workers; or remove taint: kubectl taint nodes --all node-role.kubernetes.io/control-plane-
coredns CrashLoopBackOff CNI not installed or DNS loop Install CNI first; check /etc/resolv.conf for loops
kubeadm Troubleshooting Decision Tree
  kubectl get nodes → Node "NotReady"?
       │
       ├── YES ─────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
       │   ├── Is CNI installed? ──── NO ──▶ Install CNI plugin │
       │   ├── Is containerd running? NO ──▶ systemctl start     │
       │   └── Is kubelet running? ── NO ──▶ journalctl -u kubelet
       │                                                         │
       ├── kubectl can't connect? ──────────────────────────────┐
       │   ├── Is kubeconfig set? ─── NO ──▶ export KUBECONFIG=  │
       │   ├── API server running? ── NO ──▶ Check static pod    │
       │   └── Firewall blocking? ── YES ──▶ ufw allow 6443     │
       │                                                         │
       └── Pods stuck "Pending"? ───────────────────────────────┐
           ├── Nodes available? ───── NO ──▶ Join worker nodes   │
           ├── Resources enough? ─── NO ──▶ Check requests/limits│
           └── Taints blocking? ──── YES ──▶ Remove/add tolerations
💡 CKA Debugging Lifeline: 3 Commands

When anything fails during cluster setup, these 3 commands reveal 90% of issues:
kubectl get nodes → Are nodes Ready?
kubectl get pods -n kube-system → Are system pods running?
sudo journalctl -u kubelet -f → What is kubelet complaining about?

Chapter 10: Cluster Configuration and kubeconfig

The kubeconfig file is your key to the cluster — without it, kubectl can't talk to the API server. This chapter dives deep into kubeconfig structure, managing multiple clusters, API server and kubelet configuration, admission controllers, and API groups. These are heavily tested on the CKA.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~1.5 hours ⚙️ Config 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Understand kubeconfig file structure — clusters, users, contexts
  • ✅ Manage multiple clusters and switch contexts
  • ✅ Configure the API server with custom flags
  • ✅ Understand kubelet configuration and static pods
  • ✅ Explain admission controllers and their role in the API pipeline
  • ✅ Navigate API groups and resource paths
🎓 CKA Relevance
Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration 25%
kubeconfig = Top CKA Skill

On the CKA exam, you work with multiple clusters. Switching between them quickly with kubectl config use-context is essential — fumbling with kubeconfig costs precious exam minutes.

  • Know the 3 parts — clusters, users, contexts — and how they link together
  • Know how to switch contexts fast — kubectl config use-context
  • Know where kubeconfig lives — $HOME/.kube/config or $KUBECONFIG

10.1 kubeconfig Deep Dive

The kubeconfig file tells kubectl three things: where to connect (cluster), who to authenticate as (user), and which combination to use (context).

kubeconfig File Structure — The Three Pillars
  ┌─────────────────────── kubeconfig (~/.kube/config) ───────────────────────┐
  │                                                                           │
  │  ┌── clusters ──────────┐  ┌── users ──────────────┐  ┌── contexts ────┐ │
  │  │                      │  │                        │  │                │ │
  │  │  - cluster: prod     │  │  - user: prod-admin    │  │  - context:    │ │
  │  │    server: https://  │  │    client-certificate  │  │    name: prod  │ │
  │  │      prod:6443       │  │    client-key          │  │    cluster:    │ │
  │  │    certificate-      │  │                        │  │      prod     │ │
  │  │      authority       │  │  - user: dev-admin     │  │    user:      │ │
  │  │                      │  │    token: eyJhb...     │  │      prod-    │ │
  │  │  - cluster: dev      │  │                        │  │      admin    │ │
  │  │    server: https://  │  │  - user: staging-      │  │    namespace: │ │
  │  │      dev:6443        │  │      readonly          │  │      default  │ │
  │  │    certificate-      │  │    token: abc123...    │  │               │ │
  │  │      authority       │  │                        │  │  - context:   │ │
  │  │                      │  │                        │  │    name: dev  │ │
  │  │  - cluster: staging  │  │                        │  │    cluster:   │ │
  │  │    server: https://  │  │                        │  │      dev     │ │
  │  │      staging:6443    │  │                        │  │    user:     │ │
  │  │                      │  │                        │  │      dev-    │ │
  │  └──────────┬───────────┘  └────────────┬───────────┘  │      admin  │ │
  │             │                           │              └──────┬─────┘ │
  │             └───────────┐  ┌────────────┘                     │       │
  │                         ▼  ▼                                  │       │
  │  current-context: prod  ◀─────────────────────────────────────┘       │
  │                                                                       │
  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
~/.kube/config 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Config

# The active context (used when you run kubectl)
current-context: production

# WHERE to connect
clusters:
- cluster:
    certificate-authority-data: LS0tLS1...   # Base64 CA cert
    server: https://192.168.56.10:6443
  name: production

- cluster:
    certificate-authority-data: LS0tLS1...
    server: https://10.0.0.5:6443
  name: development

# WHO to authenticate as
users:
- name: prod-admin
  user:
    client-certificate-data: LS0tLS1...   # Base64 client cert
    client-key-data: LS0tLS1...           # Base64 client key

- name: dev-admin
  user:
    token: eyJhbGciOiJSUzI1NiIs...        # Bearer token

# WHICH cluster + user combination to use
contexts:
- context:
    cluster: production
    user: prod-admin
    namespace: default
  name: production

- context:
    cluster: development
    user: dev-admin
    namespace: dev-apps
  name: development

kubeconfig — Field-by-Field Breakdown

📍 clusters[].server
API server URL. Format: https://<ip>:6443. Port 6443 is the default.
🔐 clusters[].certificate-authority-data
Base64-encoded CA certificate. Kubectl uses this to verify the API server's TLS certificate.
👤 users[].client-certificate-data
Base64-encoded client cert. The API server verifies this to authenticate the user.
🔗 contexts[].cluster + user + namespace
A context links a cluster and user together, plus an optional default namespace.
⭐ current-context
Which context kubectl uses right now. Changed via kubectl config use-context <name>

10.2 Managing Multiple Clusters & Contexts

On the CKA exam, you'll work with multiple clusters — each question tells you which context to use. Switching contexts fast is critical.

context-commands.sh 
# View all contexts
kubectl config get-contexts
# CURRENT   NAME          CLUSTER       AUTHINFO      NAMESPACE
# *         production    production    prod-admin    default
#           development   development   dev-admin     dev-apps
#           staging       staging       stg-viewer    staging

# Switch to a different context
kubectl config use-context development
# Switched to context "development".

# View current context
kubectl config current-context
# development

# Set default namespace for current context
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=kube-system
# Context "development" modified.

# View the full kubeconfig
kubectl config view
# (displays the merged kubeconfig YAML)

# View just one cluster's details
kubectl config view --minify
# (shows only the active context's config)
🆕
Create a New Context
create-context.sh 
# Add a new cluster
kubectl config set-cluster prod-cluster \
  --server=https://10.0.1.5:6443 \
  --certificate-authority=/path/to/ca.crt

# Add a new user
kubectl config set-credentials jane \
  --client-certificate=/path/to/jane.crt \
  --client-key=/path/to/jane.key

# Create a context linking them
kubectl config set-context jane@prod \
  --cluster=prod-cluster \
  --user=jane \
  --namespace=web-apps
📁
KUBECONFIG Env Variable
kubeconfig-env.sh 
# Use a specific kubeconfig file
export KUBECONFIG=/path/to/custom-config

# Merge multiple kubeconfig files
export KUBECONFIG=~/.kube/config:\
  ~/.kube/prod-config:\
  ~/.kube/dev-config

# View merged result
kubectl config view --flatten

# One-time override (no env change)
kubectl --kubeconfig=/path/to/config \
  get nodes
💡 CKA Shortcut: First Command on Every Question

Every CKA exam question starts with a context switch instruction. Make this muscle memory:
kubectl config use-context k8s-cluster-1
If you forget this step and work on the wrong cluster, you'll lose points for that question.

10.3 API Server Configuration

The kube-apiserver is the front door to the entire cluster. Understanding its flags helps you troubleshoot and is tested on the CKA.

API Server — Static Pod Configuration
  /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml
  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  apiVersion: v1                                                │
  │  kind: Pod                                                     │
  │  metadata:                                                     │
  │    name: kube-apiserver                                        │
  │    namespace: kube-system                                      │
  │  spec:                                                         │
  │    containers:                                                 │
  │    - command:                                                  │
  │      - kube-apiserver                                          │
  │      - --advertise-address=192.168.56.10                       │
  │      - --etcd-servers=https://127.0.0.1:2379                   │
  │      - --service-cluster-ip-range=10.96.0.0/12                │
  │      - --client-ca-file=/etc/kubernetes/pki/ca.crt             │
  │      - --tls-cert-file=/etc/kubernetes/pki/apiserver.crt       │
  │      - --tls-private-key-file=/etc/kubernetes/pki/apiserver.key│
  │      - --enable-admission-plugins=NodeRestriction              │
  │      - --authorization-mode=Node,RBAC                          │
  │      - --enable-bootstrap-token-auth=true                      │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                          │
                  kubelet watches this file
                  and auto-restarts the pod
                  when it changes

Key API Server Flags — CKA Must-Know

🏠 --etcd-servers
Where the API server stores all cluster data. Default: https://127.0.0.1:2379
🌐 --service-cluster-ip-range
CIDR for ClusterIP Services. Default: 10.96.0.0/12. Must not overlap pod CIDR.
🔒 --authorization-mode
Node,RBAC is the default. Order matters — first match wins. Always include RBAC.
🛡️ --enable-admission-plugins
Comma-separated list of admission controllers. NodeRestriction is critical for security.
🔑 --client-ca-file
CA certificate for verifying client certificates (mutual TLS). Located in /etc/kubernetes/pki/
⚠️ Static Pod = Auto-Restart on Change

The API server runs as a static pod. If you edit /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml, kubelet automatically restarts it. But a bad edit will crash the API server — always backup the manifest first: cp kube-apiserver.yaml kube-apiserver.yaml.bak

10.4 kubelet Configuration

The kubelet runs on every node. It's the agent that talks to the API server, manages pods, reports node status, and runs static pods. Unlike other control plane components, kubelet is NOT a pod — it's a systemd service.

kubelet — Configuration File Locations
  ┌────────────────── kubelet Configuration Paths ──────────────────┐
  │                                                                 │
  │  /etc/systemd/system/kubelet.service.d/10-kubeadm.conf          │
  │  └── Environment flags passed to kubelet binary                 │
  │      └── --config=/var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml                  │
  │                                                                 │
  │  /var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml        ← Main config file         │
  │  ├── clusterDNS: ["10.96.0.10"]                                │
  │  ├── clusterDomain: cluster.local                               │
  │  ├── staticPodPath: /etc/kubernetes/manifests   ← Static pods!  │
  │  ├── authentication:                                            │
  │  │   └── x509: clientCAFile: /etc/kubernetes/pki/ca.crt         │
  │  ├── authorization:                                             │
  │  │   └── mode: Webhook                                          │
  │  └── cgroupDriver: systemd          ← Must match containerd     │
  │                                                                 │
  │  /etc/kubernetes/kubelet.conf        ← kubelet's kubeconfig     │
  │  └── server, client-certificate, client-key                     │
  │                                                                 │
  │  /etc/kubernetes/manifests/          ← Static pod manifests     │
  │  ├── etcd.yaml                                                  │
  │  ├── kube-apiserver.yaml                                        │
  │  ├── kube-controller-manager.yaml                               │
  │  └── kube-scheduler.yaml                                        │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
kubelet-commands.sh 
# Check kubelet status
sudo systemctl status kubelet
# ● kubelet.service - kubelet: The Kubernetes Node Agent
#    Active: active (running) since...

# View kubelet logs (troubleshooting)
sudo journalctl -u kubelet -f
# (live log stream — look for errors here)

# Restart kubelet (after config changes)
sudo systemctl restart kubelet

# View kubelet config
cat /var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml

# Static pods: drop a manifest in /etc/kubernetes/manifests/
# kubelet will auto-create the pod (no API server needed!)
cat <<EOF | sudo tee /etc/kubernetes/manifests/static-nginx.yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: static-nginx
  namespace: default
spec:
  containers:
  - name: nginx
    image: nginx:1.25
    ports:
    - containerPort: 80
EOF

# Verify static pod exists
kubectl get pods | grep static-nginx
# static-nginx-control-plane   1/1   Running   0   10s
📖 Static Pods vs Regular Pods

Static pods are managed directly by kubelet, not the API server. They are created from YAML files in /etc/kubernetes/manifests/. The API server sees a mirror copy but cannot control them. All control plane components (etcd, apiserver, scheduler, controller-manager) run as static pods.

10.5 Admission Controllers

Admission controllers sit in the API request pipeline after authentication and authorization but before the object is persisted. They can validate, mutate, or reject requests.

API Request Pipeline — Where Admission Controllers Sit
  kubectl apply -f pod.yaml
       │
       ▼
  ┌─────────────────┐   ┌──────────────────┐   ┌──────────────────────────┐
  │ 1. AUTHENTICATION│──▶│ 2. AUTHORIZATION  │──▶│ 3. ADMISSION CONTROLLERS │
  │                  │   │                   │   │                          │
  │ WHO are you?     │   │ CAN you do this?  │   │ SHOULD we allow this?    │
  │ • x509 certs     │   │ • RBAC            │   │                          │
  │ • Bearer tokens  │   │ • Node            │   │ ┌────────────────────┐  │
  │ • ServiceAccount │   │ • Webhook         │   │ │ Mutating Webhooks  │  │
  │                  │   │                   │   │ │ (modify request)   │  │
  └─────────────────┘   └──────────────────┘   │ └─────────┬──────────┘  │
                                                 │           ▼            │
                                                 │ ┌────────────────────┐│
                                                 │ │ Validating Webhooks ││
                                                 │ │ (accept or reject) ││
                                                 │ └─────────┬──────────┘│
                                                 └───────────┼───────────┘
                                                              ▼
                                                 ┌──────────────────────┐
                                                 │ 4. PERSIST TO etcd   │
                                                 │ (object is stored)   │
                                                 └──────────────────────┘
Admission Controller Type What It Does CKA?
NamespaceLifecycle Validating Prevents creating objects in non-existent or terminating namespaces ✅
LimitRanger Mutating Enforces LimitRange defaults — auto-sets resource requests/limits ✅
ResourceQuota Validating Rejects requests that exceed namespace ResourceQuota ✅
NodeRestriction Validating Limits kubelet to only modify its own node and pods ✅
DefaultStorageClass Mutating Adds default StorageClass to PVCs that don't specify one 🔹
ServiceAccount Mutating Injects the default ServiceAccount into pods that don't specify one ✅
admission-controller-commands.sh 
# View enabled admission plugins
kubectl -n kube-system describe pod kube-apiserver-control-plane \
  | grep enable-admission
#   --enable-admission-plugins=NodeRestriction

# To enable additional plugins, edit the API server manifest:
sudo vi /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml
# Change:
#   --enable-admission-plugins=NodeRestriction
# To:
#   --enable-admission-plugins=NodeRestriction,NamespaceLifecycle,LimitRanger

# kubelet will auto-restart the API server
# Verify the API server comes back up
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep apiserver
💡 CKA Tip: Enabling Admission Controllers

If a CKA question asks you to enable an admission controller, you edit /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml, add it to the --enable-admission-plugins flag, save the file, and wait for the API server pod to restart automatically.

10.6 API Groups and Resource Paths

Every Kubernetes resource belongs to an API group. Understanding this helps you write RBAC rules, use kubectl api-resources, and debug API calls.

Kubernetes API Group Hierarchy
  /api/v1  (Core / Legacy group — no group name in apiVersion)
  ├── pods                  apiVersion: v1
  ├── services              apiVersion: v1
  ├── configmaps            apiVersion: v1
  ├── secrets               apiVersion: v1
  ├── namespaces            apiVersion: v1
  ├── nodes                 apiVersion: v1
  ├── persistentvolumes     apiVersion: v1
  └── serviceaccounts       apiVersion: v1

  /apis/apps/v1  (Named group: "apps")
  ├── deployments           apiVersion: apps/v1
  ├── statefulsets           apiVersion: apps/v1
  ├── daemonsets             apiVersion: apps/v1
  └── replicasets            apiVersion: apps/v1

  /apis/networking.k8s.io/v1
  ├── ingresses             apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
  └── networkpolicies       apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1

  /apis/rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
  ├── roles                 apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
  ├── rolebindings          apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
  ├── clusterroles          apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
  └── clusterrolebindings   apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1

  /apis/batch/v1
  ├── jobs                  apiVersion: batch/v1
  └── cronjobs              apiVersion: batch/v1

  /apis/storage.k8s.io/v1
  ├── storageclasses        apiVersion: storage.k8s.io/v1
  └── persistentvolumeclaims apiVersion: v1  (core group!)
api-groups-commands.sh 
# List ALL available API resources
kubectl api-resources
# NAME                  SHORTNAMES   APIVERSION          NAMESPACED   KIND
# pods                  po           v1                  true         Pod
# services              svc          v1                  true         Service
# deployments           deploy       apps/v1             true         Deployment
# nodes                 no           v1                  false        Node

# Filter by API group
kubectl api-resources --api-group=apps
# NAME           SHORTNAMES   APIVERSION   NAMESPACED   KIND
# deployments    deploy       apps/v1      true         Deployment
# statefulsets   sts          apps/v1      true         StatefulSet

# List API versions
kubectl api-versions
# apps/v1
# batch/v1
# networking.k8s.io/v1
# rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
# v1  ← core group

# Explain a resource (shows API group and version)
kubectl explain deployment
# KIND:     Deployment
# VERSION:  apps/v1
# DESCRIPTION: ...
📖 Core Group vs Named Groups

Core group resources (Pod, Service, ConfigMap, Secret) use apiVersion: v1 — no group prefix. Named group resources use apiVersion: groupName/v1 — e.g., apps/v1 for Deployments. When writing RBAC rules, core group resources use apiGroups: [""] (empty string).

🔌 Bonus: Control Plane Component Communication Map

Understanding how control plane components talk to each other is essential for debugging and CKA troubleshooting. Every arrow below represents a real network connection.

Control Plane Architecture — Who Talks to Whom
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                        CONTROL PLANE NODE                               │
  │                                                                          │
  │   ┌──────────────────┐        ┌──────────────────────────┐              │
  │   │  kube-scheduler  │───────▶│      kube-apiserver       │              │
  │   │                  │  HTTPS │  (Central communication   │              │
  │   │ "Where should    │  :6443 │   hub — ALL components    │              │
  │   │  this pod run?"  │        │   talk through me)        │              │
  │   └──────────────────┘        │                           │              │
  │                                │  Ports:                  │              │
  │   ┌──────────────────┐        │   6443 — HTTPS API       │              │
  │   │ kube-controller- │───────▶│                           │              │
  │   │ manager          │  HTTPS │                           │              │
  │   │                  │  :6443 │                           │              │

  │   │  desired state   │                  │                                │

  │                                │  (All state stored│                     │
  │                                │   here — ONLY the │                     │
  │                                │   API server talks │                    │
  │                                │   to etcd!)        │                    │
  │                                │                   │                     │
  │                                │  Ports:           │                     │
  │                                │   2379 — client   │                     │
  │                                │   2380 — peer     │                     │
  │                                └──────────────────┘                      │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                                        │
                          ┌─────────────┼─────────────┐
                          ▼             ▼             ▼
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                         WORKER NODES                                     │
  │                                                                          │
  │   ┌──────────────────┐    ┌──────────────────┐   ┌────────────────┐     │
  │   │     kubelet       │    │   kube-proxy     │   │   CNI Plugin   │     │
  │   │                  │    │                  │   │  (e.g. Calico) │     │
  │   │ Talks to API     │    │ Watches API      │   │                │     │
  │   │ server to:       │    │ server for       │   │ Manages pod    │     │
  │   │ • Report status  │    │ Service/Endpoint │   │ networking and │     │
  │   │ • Get pod specs  │    │ changes, updates │   │ network policy │     │
  │   │ • Send events    │    │ iptables/IPVS    │   │ enforcement    │     │
  │   └──┬───────────────┘    └──────────────────┘   └────────────────┘     │
  │      │                                                                   │
  │      ▼                                                                   │
  │   ┌──────────────────┐                                                   │
  │   │   containerd     │  kubelet → containerd via CRI (Container          │
  │   │   (container     │  Runtime Interface) to create/stop containers     │
  │   │    runtime)      │                                                   │
  │   └──────────────────┘                                                   │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  KEY RULE: Only the API server talks to etcd. Everything else talks to the API server.

📋 Bonus: KUBECONFIG Merge Priority Order

When multiple kubeconfig sources exist, kubectl merges them in a specific precedence order. Knowing this prevents "wrong cluster" mistakes.

kubeconfig Resolution — Priority Order (Highest → Lowest)
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  PRIORITY 1 (Highest)                                           │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │  --kubeconfig flag                                       │   │
  │  │  kubectl --kubeconfig=/path/to/config get pods           │   │
  │  │  → Uses ONLY this file, ignores everything else          │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                          │ if not set                           │
  │                          ▼                                      │
  │  PRIORITY 2                                                     │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │  $KUBECONFIG environment variable                        │   │
  │  │  export KUBECONFIG=/a/config:/b/config:/c/config         │   │
  │  │  → Merges ALL files (colon-separated on Linux)           │   │
  │  │  → First context/cluster/user wins on conflict           │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                          │ if not set                           │
  │                          ▼                                      │
  │  PRIORITY 3 (Default)                                           │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │  $HOME/.kube/config                                      │   │
  │  │  → The default kubeconfig file                           │   │
  │  │  → Created by kubeadm init (admin.conf copied here)      │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

⚡ Bonus: kubectl config — CKA Speed Reference

Key commands you'll use on exam day, organized for quick recall.

Task Command When to Use
Switch context kubectl config use-context <name> Start of every CKA question
See current context kubectl config current-context Verify you're on the right cluster
List all contexts kubectl config get-contexts Find available cluster names
Set default namespace kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=X Avoid typing -n X repeatedly
View active config kubectl config view --minify Debug connection issues
Flatten merged configs kubectl config view --flatten Export a merged config to a single file
Check API server URL kubectl cluster-info Verify cluster endpoint
Test connectivity kubectl get nodes Confirm kubectl can reach the cluster
💡 Pro Tip: Alias for Speed

The CKA exam pre-configures alias k=kubectl. You can also add: alias kgc='kubectl config get-contexts' and alias kuc='kubectl config use-context' to shave seconds off every question.

Chapter 11: etcd — The Cluster Database

etcd is the single source of truth for your entire Kubernetes cluster. Every object — every pod, service, configmap, secret — is stored in etcd. Lose etcd, lose your cluster. That's why etcd backup is one of the most important admin tasks and is guaranteed to appear on the CKA exam.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~1.5 hours 💾 etcd 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Understand etcd's role — what it stores and why it's critical
  • ✅ Know etcd's architecture and the Raft consensus algorithm
  • ✅ Backup etcd using etcdctl snapshot save (CKA guaranteed question)
  • ✅ Restore etcd from a backup snapshot
  • ✅ Secure etcd with TLS certificates and access control
🎓 CKA Relevance
Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration 25%
etcd Backup = GUARANTEED CKA Question

Almost every CKA exam includes an etcd backup and/or restore question. This is worth significant points. Memorize the backup command and the certificate paths.

  • Memorize: ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot save with all 3 cert flags
  • Know the cert paths: /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/
  • Practice restore blindfolded: stop etcd → restore snapshot → update data-dir → restart

11.1 What is etcd?

etcd is a distributed, reliable key-value store that Kubernetes uses to store all cluster state. Every kubectl apply, every pod creation, every secret — all stored in etcd.

🏛️
Key-Value Store

Stores data as key-value pairs, similar to a dictionary. Example: /registry/pods/default/nginx → pod YAML.

🔄
Distributed

Runs on multiple nodes (3 or 5 for HA) and replicates data using the Raft consensus algorithm.

🔒
Strongly Consistent

All reads return the latest write. No stale data ever — critical for cluster state correctness.

📊
Watch API

Supports efficient watchers. The API server subscribes to changes — this powers the entire controller pattern.

What etcd Stores — The Kubernetes Registry
  etcd Key Space (all under /registry/)
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                 │
  │  /registry/                                                     │
  │  ├── pods/                                                      │
  │  │   ├── default/                                               │
  │  │   │   ├── nginx-deployment-abc123     ← Pod spec + status    │
  │  │   │   └── jobpost-web-xyz789          ← Our anihpj app!      │
  │  │   └── kube-system/                                           │
  │  │       ├── coredns-7db6d8ff4d-xxx      ← System pods          │
  │  │       └── calico-node-yyy                                    │
  │  ├── deployments/                                               │
  │  │   └── default/                                               │
  │  │       └── nginx-deployment            ← Deployment spec      │
  │  ├── services/                                                  │
  │  │   ├── default/kubernetes              ← The kubernetes svc   │
  │  │   └── default/jobpost-service         ← Our app service      │
  │  ├── secrets/                                                   │
  │  │   └── default/db-credentials          ← ⚠️ Stored in etcd!   │
  │  ├── configmaps/                                                │
  │  ├── namespaces/                                                │
  │  ├── nodes/                                                     │
  │  ├── clusterroles/                                              │
  │  └── ... (every K8s object type)                                │
  │                                                                 │
  │  📌 Size: Typically 2-8 GB for a large production cluster       │
  │  📌 Default port: 2379 (client) / 2380 (peer)                  │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
🚨 etcd Contains Secrets in Plain Text

By default, Kubernetes Secrets are stored unencrypted in etcd. Anyone with direct access to etcd can read all secrets. Enable encryption at rest (covered in Section 11.5) to protect sensitive data.

11.2 etcd Architecture & Raft Consensus

In production/HA clusters, etcd runs on 3 or 5 nodes. They elect a leader using the Raft consensus algorithm, ensuring data consistency even if nodes fail.

Raft Consensus — Leader Election & Replication
  ┌─────────────────────── etcd Cluster (3 Members) ───────────────────────┐
  │                                                                         │
  │    ┌─────────────────┐   ┌─────────────────┐   ┌─────────────────┐     │
  │    │   etcd Member 1  │   │   etcd Member 2  │   │   etcd Member 3  │   │
  │    │   ★ LEADER       │   │   FOLLOWER       │   │   FOLLOWER       │   │
  │    │                  │   │                  │   │                  │     │
  │    │  ┌────────────┐  │   │  ┌────────────┐  │   │  ┌────────────┐ │   │
  │    │  │  Log: v101  │  │   │  │  Log: v101  │  │   │  │  Log: v101 │ │   │
  │    │  │  Log: v102  │  │──▶│  │  Log: v102  │  │──▶│  │  Log: v102 │ │   │
  │    │  │  Log: v103  │  │   │  │  Log: v103  │  │   │  │  Log: v103 │ │   │
  │    │  └────────────┘  │   │  └────────────┘  │   │  └────────────┘ │   │
  │    │   Port: 2379     │   │   Port: 2379     │   │   Port: 2379    │   │
  │    │   Peer:  2380    │   │   Peer:  2380    │   │   Peer:  2380   │   │
  │    └─────────────────┘   └─────────────────┘   └─────────────────┘   │
  │                                                                       │
  │    ✅ Write: Client → Leader → Replicate to majority → ACK            │
  │    ✅ Read:  Client → Any member (leader or follower)                  │
  │    ✅ Quorum: 2 of 3 must agree (tolerates 1 failure)                  │
  │                                                                       │
  │    Quorum formula: (N/2) + 1                                           │
  │    ┌─────────┬────────────┬────────────────┬──────────────────┐       │
  │    │ Members │   Quorum   │ Fault Tolerance│    Use Case      │       │
  │    ├─────────┼────────────┼────────────────┼──────────────────┤       │
  │    │    1    │     1      │       0        │ Development      │       │
  │    │    3    │     2      │       1        │ Small production │       │
  │    │    5    │     3      │       2        │ Large production │       │
  │    │    7    │     4      │       3        │ Rare (overhead)  │       │
  │    └─────────┴────────────┴────────────────┴──────────────────┘       │
  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
✍️Client Write
→
★Leader Receives
→
📤Replicate Log
→
✅Majority ACK
→
💾Commit & Respond
📖 Why Odd Numbers? (3 or 5, Never 4 or 6)

A 3-member cluster tolerates 1 failure (quorum = 2). A 4-member cluster also tolerates only 1 failure (quorum = 3). So 4 members gives no advantage over 3 but adds network overhead. Always use odd numbers.

11.3 Backing Up etcd (CKA Must-Know)

This is the #1 admin task for cluster reliability. If you can back up and restore etcd, you can recover from almost any disaster. The CKA exam will test this.

etcd-backup.sh 
# ╔════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
# ║  MEMORIZE THIS COMMAND — CKA GUARANTEED QUESTION      ║
# ╚════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝

ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot save /opt/etcd-backup.db \
  --endpoints=https://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --cacert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt \
  --cert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt \
  --key=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key

# Expected output:
# {"level":"info","msg":"created temporary snapshot file",
#  "path":"/opt/etcd-backup.db.part"}
# {"level":"info","msg":"fetching snapshot","endpoint":"https://..."}
# Snapshot saved at /opt/etcd-backup.db

# Verify the backup
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot status /opt/etcd-backup.db \
  --write-out=table
# +----------+----------+------------+------------+
# |   HASH   | REVISION | TOTAL KEYS | TOTAL SIZE |
# +----------+----------+------------+------------+
# | fe01cf57 |    10945 |        771 |    2.1 MB  |
# +----------+----------+------------+------------+

etcdctl snapshot save — Flag Breakdown

💾 /opt/etcd-backup.db
Output file path. Use whatever path the exam question specifies.
📡 --endpoints
etcd endpoint URL. Default: https://127.0.0.1:2379 (local etcd).
🔐 --cacert
etcd CA certificate: /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt
📜 --cert
etcd server certificate: /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt
🔑 --key
etcd server key: /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key

🧠 How to Find the Certificate Paths on the Exam

If you forget the cert paths, extract them from the running etcd pod:

find-etcd-certs.sh 
# Method 1: From the etcd pod spec
kubectl -n kube-system describe pod etcd-control-plane | grep -A5 "cert\|key\|ca"
#   --cert-file=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt
#   --key-file=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key
#   --trusted-ca-file=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt
#   --peer-cert-file=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/peer.crt

# Method 2: From the static pod manifest
cat /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml | grep -E "cert|key|ca"

# Method 3: From the API server (etcd client certs)
cat /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml | grep etcd
💡 CKA Tip: Always Set ETCDCTL_API=3

Some etcdctl versions default to API v2. Always prefix with ETCDCTL_API=3 or export it: export ETCDCTL_API=3. API v3 is what Kubernetes uses. If you forget this, the backup command may fail silently.

11.4 Restoring etcd from Backup

Restoring etcd is more complex than backup because you must stop etcd, restore to a new data directory, update the configuration to point to the new directory, and restart etcd.

1
Restore Snapshot

Use etcdctl snapshot restore to create a new data directory from the backup file.

2
Stop etcd

Move the etcd static pod manifest out of /etc/kubernetes/manifests/ to stop it.

3
Update data-dir

Edit the etcd manifest to point --data-dir to the restored directory.

4
Restart etcd

Move the manifest back. kubelet will auto-start etcd with the restored data.

5
Verify

Check that all previous resources are present in the restored cluster.

etcd-restore.sh 
# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
#  STEP 1: Restore the snapshot to a NEW data directory
# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot restore /opt/etcd-backup.db \
  --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restored

# Output:
# {"level":"info","msg":"restoring snapshot",
#  "path":"/opt/etcd-backup.db",
#  "wal-dir":"/var/lib/etcd-restored/member/wal",
#  "data-dir":"/var/lib/etcd-restored",
#  "snap-dir":"/var/lib/etcd-restored/member/snap"}

# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
#  STEP 2: Update the etcd static pod manifest
# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
sudo vi /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml

# Change the --data-dir flag:
#   BEFORE: --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd
#   AFTER:  --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restored

# ALSO update the hostPath volume:
#   BEFORE: path: /var/lib/etcd
#   AFTER:  path: /var/lib/etcd-restored

# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
#  STEP 3: Wait for etcd to restart automatically
# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
# kubelet detects the manifest change and restarts etcd

# Watch for the etcd pod to come back
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -w | grep etcd
# etcd-control-plane   1/1   Running   0   10s

# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
#  STEP 4: Verify the restore
# ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════
kubectl get nodes
kubectl get pods --all-namespaces
kubectl get deployments
# All resources from the backup should be present
etcd Restore Flow — Visual Walkthrough
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  ETCD RESTORE PROCEDURE                                      │
  │                                                              │
  │  /opt/etcd-backup.db                                         │
  │       │                                                      │
  │       ▼                                                      │
  │  etcdctl snapshot restore                                    │
  │       │  --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restored                   │
  │       │                                                      │
  │       ▼                                                      │
  │  /var/lib/etcd-restored/         ← New data directory        │
  │  ├── member/                                                 │
  │  │   ├── snap/                   ← Restored snapshots        │
  │  │   └── wal/                    ← Write-ahead logs          │
  │  │                                                           │
  │  │  Now update etcd manifest:                                │
  │  │                                                           │
  │  │  /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml                      │
  │  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────┐       │
  │  │  │  --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restored    ← Update │       │
  │  │  │                                                │       │
  │  │  │  volumes:                                      │       │
  │  │  │  - hostPath:                                   │       │
  │  │  │      path: /var/lib/etcd-restored     ← Update │       │
  │  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────┘       │
  │  │                                                           │
  │  └── kubelet detects manifest change → restarts etcd pod     │
  │                                                              │
  │  ✅ Cluster restored to the backup point in time              │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
🚨 Critical: Update BOTH data-dir AND hostPath

When restoring, you must update the --data-dir flag in the etcd command AND the hostPath.path in the volume section of the manifest. Missing either one will cause etcd to fail to start or use stale data.

⚠️ Post-Restore: Everything After the Backup is Lost

A restore rolls the cluster back to the point when the backup was taken. Any resources created, updated, or deleted after the backup will be lost (or reverted). This is why regular, scheduled backups are essential.

11.5 etcd Security & Encryption at Rest

etcd must be secured because it holds every secret, token, and credential in the cluster. Security comes from TLS encryption for communication and encryption at rest for stored data.

🔒
TLS Encryption (In Transit)

Default with kubeadm. All communication between API server ↔ etcd and etcd ↔ etcd peers uses mutual TLS. Certificates are in /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/.

🔐
Encryption at Rest

Not enabled by default! Without this, secrets are stored as base64 (NOT encrypted) in etcd. You must configure an EncryptionConfiguration.

/etc/kubernetes/enc/enc.yaml 
# Encryption Configuration — encrypts Secrets at rest in etcd
apiVersion: apiserver.config.k8s.io/v1
kind: EncryptionConfiguration
resources:
  - resources:
      - secrets           # Only encrypt secrets (most common)
    providers:
      - aescbc:           # AES-CBC encryption (recommended)
          keys:
            - name: key1
              secret: c2VjcmV0LWtleS1oZXJlLTMyYnl0ZXM=  # Base64 of 32-byte key
      - identity: {}       # Fallback: read unencrypted (for existing secrets)
enable-encryption.sh 
# Step 1: Create the encryption config file
sudo mkdir -p /etc/kubernetes/enc
sudo vi /etc/kubernetes/enc/enc.yaml   # (paste YAML above)

# Step 2: Add flag to API server manifest
sudo vi /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml
# Add this flag to the command:
#   --encryption-provider-config=/etc/kubernetes/enc/enc.yaml
# Add volume mount for the config file:
#   volumeMounts:
#   - name: enc
#     mountPath: /etc/kubernetes/enc
#     readOnly: true
#   volumes:
#   - name: enc
#     hostPath:
#       path: /etc/kubernetes/enc

# Step 3: Wait for API server to restart
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep apiserver

# Step 4: Re-encrypt ALL existing secrets
kubectl get secrets --all-namespaces -o json | \
  kubectl replace -f -

# Step 5: Verify encryption is working
# Read directly from etcd — should show encrypted data
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl get /registry/secrets/default/my-secret \
  --endpoints=https://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --cacert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt \
  --cert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt \
  --key=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key | hexdump -C
# Should show encrypted binary data, NOT readable text
etcd Security — Defense In Depth
  ┌────────────────────────── etcd Security Layers ──────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                          │
  │  Layer 1: Network Isolation                                              │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐            │
  │  │ etcd should ONLY be accessible from the control plane    │            │
  │  │ Firewall: Allow port 2379 only from API server IP       │            │
  │  │ Firewall: Allow port 2380 only from other etcd peers    │            │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘            │
  │                                                                          │
  │  Layer 2: TLS Encryption (In Transit)                                    │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐            │
  │  │ API Server ──TLS──▶ etcd (client certs: server.crt/key) │            │
  │  │ etcd ──TLS──▶ etcd peers (peer certs: peer.crt/key)     │            │
  │  │ All verified against etcd CA: /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt │      │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘            │
  │                                                                          │
  │  Layer 3: Encryption at Rest                                             │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐            │
  │  │ EncryptionConfiguration encrypts secrets before storage  │            │
  │  │ Provider: aescbc (AES-256-CBC) or secretbox               │            │
  │  │ Without this: secrets stored as base64 (readable!)       │            │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘            │
  │                                                                          │
  │  Layer 4: RBAC + Authentication                                          │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐            │
  │  │ Only API server should have etcd client credentials      │            │
  │  │ No user should access etcd directly (always go via API)  │            │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘            │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
💡 CKA Tip: Encryption at Rest vs TLS

TLS encrypts data in transit (between components). Encryption at rest encrypts data on disk (inside etcd). Both are independent. kubeadm sets up TLS automatically but does NOT enable encryption at rest — you must configure it manually.

🏥 Bonus: etcd Health Check & Monitoring Commands

Knowing how to check etcd's health is essential for cluster troubleshooting. These commands tell you if etcd is running, healthy, and performing well.

etcd-health-check.sh 
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
#  ETCD HEALTH CHECK COMMANDS
# ══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

# Set cert variables (reuse across commands)
export ETCDCTL_API=3
CERTS="--endpoints=https://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --cacert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt \
  --cert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt \
  --key=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key"

# Check cluster health
etcdctl endpoint health $CERTS
# https://127.0.0.1:2379 is healthy: successfully committed
#   proposal: took = 2.345ms

# Check endpoint status (shows leader, DB size, Raft index)
etcdctl endpoint status $CERTS --write-out=table
# +---------------------------+--------+---------+---------+-----------+
# |         ENDPOINT          |   ID   | VERSION | DB SIZE | IS LEADER |
# +---------------------------+--------+---------+---------+-----------+
# | https://127.0.0.1:2379    | abc123 |  3.5.12 |  4.2 MB |   true    |
# +---------------------------+--------+---------+---------+-----------+

# List all etcd cluster members
etcdctl member list $CERTS --write-out=table
# +--------+---------+-------+---------------------------+-----------+
# |   ID   | STATUS  | NAME  |       PEER ADDRS          | IS LEADER |
# +--------+---------+-------+---------------------------+-----------+
# | abc123 | started | etcd0 | https://192.168.56.10:2380|   true    |
# +--------+---------+-------+---------------------------+-----------+

# Check performance (write latency)
etcdctl check perf $CERTS
# 60/60 succeeded, took 1.234s
# PASS: Throughput is 150 writes/s

# Monitor real-time changes (debugging)
etcdctl watch /registry/pods --prefix $CERTS
# (streams all pod changes as they happen)
✅
Healthy etcd Indicators

• endpoint health says "is healthy"
• DB size under 8 GB
• Raft term stable (not incrementing)
• All members show "started"
• Write latency under 10ms
• Leader is elected (one IS LEADER = true)

🚨
Unhealthy etcd Red Flags

• endpoint health timeout/errors
• DB size over 8 GB (needs compaction)
• "no leader" in member list
• High Raft term (frequent elections)
• "mvcc: database space exceeded"
• Members showing "unstarted"

🧹 Bonus: etcd Maintenance — Compaction & Defragmentation

etcd stores all historical revisions of keys. Over time, this grows the database. Compaction removes old revisions, and defragmentation reclaims disk space.

etcd-maintenance.sh 
# Get current revision number
REV=$(etcdctl endpoint status $CERTS --write-out="json" | \
  python3 -c "import sys,json; print(json.load(sys.stdin)[0]['Status']['header']['revision'])")

# Compact old revisions (keep only latest)
etcdctl compact $REV $CERTS
# compacted revision 12345

# Defragment (reclaim disk space after compaction)
etcdctl defrag $CERTS
# Finished defragmenting etcd member[https://127.0.0.1:2379]

# Check new DB size (should be smaller)
etcdctl endpoint status $CERTS --write-out=table
⚠️ Compaction is Irreversible

Once you compact, old revisions are permanently deleted. This means watchers using old revisions will get an error. Always take an etcd backup before compacting in production.

🎯 CKA etcd Quick Reference Card

Print this or memorize it. These are the exact commands and paths you need for the CKA exam.

Item Path / Value Why You Need It
etcd CA cert /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt --cacert flag for all etcdctl commands
etcd server cert /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt --cert flag for all etcdctl commands
etcd server key /etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key --key flag for all etcdctl commands
Client port 2379 --endpoints HTTPS URL
Peer port 2380 etcd ↔ etcd communication
Data directory /var/lib/etcd --data-dir for restore
Static pod manifest /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml Edit to change --data-dir after restore
API version env ETCDCTL_API=3 Always set this before etcdctl commands
CKA etcd — Complete Backup & Restore Cheat Sheet
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │   BACKUP:                                                          │
  │   ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot save /opt/backup.db \             │
  │     --endpoints=https://127.0.0.1:2379 \                           │
  │     --cacert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt \                     │
  │     --cert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt \                   │
  │     --key=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key                      │
  │                                                                     │
  │   VERIFY BACKUP:                                                    │
  │   ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot status /opt/backup.db -w table    │
  │                                                                     │
  ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │   RESTORE:                                                          │
  │   ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot restore /opt/backup.db \          │
  │     --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restored                              │
  │                                                                     │
  │   THEN EDIT: /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml                   │
  │     1. --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restored                           │
  │     2. volumes.hostPath.path=/var/lib/etcd-restored                │
  │                                                                     │
  │   WAIT: kubelet auto-restarts etcd pod                             │
  │   VERIFY: kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep etcd              │
  ├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │   FIND CERT PATHS (if you forget):                                 │
  │   kubectl -n kube-system describe pod etcd-<node> | grep cert     │
  │   cat /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml | grep -E "cert|key|ca"  │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
💡 CKA Exam Strategy: etcd Questions

Step 1: Read the question carefully — does it ask for backup, restore, or both?
Step 2: If you forget cert paths, run: kubectl describe pod etcd-controlplane -n kube-system
Step 3: After restore, always verify with kubectl get nodes and kubectl get pods -A
Step 4: Don't panic if the API server takes 30-60 seconds to restart after a restore.
