🟡 Part 4: Workloads & Scheduling Chapters 13-16

Chapter 13: Deployments, ReplicaSets, and Scaling

Deployments are the heart of Kubernetes workload management. In this chapter you'll master the complete lifecycle — creating, scaling, updating, and rolling back applications. We'll trace how a Deployment creates a ReplicaSet, which in turn creates Pods, and how the Horizontal Pod Autoscaler (HPA) adds elastic scaling. Every example uses our anihpj/jobpost Django app so you see real-world patterns, not toy demos.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~1.5 hours 🚀 Workloads 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Create Deployments imperatively and declaratively — know the full YAML anatomy
  • ✅ Perform rolling updates and instant rollbacks using kubectl rollout
  • ✅ Understand how Deployments → ReplicaSets → Pods chain together under the hood
  • ✅ Scale manually with kubectl scale and automatically with HPA
  • ✅ Choose the right deployment strategy — RollingUpdate vs Recreate
  • ✅ Control update pace with maxSurge and maxUnavailable
🎓 CKA Relevance
Workloads & Scheduling 15% Troubleshooting 30%
Deployments Are CKA Exam Bread & Butter

Nearly every CKA exam has at least 2-3 tasks involving Deployments. You must be fast at:

  • kubectl create deployment NAME --image=IMG --replicas=N — imperative creation
  • kubectl set image deployment/NAME container=IMG:TAG — trigger rolling update
  • kubectl rollout undo deployment/NAME --to-revision=N — rollback
  • kubectl scale deployment/NAME --replicas=N — manual scaling
  • kubectl rollout status deployment/NAME — verify update progress

13.1 Deployments — Complete Lifecycle

A Deployment is Kubernetes' recommended way to manage stateless applications. It provides declarative updates, self-healing, scaling, and version history — all in one resource.

📖 Key Concept: Declarative vs Imperative

Kubernetes supports both styles. Imperative: kubectl create deployment anihpj --image=anihpj:v1. Declarative: kubectl apply -f deployment.yaml. On the CKA, use imperative commands to generate YAML fast, then edit if needed.

Deployment
🎛️ Manages ReplicaSets
📦 Creates Pods via RS
🔄 Rolling Updates
⏪ Rollback History
📈 Scale In/Out
✅ Self-Healing

a) Creating a Deployment

Imperative — Fast Exam Creation 
# Create deployment (CKA exam speed)
kubectl create deployment anihpj --image=anihpj:v1 --replicas=3

# Generate YAML without creating (save to file, then edit)
kubectl create deployment anihpj --image=anihpj:v1 --replicas=3 \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > anihpj-deploy.yaml

# Verify
kubectl get deployments
kubectl describe deployment anihpj
anihpj-deployment.yaml 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
  labels:
    app: anihpj
    project: jobpost
spec:
  replicas: 3                    # Desired number of pods
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj                # Must match template labels
  strategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxSurge: 1                # 1 extra pod during update
      maxUnavailable: 0          # Never fewer than 3 running
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj              # Must match selector
        version: v1
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
        ports:
        - containerPort: 8000
        resources:
          requests:
            cpu: "250m"
            memory: "256Mi"
          limits:
            cpu: "500m"
            memory: "512Mi"
        readinessProbe:
          httpGet:
            path: /health
            port: 8000
          initialDelaySeconds: 5
          periodSeconds: 10

Deployment YAML Anatomy

🔢 replicas
Desired pod count — the controller constantly ensures this number is running
🏷️ selector.matchLabels
How the Deployment finds its pods — must match template labels exactly
🔄 strategy
RollingUpdate (default) or Recreate — controls how pods are replaced
📦 template
The Pod spec — this is a "Pod blueprint" stamped out for each replica
📈 maxSurge
How many extra pods beyond replicas are allowed during an update (number or %)
📉 maxUnavailable
How many pods can be unavailable during an update (number or %). 0 = zero downtime.

b) Scaling

Scaling changes the spec.replicas field. The Deployment's ReplicaSet controller immediately creates or deletes pods to match.

Manual Scaling Commands 
# Scale up — handle more traffic
kubectl scale deployment anihpj --replicas=5

# Scale down — save resources
kubectl scale deployment anihpj --replicas=2

# Verify scaling
kubectl get deployment anihpj
# NAME      READY   UP-TO-DATE   AVAILABLE   AGE
# anihpj   5/5     5            5           10m

# Watch pods appear/disappear in real-time
kubectl get pods -w -l app=anihpj
Manual Scaling — Pods Appear Instantly
  kubectl scale deployment anihpj --replicas=5

  BEFORE (3 replicas):             AFTER (5 replicas):
  ┌────────┐ ┌────────┐ ┌────────┐    ┌────────┐ ┌────────┐ ┌────────┐ ┌────────┐ ┌────────┐
  │anihpj-1│ │anihpj-2│ │anihpj-3│    │anihpj-1│ │anihpj-2│ │anihpj-3│ │anihpj-4│ │anihpj-5│
  │  📦 v1 │ │  📦 v1 │ │  📦 v1 │    │  📦 v1 │ │  📦 v1 │ │  📦 v1 │ │ 🆕 v1  │ │ 🆕 v1  │
  └────────┘ └────────┘ └────────┘    └────────┘ └────────┘ └────────┘ └────────┘ └────────┘
                                                                          ↑ NEW       ↑ NEW

c) Rolling Updates

Rolling updates replace pods one at a time, ensuring your application stays available throughout the upgrade. This is the default strategy and is heavily tested on the CKA.

Triggering a Rolling Update 
# Update image — triggers rolling update
kubectl set image deployment/anihpj django=anihpj:v2

# Watch rollout progress (CKA exam: verify your work!)
kubectl rollout status deployment/anihpj
# Waiting for deployment "anihpj" rollout to finish:
#   1 out of 3 new replicas have been updated...
#   2 out of 3 new replicas have been updated...
#   deployment "anihpj" successfully rolled out

# Check rollout history
kubectl rollout history deployment/anihpj
# REVISION  CHANGE-CAUSE
# 1         <none>
# 2         <none>

# Add change cause for audit trail
kubectl annotate deployment/anihpj \
  kubernetes.io/change-cause="Update to v2 — new job search feature"
Rolling Update: anihpj v1 → v2 (Zero Downtime)
  maxSurge: 1       (1 extra pod allowed during update)
  maxUnavailable: 0 (never fewer than 3 running)

  Time 0:  [📦v1] [📦v1] [📦v1]              ← All running v1
           ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Time 1:  [📦v1] [📦v1] [📦v1] [🆕v2]       ← v2 pod starting (surge=1)
           ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Time 2:  [📦v1] [📦v1] [🗑️  ] [✅v2]       ← v2 ready → old v1 terminating
           ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Time 3:  [📦v1] [📦v1] [✅v2] [🆕v2]       ← Next v2 pod starting
           ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Time 4:  [📦v1] [🗑️  ] [✅v2] [✅v2]       ← Second v1 terminating
           ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Time 5:  [📦v1] [✅v2] [✅v2] [🆕v2]       ← Last v2 starting
           ────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Time 6:  [✅v2] [✅v2] [✅v2]              ← All v2 ✅ Zero downtime! 🎉

  Key insight: At NO point were there fewer than 3 healthy pods serving traffic!

d) Rollbacks

If v2 is broken, you can instantly rollback. Kubernetes keeps a revision history of ReplicaSets.

⚠️ CKA Exam Alert: Rollback Commands

Rollback questions are extremely common. Know these cold:

  • kubectl rollout undo deployment/NAME — go back one revision
  • kubectl rollout undo deployment/NAME --to-revision=N — go to specific revision
  • kubectl rollout history deployment/NAME — see all revisions
Rollback Commands 
# Undo last update (go back to previous revision)
kubectl rollout undo deployment/anihpj

# Go to a specific revision
kubectl rollout undo deployment/anihpj --to-revision=2

# Check what happened
kubectl rollout history deployment/anihpj --revision=2

# Verify rollback succeeded
kubectl rollout status deployment/anihpj
kubectl get pods -l app=anihpj -o jsonpath='{.items[*].spec.containers[*].image}'
# anihpj:v1 anihpj:v1 anihpj:v1  ← back to v1!
Rollback Flow: v2 Broken → Instant Recovery to v1
  Deployment: anihpj
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                          │
  │  Revision 1 (v1) ─── Revision 2 (v2) ─── Revision 3    │
  │  ┌──────────┐        ┌──────────┐        ┌──────────┐   │
  │  │ReplicaSet│        │ReplicaSet│        │ReplicaSet│   │
  │  │ (scaled  │  ──→   │ (scaled  │  ──→   │ = rev 1  │   │
  │  │  to 0)   │        │  to 0)   │  undo  │ (active) │   │
  │  └──────────┘        └──────────┘        └──────────┘   │
  │                        ↑ 💀 broken          ↑ ✅ fixed  │
  │                                                          │
  │  kubectl rollout undo deployment/anihpj                  │
  │  → K8s scales old RS back up, scales broken RS down     │
  │  → Pods running v1 again within seconds! 🎉             │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
1
Create

kubectl create deployment or kubectl apply -f

2
Verify

kubectl get deploy,rs,pods

3
Scale

kubectl scale deploy --replicas=N

4
Update

kubectl set image deploy/NAME

5
Monitor

kubectl rollout status

6
Rollback

kubectl rollout undo if needed

e) Pause & Resume — Batch Multiple Changes

Sometimes you need to make multiple changes (image + resources + env vars) in one rollout instead of triggering separate updates for each. Pause the deployment, make all changes, then resume to trigger a single rollout.

Pause & Resume Workflow 
# Pause — no changes will trigger a rollout
kubectl rollout pause deployment/anihpj

# Make multiple changes (none trigger rollout yet)
kubectl set image deployment/anihpj django=anihpj:v3
kubectl set resources deployment/anihpj -c django \
  --requests=cpu=500m,memory=512Mi
kubectl set env deployment/anihpj DEBUG=false

# Resume — all changes deploy in ONE rollout
kubectl rollout resume deployment/anihpj

# Verify single rollout applied all changes
kubectl rollout status deployment/anihpj
Pause/Resume vs Normal — Rollout Count
  WITHOUT Pause/Resume:              WITH Pause/Resume:

  Change image → Rollout #1  🔄      kubectl rollout pause
  Change CPU   → Rollout #2  🔄      Change image  ┐
  Change env   → Rollout #3  🔄      Change CPU    ├─ no rollouts
                                      Change env    ┘
  3 separate rollouts!               kubectl rollout resume
  3 ReplicaSets created!              → Rollout #1 only  🔄
  ⚠️ Wasteful & slower               ✅ Single clean rollout!

13.2 ReplicaSets — Under the Hood

A ReplicaSet ensures a specified number of pod replicas are running at any time. You rarely create ReplicaSets directly — Deployments create and manage them for you. But understanding them is essential for troubleshooting.

💡 Why You Need to Know This

When kubectl get pods shows a pod stuck in ImagePullBackOff, you need to trace: Pod → ReplicaSet → Deployment to find the root cause. The CKA tests this troubleshooting chain.

The Deployment → ReplicaSet → Pod Chain
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  DEPLOYMENT: anihpj                                              │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │ You manage this layer                                       │ │
  │  │ "I want 3 pods running anihpj:v1"                          │ │
  │  └──────────────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────┘ │
  │                             │ creates & manages                 │
  │                             ▼                                    │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │  REPLICASET: anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d                           │   │
  │  │  Kubernetes manages this layer automatically              │   │
  │  │  selector: matchLabels: { app: anihpj }                  │   │
  │  │  replicas: 3                                              │   │
  │  └───────┬──────────────────┬──────────────────┬────────────┘   │
  │          │ owns             │ owns             │ owns           │
  │          ▼                  ▼                  ▼                │
  │  ┌──────────────┐  ┌──────────────┐  ┌──────────────┐          │
  │  │ POD          │  │ POD          │  │ POD          │          │
  │  │ anihpj-..abc │  │ anihpj-..def │  │ anihpj-..ghi │          │
  │  │ 📦 anihpj:v1│  │ 📦 anihpj:v1│  │ 📦 anihpj:v1│          │
  │  │ labels:      │  │ labels:      │  │ labels:      │          │
  │  │  app: anihpj │  │  app: anihpj │  │  app: anihpj │          │
  │  └──────────────┘  └──────────────┘  └──────────────┘          │
  │                                                                  │
  │  Delete a pod? → ReplicaSet auto-creates a replacement! 🔄     │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
replicaset-example.yaml (for understanding — rarely created directly) 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: ReplicaSet
metadata:
  name: anihpj-replicaset
  labels:
    app: anihpj
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:                 # Which pods this RS owns
      app: anihpj
    # Advanced: matchExpressions
    # matchExpressions:
    #   - key: app
    #     operator: In           # In, NotIn, Exists, DoesNotExist
    #     values: [anihpj, anihpj-staging]
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
        ports:
        - containerPort: 8000
🏷️
matchLabels

Simple equality: app: anihpj. All labels must match. Most common approach.

🔍
matchExpressions

Operators: In, NotIn, Exists, DoesNotExist. For complex multi-value selectors.

Inspecting the Chain 
# See all three layers at once
kubectl get deploy,rs,pods -l app=anihpj

# NAME                     READY   UP-TO-DATE   AVAILABLE   AGE
# deployment.apps/anihpj   3/3     3            3           5m
#
# NAME                                DESIRED   CURRENT   READY   AGE
# replicaset.apps/anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d  3         3         3       5m
#
# NAME                          READY   STATUS    RESTARTS   AGE
# pod/anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d-abc12  1/1     Running   0          5m
# pod/anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d-def34  1/1     Running   0          5m
# pod/anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d-ghi56  1/1     Running   0          5m

# See owner references (pod → RS → Deployment)
kubectl get pod anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d-abc12 -o jsonpath='{.metadata.ownerReferences[0].name}'
# anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d  ← owned by the ReplicaSet
⚠️ Never Delete ReplicaSets Directly

If you delete a ReplicaSet that belongs to a Deployment, the Deployment controller will immediately recreate it. To scale down, always modify the Deployment. Delete the Deployment to clean up all RS and Pods together.

💡 revisionHistoryLimit — Control Revision History

By default, Kubernetes keeps 10 old ReplicaSets (revision history) for rollback. Each old RS consumes etcd storage. You can control this with spec.revisionHistoryLimit:

  • revisionHistoryLimit: 5 — keep last 5 revisions (good for production)
  • revisionHistoryLimit: 0 — keep none (⚠️ no rollback possible!)
  • revisionHistoryLimit: 10 — default

13.3 Horizontal Pod Autoscaler (HPA)

The HPA automatically scales your Deployment based on observed CPU, memory, or custom metrics. When traffic spikes, HPA adds pods. When traffic drops, it removes them. This is critical for production and tested on the CKA.

📋 Prerequisite: Metrics Server

HPA requires the Metrics Server to be installed in the cluster. Without it, HPA has no data to make scaling decisions. Verify: kubectl top nodes — if this works, Metrics Server is running.

HPA — Imperative (CKA Exam Speed) 
# Create HPA bound to deployment
kubectl autoscale deployment anihpj --min=3 --max=10 --cpu-percent=70

# Check HPA status
kubectl get hpa
# NAME     REFERENCE           TARGETS   MINPODS   MAXPODS   REPLICAS   AGE
# anihpj  Deployment/anihpj   45%/70%   3         10        3          2m

# Detailed view
kubectl describe hpa anihpj
anihpj-hpa.yaml 
apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
metadata:
  name: anihpj-hpa
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    apiVersion: apps/v1
    kind: Deployment
    name: anihpj
  minReplicas: 3
  maxReplicas: 10
  metrics:
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: cpu
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: 70    # Scale when avg CPU > 70%
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: memory
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: 80    # Also watch memory
  behavior:
    scaleUp:
      stabilizationWindowSeconds: 30   # React quickly to spikes
    scaleDown:
      stabilizationWindowSeconds: 300  # Wait 5 min before scaling down
HPA Scaling Flow — Automatic Response to Load
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  HPA: Horizontal Pod Autoscaler for anihpj                      │
  │                                                                  │
  │  📊 Metrics Server reports: CPU = 85%  (above target of 70%)   │
  │       │                                                          │
  │       ▼                                                          │
  │  🤖 HPA evaluates every 15 seconds:                             │
  │     desiredReplicas = ceil(currentReplicas × (85% / 70%))       │
  │     desiredReplicas = ceil(3 × 1.21) = 4                        │
  │       │                                                          │
  │       ▼                                                          │
  │  📈 HPA updates: deployment.spec.replicas = 4                   │
  │       │                                                          │
  │       ▼                                                          │
  │  📦📦📦📦  4 pods running → CPU drops to 60%                    │
  │                                                                  │
  │  ... traffic keeps climbing ...                                  │
  │                                                                  │
  │  📊 CPU = 90%  →  HPA: replicas = 6                             │
  │  📦📦📦📦📦📦  6 pods running → CPU stabilizes at 55%           │
  │                                                                  │
  │  ... traffic decreases (night time) ...                          │
  │                                                                  │
  │  📊 CPU = 25%  →  HPA waits 5 min (stabilization window)       │
  │               →  then scales down to minReplicas = 3            │
  │  📦📦📦  3 pods — cost efficient! 💰                             │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
⬆️
Scale Up (Fast)

Default stabilization: 0 seconds. HPA reacts immediately to load spikes to protect users.

⬇️
Scale Down (Slow)

Default stabilization: 300 seconds. Avoids flapping — waits 5 min to confirm load is truly gone.

💡 CKA Tip: HPA Requires Resource Requests

HPA can only measure utilization as a percentage if resources.requests is set on the container. Without requests, HPA cannot calculate CPU percentage and will report <unknown>/70%. Always set resource requests on Deployments that use HPA.

13.4 Deployment Strategies

Kubernetes supports two built-in deployment strategies. Choosing the right one depends on your application's ability to handle multiple versions running simultaneously.

Strategy How It Works Downtime When to Use anihpj Example
RollingUpdate Replace pods gradually, one (or few) at a time ❌ None Default — use for most updates New Django feature release
Recreate Kill ALL old pods first, then start ALL new pods ✅ Yes — brief outage Breaking changes, DB schema migrations, incompatible API versions Major Django version upgrade with DB migration
RollingUpdate vs Recreate — Visual Comparison
  ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
  ROLLINGUPDATE STRATEGY (default)          RECREATE STRATEGY
  ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

  T0: [v1] [v1] [v1]                       [v1] [v1] [v1]
       │                                         │
  T1: [v1] [v1] [v1] [v2] ← surge              [💀] [💀] [💀] ← all killed
       │                                         │
  T2: [v1] [v1] [v2]      ← replace 1           [  ] [  ] [  ] ← downtime! ⚠️
       │                                         │
  T3: [v1] [v2] [v2]      ← replace 2           [v2] [v2] [v2] ← all new
       │
  T4: [v2] [v2] [v2]      ← complete ✅

  Availability:                             Availability:
  ██████████████████ 100%                   ████░░░░████ ~70%
  Never less than minAvailable              Brief outage window

  ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

RollingUpdate — Tuning Parameters

strategy-examples.yaml 
# Conservative — zero downtime, slow rollout
strategy:
  type: RollingUpdate
  rollingUpdate:
    maxSurge: 1          # Only 1 extra pod at a time
    maxUnavailable: 0    # Never fewer than desired replicas

# Aggressive — faster rollout, allows brief capacity dip
strategy:
  type: RollingUpdate
  rollingUpdate:
    maxSurge: "50%"      # Up to 50% extra pods
    maxUnavailable: "25%" # Allow 25% to be unavailable

# Recreate — kill all, then create all
strategy:
  type: Recreate         # No rollingUpdate params needed
RollingUpdate — Safety ★★★★★
RollingUpdate — Speed ★★★☆☆
Recreate — Safety ★★☆☆☆
Recreate — Speed ★★★★★
💡 CKA Exam Strategy

On the exam, the default RollingUpdate is almost always what you want. Only use Recreate if the question explicitly says "all pods must be running the same version" or mentions database migrations that can't handle mixed versions.

📖 Beyond Built-in: Blue-Green & Canary Deployments

Kubernetes only has RollingUpdate and Recreate built in. Two other common strategies require manual setup or tools like Argo Rollouts / Istio:

  • Blue-Green — Run two full Deployments (blue=current, green=new). Switch the Service selector from blue to green once verified. Instant rollback by switching back.
  • Canary — Deploy new version alongside old with fewer replicas (e.g., 1 of 10). Route a small percentage of traffic to canary. If healthy, gradually increase. Popular with service meshes.
Blue-Green vs Canary — Not Built-in but Important to Know
  BLUE-GREEN:                           CANARY:

  ┌─────── Service ──────┐              ┌─────── Service ──────┐
  │  selector: ver=blue  │              │  selector: app=web   │
  └──────────┬───────────┘              └──────────┬───────────┘
             │                                     │
    ┌────────┴────────┐                   ┌────────┴────────┐
    ▼                 ▼                   ▼                 ▼
  Blue Deploy      Green Deploy        v1 Deploy (9)    v2 Deploy (1)
  (current ✅)     (new, testing)      (90% traffic)    (10% canary)
  [v1][v1][v1]     [v2][v2][v2]        [v1]×9           [v2]×1

  Switch: change   Promote: scale      Promote: scale v2 up,
  selector to      green, delete       scale v1 down gradually
  ver=green        blue

🔑 Chapter 13 Quick Reference

Action Command

Scalekubectl scale deployment NAME --replicas=N

Chapter 14: Scheduling and Node Management

The Kubernetes scheduler decides where every pod runs. As a CKA administrator, you must master how to influence scheduling decisions — from simple node selectors to complex affinity rules, taints, tolerations, and node maintenance procedures. This chapter is one of the most heavily tested topics on the CKA exam.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~2 hours 📍 Scheduling 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ How the Kubernetes scheduler makes placement decisions (filtering → scoring → binding)
  • ✅ Use nodeSelector for simple node placement
  • ✅ Configure node affinity (required vs preferred) for flexible scheduling
  • ✅ Use pod affinity/anti-affinity for co-location and spreading
  • ✅ Apply taints and tolerations to control which pods can run on which nodes
  • ✅ Perform safe node maintenance with cordon, drain, uncordon
  • ✅ Spread pods evenly with topology spread constraints
  • ✅ Use PriorityClasses for pod preemption
🎓 CKA Relevance
Workloads & Scheduling 15% Cluster Architecture 25%
Scheduling Is a CKA Exam Favourite

Expect at least 2-3 scheduling questions. The most common patterns:

  • Taints & Tolerations — taint a node, create a pod with/without toleration, verify placement
  • Node Affinity — label a node, create a pod with nodeAffinity targeting that label
  • Node Maintenance — cordon → drain → perform work → uncordon
  • Static Pods — create a manifest in /etc/kubernetes/manifests/

14.1 How the Scheduler Works

The kube-scheduler runs as a control plane component. When a new pod is created without a nodeName, it enters the scheduling queue. The scheduler then executes a multi-phase pipeline to find the best node.

1
Filtering

Which nodes CAN run this pod? Checks: sufficient resources, node taints, node selectors, affinity rules, port availability.

2
Scoring

Which passing node is the BEST fit? Scores based on: resource balance, affinity preference, spread, image locality.

3
Binding

The highest-scoring node wins. Scheduler sets pod.spec.nodeName and the kubelet on that node starts the container.

Scheduler Decision Flowchart — anihpj Pod Placement
  NEW POD: anihpj (requests: 256Mi memory, 250m CPU)
       │
       ▼
  ════════════════ PHASE 1: FILTERING ════════════════
  "Which nodes CAN run this pod?"

  Node 1 (512Mi free, 500m CPU free)   → ✅ PASSES — has enough resources
  Node 2 (128Mi free, 100m CPU free)   → ❌ FAILS  — insufficient memory
  Node 3 (1Gi free, 1000m CPU free)    → ✅ PASSES — has enough resources
  Node 4 (taint: gpu=true:NoSchedule)  → ❌ FAILS  — no matching toleration
       │
       ▼ (only passing nodes continue)
  ════════════════ PHASE 2: SCORING ═════════════════
  "Which passing node is the BEST fit?"

  Node 1: Score 65  — good fit, resources balanced
  Node 3: Score 82  — better fit, more headroom,
                      topology spread bonus, affinity match
       │
       ▼
  ════════════════ PHASE 3: BINDING ═════════════════

  🏆 Winner: Node 3 (highest score: 82)
  → pod.spec.nodeName = "node-3"
  → kubelet on Node 3 pulls image → starts container
  → Pod status: Running ✅
🎯
Resource Fit

Does the node have enough CPU & memory to satisfy the pod's requests?

🏷️
Label Match

Does the node have the labels required by nodeSelector or nodeAffinity?

🚫
Taint Check

Does the pod tolerate the node's taints? No toleration = rejected.

📊
Spread Score

How well does this placement spread pods across nodes/zones?

Static Pods — Bypass the Scheduler

Static pods are managed directly by the kubelet on a specific node, not by the API server. They're used for control plane components (etcd, apiserver, scheduler itself). The CKA may ask you to create one.

Creating a Static Pod 
# Static pods live in the kubelet's manifest directory
# Default: /etc/kubernetes/manifests/

# Find the staticPodPath on any node
ssh worker-1 "cat /var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml | grep staticPodPath"
# staticPodPath: /etc/kubernetes/manifests

# Create a static pod (just place a YAML file there)
ssh worker-1 "cat > /etc/kubernetes/manifests/static-nginx.yaml" <<'EOF'
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: static-nginx
spec:
  containers:
  - name: nginx
    image: nginx:1.25
    ports:
    - containerPort: 80
EOF

# kubelet auto-starts it! Check from control plane:
kubectl get pods
# NAME                       READY   STATUS    AGE
# static-nginx-worker-1      1/1     Running   10s
#                  ^^^^^^^^^ node name appended!

# Delete: remove the file from /etc/kubernetes/manifests/
# kubectl delete won't work — kubelet will recreate it!
⚠️ CKA Exam: Static Pod Key Facts
  • Static pod names always end with -<nodename>
  • They can only be created/deleted by adding/removing YAML from the manifest directory
  • kubectl delete on a static pod will NOT permanently remove it — kubelet recreates it
  • Control plane components (apiserver, etcd, scheduler) are static pods on the control plane node

14.2 Node Selectors — Simple Placement

The simplest way to constrain a pod to specific nodes. Label a node, then reference that label in the pod spec. It's a hard requirement — the pod won't schedule if no node matches.

Label a Node, Then Select It 
# Step 1: Label the node
kubectl label node worker-1 disk=ssd

# Verify label was applied
kubectl get node worker-1 --show-labels | grep disk

# Step 2: Create pod with nodeSelector (see YAML below)
kubectl apply -f anihpj-ssd-pod.yaml

# Verify pod landed on the right node
kubectl get pod anihpj-ssd -o wide
# NAME         READY   STATUS    NODE       
# anihpj-ssd   1/1     Running   worker-1   ← correct!

# Clean up: remove label
kubectl label node worker-1 disk-
anihpj-ssd-pod.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj-ssd
spec:
  nodeSelector:
    disk: ssd                    # Only schedule on nodes with disk=ssd
  containers:
  - name: django
    image: anihpj:v1
    ports:
    - containerPort: 8000
💡 nodeSelector vs nodeAffinity

nodeSelector is simple but limited — it only supports exact equality matching. For "prefer but don't require" or "match any of these values" logic, use Node Affinity (see 14.3).

14.3 Node Affinity — Advanced Placement

Node affinity is the more powerful version of nodeSelector. It supports operators (In, NotIn, Exists, Gt, Lt) and offers two types: hard rules (required) and soft rules (preferred).

Type Behavior When Pod Can't Match Use Case
requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution Hard rule — MUST match Pod stays Pending forever GPU workloads, compliance zones
preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution Soft rule — TRY to match Scheduled anywhere available Prefer SSD, prefer same zone
anihpj-node-affinity.yaml 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj
    spec:
      affinity:
        nodeAffinity:
          # HARD: Must be on an SSD node
          requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
            nodeSelectorTerms:
            - matchExpressions:
              - key: disk
                operator: In
                values:
                - ssd
                - nvme
          # SOFT: Prefer us-east zone (but ok if not available)
          preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
          - weight: 80           # 1-100, higher = stronger preference
            preference:
              matchExpressions:
              - key: topology.kubernetes.io/zone
                operator: In
                values:
                - us-east-1a
                - us-east-1b
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
Node Affinity Decision Matrix
  Pod: anihpj (requires: disk ∈ {ssd, nvme}, prefers: zone ∈ {us-east-1a})

  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  Node          disk    zone          Required  Preferred   │
  │  ──────────    ────    ──────────    ────────  ─────────   │
  │  worker-1      ssd     us-east-1a   ✅ PASS   ✅ Match    │ ← Best (score 82)
  │  worker-2      ssd     us-west-1a   ✅ PASS   ❌ No match │ ← OK (score 65)
  │  worker-3      hdd     us-east-1a   ❌ FAIL   —           │ ← Rejected
  │  worker-4      nvme    us-east-1b   ✅ PASS   ✅ Match    │ ← Good (score 78)
  │  worker-5      —       us-east-1a   ❌ FAIL   —           │ ← Rejected
  │                                                             │
  │  Result: Pod scheduled on worker-1 (highest combined score)│
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
⚠️ "IgnoredDuringExecution" — What It Means

If you remove a node's label after a pod is already scheduled there, the pod keeps running. The affinity rule is only checked at scheduling time, not continuously. A future Kubernetes version may add RequiredDuringExecution to also evict running pods.

14.4 Pod Affinity and Anti-Affinity

While Node Affinity places pods relative to nodes, Pod Affinity/Anti-Affinity places pods relative to other pods. This is essential for performance (co-locate) and high availability (spread out).

🤝
Pod Affinity

"Place me near this pod." Put anihpj Django on the same node/zone as its Redis cache for low latency.

↔️
Pod Anti-Affinity

"Keep me away from that pod." Spread anihpj replicas across different nodes so one node failure doesn't take all of them down.

anihpj-pod-antiaffinity.yaml — HA Deployment 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj
    spec:
      affinity:
        # Anti-Affinity: Spread replicas across nodes
        podAntiAffinity:
          requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
          - labelSelector:
              matchExpressions:
              - key: app
                operator: In
                values:
                - anihpj
            topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname  # "different nodes"

        # Affinity: Place near Redis cache
        podAffinity:
          preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
          - weight: 100
            podAffinityTerm:
              labelSelector:
                matchExpressions:
                - key: app
                  operator: In
                  values:
                  - anihpj-redis
              topologyKey: topology.kubernetes.io/zone  # "same zone"
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
Pod Anti-Affinity — anihpj Replicas Spread Across Nodes
  Deployment: anihpj (replicas: 3)
  podAntiAffinity: kubernetes.io/hostname (no 2 on same node)

  ┌────────────────┐  ┌────────────────┐  ┌────────────────┐
  │   worker-1     │  │   worker-2     │  │   worker-3     │
  │ ┌────────────┐ │  │ ┌────────────┐ │  │ ┌────────────┐ │
  │ │ anihpj-abc │ │  │ │ anihpj-def │ │  │ │ anihpj-ghi │ │
  │ │   📦 v1    │ │  │ │   📦 v1    │ │  │ │   📦 v1    │ │
  │ └────────────┘ │  │ └────────────┘ │  │ └────────────┘ │
  │                │  │                │  │                │
  │ ┌────────────┐ │  │                │  │                │
  │ │ redis-xyz  │ │  │                │  │                │
  │ │   🔴 cache │ │  │                │  │                │
  │ └────────────┘ │  │                │  │                │
  └────────────────┘  └────────────────┘  └────────────────┘

  ✅ Each node has exactly 1 anihpj pod — survives any single node failure!
  ✅ anihpj-abc co-located with redis (podAffinity) for low latency

14.5 Taints and Tolerations

Taints are the opposite of affinity — taints repel pods from a node, while tolerations let specific pods override that repulsion. This is a CKA must-know concept and appears on almost every exam.

📖 The Analogy

Think of a node's taint as a "No Entry" sign. A toleration is the "VIP pass" that lets a pod in despite the sign. Without the pass, the pod is rejected.

Effect Behavior Running Pods Use Case
NoSchedule New pods WITHOUT tolerance are rejected Existing pods stay Dedicated GPU nodes, team isolation
PreferNoSchedule Try NOT to schedule here, but allow if no other option Existing pods stay Soft preference — avoid but don't block
NoExecute Reject new pods AND evict running pods without tolerance Evicted! Node going into maintenance, toxic node
Taint & Toleration Commands 
# Add a taint to a node
kubectl taint node worker-1 gpu=true:NoSchedule

# Verify taint
kubectl describe node worker-1 | grep -A5 Taints
# Taints:  gpu=true:NoSchedule

# Remove a taint (add dash at the end)
kubectl taint node worker-1 gpu=true:NoSchedule-

# See control plane taints (this is why your pods don't run on master!)
kubectl describe node control-plane | grep Taints
# Taints:  node-role.kubernetes.io/control-plane:NoSchedule
pod-with-toleration.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: ml-training-job
spec:
  tolerations:
  - key: "gpu"
    operator: "Equal"        # Match exact value
    value: "true"
    effect: "NoSchedule"
  # Alternative: tolerate ANY value for the key
  # - key: "gpu"
  #   operator: "Exists"    # Don't care about value
  #   effect: "NoSchedule"
  containers:
  - name: trainer
    image: ml-trainer:v1
    resources:
      limits:
        nvidia.com/gpu: 1
Taints & Tolerations — Access Control
  ┌────────────────── Node: gpu-node ──────────────────┐
  │ Taint: gpu=true:NoSchedule   🚫                    │
  │                                                     │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │  anihpj pod (no toleration)                  │   │
  │  │  → ❌ REJECTED — "You don't have a VIP pass" │   │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                                                     │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │  ml-job pod (toleration: gpu=true)           │   │
  │  │  → ✅ SCHEDULED — "VIP pass accepted!"      │   │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ┌────────────── Control Plane Node ──────────────────┐
  │ Taint: node-role.kubernetes.io/control-plane:      │
  │        NoSchedule                                   │
  │                                                     │
  │  That's why your pods DON'T run on control plane!  │
  │  Only system pods (with tolerations) run here:     │
  │  ├── kube-apiserver                                 │
  │  ├── kube-scheduler                                 │
  │  ├── kube-controller-manager                        │
  │  └── etcd                                           │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
⚠️ CKA Trap: Taint Removal Syntax

To remove a taint, append a - (minus sign) at the end. Many candidates forget this on the exam:

  • Add: kubectl taint node worker-1 key=value:NoSchedule
  • Remove: kubectl taint node worker-1 key=value:NoSchedule-

14.6 Node Management for Admins

Cluster administrators regularly need to perform node maintenance — OS upgrades, kernel patches, hardware replacement. Kubernetes provides three commands for safe node management. This is a very common CKA exam scenario.

1
Cordon

Mark node as unschedulable. Existing pods stay, but no new pods can be scheduled here.

2
Drain

Safely evict all pods to other nodes. Respects PDB, grace periods, and pod lifecycle.

3
Maintenance

Perform the actual work — OS upgrade, kernel patch, reboot, hardware swap.

4
Uncordon

Mark node as schedulable again. New pods can now be placed here.

Node Maintenance Workflow 
# Step 1: Cordon — stop new pods from scheduling
kubectl cordon worker-1
# node/worker-1 cordoned

# Verify: node shows SchedulingDisabled
kubectl get nodes
# NAME        STATUS                     ROLES    AGE
# worker-1    Ready,SchedulingDisabled   worker   30d

# Step 2: Drain — safely evict all pods
kubectl drain worker-1 --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data
# evicting pod default/anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d-abc12
# evicting pod default/anihpj-7d9f8b6c5d-def34
# node/worker-1 drained

# Step 3: Perform maintenance (OS upgrade, reboot, etc.)
# ... work happens here ...

# Step 4: Uncordon — allow pods again
kubectl uncordon worker-1
# node/worker-1 uncordoned

# Verify: node is Ready again (no SchedulingDisabled)
kubectl get nodes
# NAME        STATUS   ROLES    AGE
# worker-1    Ready    worker   30d
Node Maintenance Flow — Safe Pod Migration
  ┌─────── worker-1 (BEFORE) ───────┐     ┌─────── worker-2 ────────┐
  │                                  │     │                          │
  │  📦 anihpj-abc    📦 redis-xyz  │     │  📦 anihpj-ghi          │
  │  📦 anihpj-def                  │     │                          │
  │                                  │     │                          │
  └──────────────────────────────────┘     └──────────────────────────┘

  Step 1: kubectl cordon worker-1
  ┌─── worker-1 (🚫 SchedulingDisabled) ──┐
  │  📦 anihpj-abc  📦 redis-xyz  (stay)  │  ← existing pods remain
  │  📦 anihpj-def                (stay)  │  ← new pods blocked
  └────────────────────────────────────────┘

  Step 2: kubectl drain worker-1 --ignore-daemonsets
  ┌─── worker-1 (EMPTY) ────┐     ┌─────── worker-2 ────────────┐
  │                          │     │  📦 anihpj-ghi (existing)   │
  │  (all pods evicted)      │     │  📦 anihpj-abc (migrated)   │
  │                          │     │  📦 anihpj-def (migrated)   │
  │  🔧 Maintenance time!   │     │  📦 redis-xyz  (migrated)   │
  └──────────────────────────┘     └──────────────────────────────┘

  Step 3-4: Maintenance → uncordon
  ┌─── worker-1 (✅ Ready) ──┐
  │  Now accepts new pods!    │
  └───────────────────────────┘
🚨 Drain Gotchas
  • --ignore-daemonsets is required if DaemonSet pods exist (they can't be evicted)
  • --delete-emptydir-data is needed if pods use emptyDir volumes
  • --force only if there are standalone pods (not managed by RS/Deployment) — data will be lost!
  • Without these flags, drain will refuse and show an error — know which flag to add!

14.7 Topology Spread Constraints

Topology spread constraints give you fine-grained control over how pods are distributed across topology domains (zones, nodes, regions). Unlike pod anti-affinity which is "avoid same node", topology spread says "distribute evenly".

anihpj-topology-spread.yaml 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
spec:
  replicas: 6
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj
    spec:
      topologySpreadConstraints:
      - maxSkew: 1                           # Max diff in pod count between zones
        topologyKey: topology.kubernetes.io/zone  # Spread across zones
        whenUnsatisfiable: DoNotSchedule     # Hard constraint
        labelSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: anihpj
      - maxSkew: 1
        topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname   # Also spread across nodes
        whenUnsatisfiable: ScheduleAnyway    # Soft constraint
        labelSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: anihpj
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
Topology Spread — Even Distribution Across Zones
  6 replicas, maxSkew: 1, topologyKey: zone

  ┌───── us-east-1a ──────┐  ┌───── us-east-1b ──────┐  ┌───── us-east-1c ──────┐
  │  📦 anihpj-1           │  │  📦 anihpj-3           │  │  📦 anihpj-5           │
  │  📦 anihpj-2           │  │  📦 anihpj-4           │  │  📦 anihpj-6           │
  │  Count: 2              │  │  Count: 2              │  │  Count: 2              │
  └────────────────────────┘  └────────────────────────┘  └────────────────────────┘

  maxSkew = max(count) - min(count) = 2 - 2 = 0  ✅ (within maxSkew of 1)

  ❌ Without topology spread (all could land on one zone):
  ┌───── us-east-1a ──────┐  ┌───── us-east-1b ──────┐  ┌───── us-east-1c ──────┐
  │  📦📦📦📦📦📦           │  │  (empty)               │  │  (empty)               │
  │  Count: 6   ← BAD!    │  │  Count: 0              │  │  Count: 0              │
  └────────────────────────┘  └────────────────────────┘  └────────────────────────┘
  Zone failure = total outage! 💀

Topology Spread Parameters

📏 maxSkew
Maximum allowed difference in pod counts between topology domains. Lower = more even.
🗺️ topologyKey
Node label to group by: kubernetes.io/hostname (node), topology.kubernetes.io/zone (zone)
🚫 whenUnsatisfiable
DoNotSchedule (hard — pod stays Pending) or ScheduleAnyway (soft — best effort)

14.8 Priority and Preemption

PriorityClasses let you assign importance levels to pods. When the cluster is full, a high-priority pod can preempt (evict) a lower-priority pod to make room.

priority-classes.yaml 
# High priority — critical production workloads
apiVersion: scheduling.k8s.io/v1
kind: PriorityClass
metadata:
  name: high-priority
value: 1000000
globalDefault: false
preemptionPolicy: PreemptLowerPriority
description: "Critical production workloads like anihpj"
---
# Low priority — batch jobs, can be evicted
apiVersion: scheduling.k8s.io/v1
kind: PriorityClass
metadata:
  name: low-priority
value: 100
globalDefault: false
preemptionPolicy: Never          # Don't preempt others
description: "Batch jobs and background tasks"
---
# Usage in Deployment
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: anihpj
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: anihpj
    spec:
      priorityClassName: high-priority   # ← assign priority
      containers:
      - name: django
        image: anihpj:v1
Preemption — High Priority Pod Evicts Low Priority
  Cluster is FULL — no free capacity

  ┌──── worker-1 ─────┐  ┌──── worker-2 ─────┐
  │ 📦 batch-job-1     │  │ 📦 batch-job-3     │
  │ (low-priority:100) │  │ (low-priority:100) │
  │ 📦 batch-job-2     │  │ 📦 batch-job-4     │
  │ (low-priority:100) │  │ (low-priority:100) │
  └────────────────────┘  └────────────────────┘

  NEW POD: anihpj (high-priority: 1000000) needs scheduling!

  Scheduler: "No room, but anihpj has higher priority..."
  → PREEMPTS batch-job-1 on worker-1

  ┌──── worker-1 ─────┐  ┌──── worker-2 ─────┐
  │ 🌟 anihpj          │  │ 📦 batch-job-3     │
  │ (high-priority!)   │  │ (low-priority:100) │
  │ 📦 batch-job-2     │  │ 📦 batch-job-4     │
  │ (low-priority:100) │  │ (low-priority:100) │
  └────────────────────┘  └────────────────────┘

  batch-job-1 was evicted to make room for anihpj  ⚡
💡 When to Use PriorityClasses

Use high priority for production-critical workloads (web servers, APIs). Use low priority for batch jobs, CI/CD runners, dev environments. This ensures production always gets resources first.

🧩 Scheduling Mechanisms Comparison — When to Use What

Mechanism Controls Direction Best For
nodeSelector Pod → Node 🟢 Attract (hard) Simple label matching
Node Affinity Pod → Node 🟢 Attract (hard/soft) Complex rules, preferred placement
Pod Affinity Pod → Pod 🟢 Attract (co-locate) Low latency (app near cache)
Pod Anti-Affinity Pod → Pod 🔴 Repel (spread) HA (replicas on different nodes)
Taints/Tolerations Node → Pod 🔴 Repel + allow Dedicated nodes, maintenance
Topology Spread Pod distribution ⚖️ Even spread Balance across zones/nodes
Static Pods Fixed to node 📌 Pinned Control plane components
PriorityClass Pod importance ⬆️ Preempt lower Critical vs batch workloads

14.9 Practice Exercises

These exercises mirror actual CKA exam questions. Practice each one until you can complete it in under 5 minutes.

🏋️
Exercise 14.1: Taints & Tolerations

1. Taint node01 with env=prod:NoSchedule
2. Create an nginx pod — verify it does NOT schedule on node01
3. Add a toleration — verify it now schedules there
4. Remove the taint

🏋️
Exercise 14.2: Node Affinity

1. Label node01 with disktype=ssd
2. Create pod with required nodeAffinity for ssd nodes
3. Create pod with preferred nodeAffinity for ssd
4. Verify with kubectl get pods -o wide

🏋️
Exercise 14.3: Pod Anti-Affinity

1. Create anihpj deployment (replicas: 3)
2. Add podAntiAffinity — no 2 pods on same node
3. Verify: kubectl get pods -o wide (all on different nodes)
4. Scale to 4 — what happens if only 3 nodes?

🏋️
Exercise 14.4: Node Maintenance

1. Cordon worker-1
2. Deploy nginx (replicas: 3) — verify none land on worker-1
3. Drain worker-1 safely
4. Uncordon and verify

Exercise 14.1 — Solution (try it yourself first!) 
# 1. Taint the node
kubectl taint node node01 env=prod:NoSchedule

# 2. Create nginx pod (no toleration)
kubectl run nginx-test --image=nginx
kubectl get pod nginx-test -o wide
# → Should be on a different node (not node01)

# 3. Create pod WITH toleration
cat <<'EOF' | kubectl apply -f -
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: nginx-tolerant
spec:
  tolerations:
  - key: "env"
    operator: "Equal"
    value: "prod"
    effect: "NoSchedule"
  containers:
  - name: nginx
    image: nginx
EOF
kubectl get pod nginx-tolerant -o wide
# → Can schedule on node01

# 4. Remove taint
kubectl taint node node01 env=prod:NoSchedule-

# Clean up
kubectl delete pod nginx-test nginx-tolerant

Remove labelkubectl label node NAME key-
Add taintkubectl taint node NAME key=val:Effect
Remove taintkubectl taint node NAME key=val:Effect-
Cordon nodekubectl cordon NAME
Drain nodekubectl drain NAME --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data
