Check pod placementkubectl get pods -o wide

Chapter 15: Resource Management

Every container in Kubernetes can request a minimum amount of CPU and memory, and be limited to a maximum. Understanding requests, limits, QoS classes, LimitRanges, and ResourceQuotas is critical for cluster stability — and prominently tested on the CKA exam. A misconfigured resource setting can crash an entire node.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~1 hour 📊 Resources 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Set CPU and memory requests (scheduling guarantee) and limits (hard ceiling)
  • ✅ Understand QoS classes — Guaranteed, Burstable, BestEffort
  • ✅ Configure LimitRange for namespace-wide default limits
  • ✅ Configure ResourceQuota to cap total namespace consumption
  • ✅ Know what happens when CPU is exceeded (throttle) vs memory (OOMKilled)
🎓 CKA Relevance
Workloads & Scheduling 15% Cluster Architecture 25%
Resource Questions on the CKA

You'll likely be asked to:

  • Create pods with specific resource requests and limits
  • Create LimitRange or ResourceQuota for a namespace
  • Identify why a pod is OOMKilled or stuck in Pending
  • Know: kubectl top pods and kubectl top nodes

15.1 Resource Requests and Limits

Every container can specify requests (minimum guaranteed) and limits (maximum allowed). The scheduler uses requests to decide where to place pods. The kubelet enforces limits at runtime.

📋
Requests

"I need at least this much." Used by the scheduler for placement. The node must have this amount free or the pod won't be scheduled.

🚧
Limits

"Don't use more than this." Enforced by the kubelet. CPU exceeds limit → throttled. Memory exceeds limit → OOMKilled.

anihpj-resources.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: anihpj
spec:
  containers:
  - name: django
    image: anihpj:v1
    resources:
      requests:                  # Scheduling guarantee
        cpu: "250m"              # 0.25 CPU cores (250 millicores)
        memory: "256Mi"          # 256 MiB RAM
      limits:                    # Hard ceiling
        cpu: "500m"              # 0.5 CPU cores max
        memory: "512Mi"          # 512 MiB RAM max

CPU & Memory Units

⚙️ CPU: 1000m = 1 core
100m = 0.1 core, 250m = 0.25 core, 1 = 1 full core, 2 = 2 cores
💾 Memory: Mi, Gi
128Mi = 128 MiB, 1Gi = 1 GiB. Use Mi/Gi (binary) not MB/GB (decimal).
⏳ CPU exceeded
Throttled — process slows down but keeps running. Not killed.
💀 Memory exceeded
OOMKilled — container is killed immediately. Restarts per restartPolicy.
Requests vs Limits — Visual Explanation
  Node Capacity: 4 CPU, 8Gi Memory
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                  │
  │  ┌──────────── anihpj container ─────────────┐                  │
  │  │                                            │                  │
  │  │  CPU:    [====request 250m====|...limit 500m....]             │
  │  │          ├─── guaranteed ────┤├── can burst ──┤               │
  │  │                                            │                  │
  │  │  Memory: [====request 256Mi===|...limit 512Mi...]             │
  │  │          ├─── guaranteed ────┤├── can use ───┤                │
  │  │                                   ↑ exceed = OOMKill 💀      │
  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────┘                  │
  │                                                                  │
  │  What happens:                                                   │
  │  ├── Actual CPU  = 300m  → ✅ Within limit, no throttle         │
  │  ├── Actual CPU  = 600m  → ⏳ Throttled to 500m                 │
  │  ├── Actual Mem  = 400Mi → ✅ Within limit, fine                │
  │  └── Actual Mem  = 600Mi → 💀 OOMKilled! Container restarts    │
  │                                                                  │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
🚨 Never Skip Resource Settings in Production
  • No requests: Scheduler doesn't know what the pod needs → random placement → resource contention
  • No limits: Pod can consume ALL available node memory → OOMs other pods → cascading failure
  • Always set both on every production container!
Monitoring Resource Usage 
# Check actual usage vs requests/limits
kubectl top pods
# NAME      CPU(cores)   MEMORY(bytes)
# anihpj   120m         180Mi

kubectl top nodes
# NAME       CPU(cores)   CPU%   MEMORY(bytes)   MEMORY%
# worker-1   850m         42%    3200Mi           40%

# Find pods with no resource limits (dangerous!)
kubectl get pods -A -o json | jq '.items[] | 
  select(.spec.containers[].resources.limits == null) | 
  .metadata.name'

Troubleshooting OOMKilled Pods

One of the most common issues on the CKA and in production. Here's a systematic approach:

OOMKilled Diagnosis Steps 
# Step 1: Find OOMKilled pods
kubectl get pods -A | grep OOMKilled
kubectl get pods -A | grep CrashLoopBackOff   # often caused by OOM

# Step 2: Check the pod's last state
kubectl describe pod anihpj-abc12 | grep -A5 "Last State"
#   Last State:  Terminated
#     Reason:    OOMKilled
#     Exit Code: 137       ← 137 = SIGKILL (memory limit hit)

# Step 3: Check current resource config
kubectl get pod anihpj-abc12 -o jsonpath='{.spec.containers[0].resources}'
# {"limits":{"memory":"256Mi"},"requests":{"memory":"128Mi"}}

# Step 4: Check actual usage (if pod is running)
kubectl top pod anihpj-abc12
# CPU: 100m    MEMORY: 245Mi   ← close to 256Mi limit!

# Step 5: Fix — increase memory limit
kubectl set resources deployment/anihpj -c django \
  --limits=memory=512Mi --requests=memory=256Mi
OOMKill Decision Tree — Is It the Container or the Node?
  Pod terminated with Exit Code 137?
       │
       ├── YES → OOMKilled!
       │        │
       │   Was the pod using more memory
       │   than its spec.resources.limits.memory?
       │        │
       │   ┌────┴────┐
       │   YES        NO
       │   │          │
       │   Container  Node-level OOM
       │   limit hit  (node ran out of memory)
       │   │          │
       │   Fix:       Fix: add more nodes,
       │   Increase   reduce other workloads,
       │   memory     or fix memory leaks
       │   limits
       │
       └── Exit Code 1 = app error (not OOM)

15.2 QoS Classes — Quality of Service

Kubernetes automatically assigns a QoS class to every pod based on its resource configuration. This determines the eviction priority when a node runs out of resources.

QoS Class Criteria Eviction Priority anihpj Use Case
Guaranteed Every container has requests == limits (both CPU & memory set and equal) Last to be evicted (safest) 🛡️ ✅ Production anihpj
Burstable At least one container has requests < limits (or only requests set) Evicted after BestEffort ⚠️ ⚠️ Dev/staging anihpj
BestEffort No requests or limits set on any container First to be evicted (riskiest) 💀 ❌ Never in production
QoS Eviction Order — Node Under Memory Pressure
  Node memory: 95% used — kubelet starts evicting pods!

  Eviction Order:
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                   │
  │  💀 FIRST: BestEffort pods                       │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │ Pod: test-pod (no requests, no limits)      │ │
  │  │ → EVICTED immediately                       │ │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
  │          │                                        │
  │          ▼ (still need more memory?)              │
  │  ⚠️ SECOND: Burstable pods                      │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │ Pod: dev-anihpj (requests < limits)         │ │
  │  │ → EVICTED if using more than requests       │ │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
  │          │                                        │
  │          ▼ (extreme pressure only)                │
  │  🛡️ LAST: Guaranteed pods                       │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │ Pod: prod-anihpj (requests == limits)       │ │
  │  │ → Only evicted as absolute last resort      │ │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
QoS Examples 
# Guaranteed QoS — requests EQUAL limits
resources:
  requests:
    cpu: "500m"
    memory: "512Mi"
  limits:
    cpu: "500m"          # same as request
    memory: "512Mi"      # same as request
# → QoS: Guaranteed ✅

---
# Burstable QoS — requests LESS THAN limits
resources:
  requests:
    cpu: "250m"
    memory: "256Mi"
  limits:
    cpu: "500m"          # more than request
    memory: "512Mi"      # more than request
# → QoS: Burstable ⚠️

---
# BestEffort QoS — NO resources at all
# (just don't include the resources section)
# → QoS: BestEffort 💀
Check Pod QoS Class 
# Check QoS class of a pod
kubectl get pod anihpj -o jsonpath='{.status.qosClass}'
# Guaranteed

# Check QoS of all pods
kubectl get pods -o custom-columns='NAME:.metadata.name,QOS:.status.qosClass'
# NAME              QOS
# anihpj-abc12      Guaranteed
# dev-app-xyz       Burstable
# test-pod          BestEffort
💡 Production QoS Strategy for anihpj

For your production stack, aim for these QoS classes:

  • Guaranteed: anihpj Django (production), PostgreSQL — set requests == limits
  • Burstable: anihpj staging, Redis cache — allow bursting for efficiency
  • BestEffort: Only for throwaway test pods in dev. Never in production!

Pro tip: Use Guaranteed for anything facing users. Use Burstable for internal services that benefit from occasional burst capacity.

15.3 LimitRange — Namespace Default Limits

A LimitRange sets default resource requests and limits for containers in a namespace. It also enforces min/max boundaries. This prevents pods from running without any resource configuration.

limitrange.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: LimitRange
metadata:
  name: anihpj-limits
  namespace: anihpj-dev
spec:
  limits:
  - type: Container
    default:                     # Default LIMITS (if not specified)
      cpu: "500m"
      memory: "512Mi"
    defaultRequest:              # Default REQUESTS (if not specified)
      cpu: "250m"
      memory: "256Mi"
    max:                         # Maximum allowed
      cpu: "2"
      memory: "2Gi"
    min:                         # Minimum allowed
      cpu: "50m"
      memory: "32Mi"
  - type: Pod
    max:
      cpu: "4"
      memory: "4Gi"
LimitRange in Action 
# Create namespace and LimitRange
kubectl create namespace anihpj-dev
kubectl apply -f limitrange.yaml

# Create pod WITHOUT specifying resources
kubectl run test-pod --image=nginx -n anihpj-dev

# Check — LimitRange injected default values!
kubectl get pod test-pod -n anihpj-dev -o jsonpath='{.spec.containers[0].resources}'
# {"limits":{"cpu":"500m","memory":"512Mi"},"requests":{"cpu":"250m","memory":"256Mi"}}

# Try to create pod exceeding max
# → Error: maximum cpu usage per Container is 2, but limit is 4
LimitRange — Automatic Defaults
  Namespace: anihpj-dev (with LimitRange)

  Pod created WITHOUT resources:        Pod received DEFAULTS:
  ┌──────────────────────────┐         ┌──────────────────────────────┐
  │ spec:                    │   →     │ spec:                        │
  │   containers:            │   →     │   containers:                │
  │   - name: nginx          │   →     │   - name: nginx              │
  │     image: nginx         │   →     │     image: nginx             │
  │     # no resources!      │   →     │     resources:               │
  │                          │   →     │       requests:              │
  │                          │   →     │         cpu: "250m"    ← injected! │
  │                          │   →     │         memory: "256Mi" ← injected!│
  │                          │   →     │       limits:               │
  │                          │   →     │         cpu: "500m"    ← injected! │
  │                          │   →     │         memory: "512Mi" ← injected!│
  └──────────────────────────┘         └──────────────────────────────┘

15.4 ResourceQuota — Namespace Capacity Control

A ResourceQuota limits the total resources consumed across all pods in a namespace. This prevents one team from using all cluster resources and is a key tool for multi-tenant clusters.

resourcequota.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: ResourceQuota
metadata:
  name: anihpj-quota
  namespace: anihpj-dev
spec:
  hard:
    # Compute resources
    requests.cpu: "4"            # Total CPU requests across all pods
    requests.memory: "8Gi"       # Total memory requests
    limits.cpu: "8"              # Total CPU limits
    limits.memory: "16Gi"        # Total memory limits

    # Object count limits
    pods: "10"                   # Max 10 pods in this namespace
    services: "5"                # Max 5 services
    persistentvolumeclaims: "5"  # Max 5 PVCs
    configmaps: "20"
    secrets: "20"
ResourceQuota Commands 
# Apply quota
kubectl apply -f resourcequota.yaml

# Check quota usage
kubectl get resourcequota -n anihpj-dev
# NAME           AGE   REQUEST                                              LIMIT
# anihpj-quota   5m    requests.cpu: 750m/4, requests.memory: 768Mi/8Gi    limits.cpu: 1500m/8, limits.memory: 1536Mi/16Gi

# Detailed view
kubectl describe resourcequota anihpj-quota -n anihpj-dev
# Name:                   anihpj-quota
# Namespace:              anihpj-dev
# Resource                Used    Hard
# --------                ----    ----
# limits.cpu              1500m   8
# limits.memory           1536Mi  16Gi
# pods                    3       10
# requests.cpu            750m    4
# requests.memory         768Mi   8Gi
Resource Control Hierarchy — 3 Levels
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  LEVEL 1: ResourceQuota (namespace total budget)               │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ "anihpj-dev can use max 4 CPUs, 8Gi memory total"       │  │
  │  │  Used: 750m / 4 CPU    Used: 768Mi / 8Gi memory         │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │       │                                                        │
  │       ▼                                                        │
  │  LEVEL 2: LimitRange (per-container defaults & bounds)       │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │ "Each container: default 250m req, 500m limit"           │  │
  │  │ "Min: 50m CPU, Max: 2 CPU per container"                 │  │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  │       │                                                        │
  │       ▼                                                        │
  │  LEVEL 3: Pod spec (individual container settings)            │
  │  ┌────────────┐  ┌────────────┐  ┌────────────┐              │
  │  │ anihpj-1   │  │ anihpj-2   │  │ anihpj-3   │              │
  │  │ req: 250m  │  │ req: 250m  │  │ req: 250m  │              │
  │  │ lim: 500m  │  │ lim: 500m  │  │ lim: 500m  │              │
  │  └────────────┘  └────────────┘  └────────────┘              │
  │  Total: 750m request, 1500m limit (within quota ✅)           │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
⚠️ ResourceQuota Requires Resource Specs

When a ResourceQuota is active in a namespace, every pod must specify resource requests and limits. Without them, the pod creation will be rejected. Use a LimitRange to auto-inject defaults so developers don't have to specify them manually.

🔑 Chapter 15 Quick Reference

Action Command
Check pod resource usagekubectl top pods
Check node resource usagekubectl top nodes
View pod QoS classkubectl get pod NAME -o jsonpath='{.status.qosClass}'
View LimitRangekubectl describe limitrange -n NAMESPACE
View ResourceQuotakubectl describe resourcequota -n NAMESPACE
Check OOMKilled podskubectl get pods | grep OOMKilled
View container resourceskubectl get pod NAME -o jsonpath='{.spec.containers[*].resources}'

Chapter 16: Jobs, CronJobs, DaemonSets, and StatefulSets

Deployments aren't the only workload type. Kubernetes provides specialized controllers for run-to-completion tasks (Jobs), scheduled tasks (CronJobs), one-pod-per-node daemons (DaemonSets), and stateful applications (StatefulSets). Mastering when to use each is critical for the CKA exam.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~1.5 hours ⚙️ Workload Types 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Create Jobs for one-off tasks (database migrations, batch processing)
  • ✅ Schedule recurring tasks with CronJobs (backups, reports)
  • ✅ Deploy DaemonSets for per-node system services (logging, monitoring)
  • ✅ Understand StatefulSets for databases and stateful applications
  • ✅ Choose the right workload type for any scenario
🎓 CKA Relevance
Workloads & Scheduling 15% Troubleshooting 30%
Workload Type Questions on the CKA

The CKA often asks you to create Jobs and CronJobs. Know these imperative shortcuts:

  • kubectl create job NAME --image=IMG -- COMMAND
  • kubectl create cronjob NAME --image=IMG --schedule="*/5 * * * *" -- COMMAND
  • DaemonSets require YAML (no imperative shortcut)

16.1 Jobs — Run to Completion

A Job creates one or more pods and ensures they run to successful completion. Unlike Deployments (which run forever), Jobs stop once the task is done. Perfect for batch processing, database migrations, and one-off scripts.

Imperative Job Creation 
# Create a simple job
kubectl create job db-migrate --image=anihpj:v1 -- python manage.py migrate

# Generate YAML first (exam tip: always verify before applying)
kubectl create job db-migrate --image=anihpj:v1 \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml -- python manage.py migrate > job.yaml

# Check job status
kubectl get jobs
# NAME         COMPLETIONS   DURATION   AGE
# db-migrate   1/1           12s        30s

# View job pods
kubectl get pods --selector=job-name=db-migrate
# NAME               READY   STATUS      RESTARTS   AGE
# db-migrate-abc12   0/1     Completed   0          45s

# Check logs
kubectl logs job/db-migrate
anihpj-migrate-job.yaml 
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: Job
metadata:
  name: anihpj-db-migrate
spec:
  completions: 1              # How many pods must complete successfully
  parallelism: 1              # How many pods run simultaneously
  backoffLimit: 3             # Retry up to 3 times before marking failed
  activeDeadlineSeconds: 300  # Timeout: kill after 5 minutes
  ttlSecondsAfterFinished: 60 # Auto-delete finished job after 60s
  template:
    spec:
      restartPolicy: Never    # Jobs: Never or OnFailure (NOT Always!)
      containers:
      - name: migrate
        image: anihpj:v1
        command: ["python", "manage.py", "migrate"]
        env:
        - name: DATABASE_URL
          valueFrom:
            secretKeyRef:
              name: anihpj-db-secret
              key: url

Job Configuration Fields

✅ completions
How many pods must succeed. Default: 1. Set to N for batch: process N items.
⚡ parallelism
How many pods run at once. Default: 1. Increase for faster batch processing.
🔄 backoffLimit
Retry count before job is marked Failed. Default: 6. Set lower for fast failure.
⏱️ activeDeadlineSeconds
Hard timeout. Job is killed if it runs longer, regardless of retries.
🗑️ ttlSecondsAfterFinished
Auto-cleanup. Completed/failed job is deleted after this many seconds.
🔁 restartPolicy
Never (creates new pod on failure) or OnFailure (restarts container in same pod). Never Always!
Job Lifecycle — Run, Complete, Stop
  Job: anihpj-db-migrate (completions: 1, backoffLimit: 3)

  Attempt 1:
  ┌──────────────┐
  │ Pod: migrate │ → Runs python manage.py migrate
  │   🔄 Running │ → ...
  │   ✅ Success │ → Exit code 0
  └──────────────┘
  Job Status: Complete ✅  (1/1 completions)

  ─── What if it fails? ───

  Attempt 1:
  ┌──────────────┐
  │ Pod: migrate │ → DB connection refused
  │   ❌ Failed  │ → Exit code 1
  └──────────────┘
  Attempt 2:  (backoff: 10s wait)
  ┌──────────────┐
  │ Pod: migrate │ → DB now available
  │   ✅ Success │ → Exit code 0
  └──────────────┘
  Job Status: Complete ✅  (1/1 completions, 1 retry)
⚠️ CKA Trap: restartPolicy

Jobs CANNOT use restartPolicy: Always (that's for Deployments). If you forget this, the Job will be rejected. Use Never or OnFailure.

Batch Processing with Parallelism

Jobs can process multiple items in parallel. Set completions for how many must finish and parallelism for how many run at once.

batch-processing-job.yaml 
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: Job
metadata:
  name: anihpj-image-processor
spec:
  completions: 10       # 10 total items to process
  parallelism: 3        # Run 3 pods at a time
  backoffLimit: 5
  template:
    spec:
      restartPolicy: Never
      containers:
      - name: processor
        image: anihpj:v1
        command: ["python", "manage.py", "process_images"]
Batch Job: 10 Completions, 3 Parallel
  Job: anihpj-image-processor (completions: 10, parallelism: 3)

  Wave 1: [📦 Pod-1] [📦 Pod-2] [📦 Pod-3]   ← 3 running
               ✅         ✅         ✅
  Wave 2: [📦 Pod-4] [📦 Pod-5] [📦 Pod-6]   ← 3 running
               ✅         ✅         ✅
  Wave 3: [📦 Pod-7] [📦 Pod-8] [📦 Pod-9]   ← 3 running
               ✅         ✅         ✅
  Wave 4: [📦 Pod-10]                        ← 1 running (last item)
               ✅
  Job Status: Complete ✅  (10/10 completions)

  Total time: ~4 waves instead of 10 sequential runs!
  Parallelism gives ~3x speedup ⚡

16.2 CronJobs — Scheduled Tasks

CronJobs are like Unix cron but in Kubernetes. They create Jobs on a schedule — perfect for backups, reports, cleanup tasks, and periodic data processing.

Imperative CronJob Creation 
# Create a CronJob — daily backup at 2 AM
kubectl create cronjob anihpj-backup \
  --image=anihpj:v1 \
  --schedule="0 2 * * *" \
  -- python manage.py dumpdata --output=/backup/db.json

# Check CronJob
kubectl get cronjobs
# NAME             SCHEDULE      SUSPEND   ACTIVE   LAST SCHEDULE   AGE
# anihpj-backup    0 2 * * *     False     0        30s             5m

# View recently created Jobs
kubectl get jobs --sort-by=.metadata.creationTimestamp
anihpj-backup-cronjob.yaml 
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: CronJob
metadata:
  name: anihpj-backup
spec:
  schedule: "0 2 * * *"          # Daily at 2:00 AM
  concurrencyPolicy: Forbid       # Don't start new if previous still running
  successfulJobsHistoryLimit: 3   # Keep last 3 successful job records
  failedJobsHistoryLimit: 1       # Keep last 1 failed job record
  startingDeadlineSeconds: 200    # If job can't start in 200s, skip this run
  jobTemplate:
    spec:
      backoffLimit: 2
      template:
        spec:
          restartPolicy: OnFailure
          containers:
          - name: backup
            image: anihpj:v1
            command:
            - /bin/sh
            - -c
            - "python manage.py dumpdata | gzip > /backup/$(date +%Y%m%d).json.gz"
            volumeMounts:
            - name: backup-volume
              mountPath: /backup
          volumes:
          - name: backup-volume
            persistentVolumeClaim:
              claimName: backup-pvc
Schedule Meaning Use Case
*/5 * * * *Every 5 minutesHealth checks, cache refresh
0 2 * * *Daily at 2:00 AMDatabase backup
0 0 * * 0Every Sunday at midnightWeekly report generation
0 */6 * * *Every 6 hoursData sync, cleanup
30 8 1 * *1st of each month at 8:30 AMMonthly invoice generation
Cron Expression Format
  ┌───────────── minute (0-59)
  │ ┌───────────── hour (0-23)
  │ │ ┌───────────── day of month (1-31)
  │ │ │ ┌───────────── month (1-12)
  │ │ │ │ ┌───────────── day of week (0-6, Sun=0)
  │ │ │ │ │
  * * * * *

  Examples:
  0 2 * * *     = "At 02:00 every day"
  */5 * * * *   = "Every 5 minutes"
  0 0 * * 0     = "At midnight every Sunday"
  30 8 1 * *    = "At 08:30 on the 1st of every month"
🔒
concurrencyPolicy: Forbid

Don't start new job if previous is still running. Prevents overlap. Safest for DB backups.

🔄
concurrencyPolicy: Replace

Kill running job and start a new one. Good for "latest data only" scenarios.

📖 Third Option: concurrencyPolicy: Allow (default)

Allow lets multiple jobs run simultaneously. If CronJob fires every 5 minutes but a job takes 7 minutes, you'll have overlapping runs. This is fine for independent tasks (e.g., sending reports) but dangerous for database operations where concurrent writes could corrupt data. Always set Forbid for DB backups.

16.3 DaemonSets — One Pod Per Node

A DaemonSet ensures that exactly one copy of a pod runs on every node (or every selected node). When a new node joins the cluster, the DaemonSet automatically schedules a pod there. When a node is removed, the pod is garbage-collected.

📖 Common DaemonSet Use Cases
  • 📋 Log collectors — Fluentd, Filebeat, Promtail
  • 📊 Monitoring agents — Node Exporter, Datadog Agent
  • 🌐 Network plugins — Calico, Cilium, Flannel (CNI)
  • 💾 Storage daemons — Ceph, GlusterFS
  • 🔐 Security agents — Falco, Twistlock
fluentd-daemonset.yaml 
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: DaemonSet
metadata:
  name: fluentd
  namespace: kube-system
  labels:
    app: fluentd
spec:
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: fluentd
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: fluentd
    spec:
      tolerations:
      # Tolerate control plane taint to run on ALL nodes
      - key: node-role.kubernetes.io/control-plane
        operator: Exists
        effect: NoSchedule
      containers:
      - name: fluentd
        image: fluentd:v1.16
        resources:
          requests:
            cpu: "100m"
            memory: "200Mi"
          limits:
            cpu: "200m"
            memory: "400Mi"
        volumeMounts:
        - name: varlog
          mountPath: /var/log
        - name: varlibdockercontainers
          mountPath: /var/lib/docker/containers
          readOnly: true
      volumes:
      - name: varlog
        hostPath:
          path: /var/log
      - name: varlibdockercontainers
        hostPath:
          path: /var/lib/docker/containers
DaemonSet — Automatic Pod Per Node
  DaemonSet: "fluentd"   (replicas: N/A — auto-managed)

  ┌──────────────────┐  ┌──────────────────┐  ┌──────────────────┐
  │    worker-1      │  │    worker-2      │  │    worker-3      │
  │  ┌────────────┐  │  │  ┌────────────┐  │  │  ┌────────────┐  │
  │  │  fluentd   │  │  │  │  fluentd   │  │  │  │  fluentd   │  │
  │  │  📋 logs   │  │  │  │  📋 logs   │  │  │  │  📋 logs   │  │
  │  └────────────┘  │  │  └────────────┘  │  │  └────────────┘  │
  │  + anihpj pods   │  │  + anihpj pods   │  │  + anihpj pods   │
  └──────────────────┘  └──────────────────┘  └──────────────────┘

  ┌──────────────────┐
  │    worker-4      │  ← NEW node joins cluster!
  │  ┌────────────┐  │
  │  │  fluentd   │  │  DaemonSet controller AUTOMATICALLY
  │  │  📋 logs   │  │  schedules a pod here! 🔄
  │  └────────────┘  │
  └──────────────────┘

  ✅ Every node always has exactly 1 fluentd pod
  ✅ New nodes get a pod automatically
  ✅ Removed nodes — pod is cleaned up automatically
DaemonSet Commands 
# View DaemonSets
kubectl get daemonsets -A
# NAMESPACE     NAME         DESIRED   CURRENT   READY   UP-TO-DATE   NODE SELECTOR   AGE
# kube-system   fluentd      4         4         4       4            <none>          10d
# kube-system   kube-proxy   4         4         4       4            <none>          30d

# DaemonSet on specific nodes only (using nodeSelector)
# Add nodeSelector to template.spec to limit which nodes get the pod

# Check which nodes have DaemonSet pods
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l app=fluentd -o wide
💡 CKA Tip: No Imperative Command for DaemonSets

Unlike Deployments and Jobs, there's no kubectl create daemonset command. You must write YAML. Quick trick: generate a Deployment YAML, then change kind: Deployment to kind: DaemonSet and remove the replicas field.

DaemonSet Update Strategy

DaemonSets support two update strategies, similar to Deployments:

Strategy Behavior Use Case
RollingUpdate (default) Pods updated node by node — old pod killed, new pod started on each node sequentially Most updates — zero gap per node
OnDelete Pods only updated when you manually delete them — full control over rollout pace Critical system daemons where you want to control timing (e.g., CNI plugin)
DaemonSet Update Strategy YAML 
# In the DaemonSet spec:
spec:
  updateStrategy:
    type: RollingUpdate        # or OnDelete
    rollingUpdate:
      maxUnavailable: 1        # Update 1 node at a time (default)
      # maxSurge: 0            # Not supported for DaemonSets

16.4 StatefulSets — Stateful Applications

StatefulSets are for applications that need stable, unique identity and persistent storage. Unlike Deployments (random pod names, shared storage), StatefulSets provide predictable pod names, ordered deployment, and per-pod persistent volumes.

Feature Deployment StatefulSet
Pod Names Random: app-x7k2z, app-m9p4q Ordered: app-0, app-1, app-2
DNS Identity Only via Service (shared) Each pod gets own DNS: app-0.svc.ns
Storage Shared or none Per-pod PVC (survives pod restarts)
Creation Order All at once (parallel) Sequential: 0 → 1 → 2 (ordered)
Deletion Order Random Reverse: 2 → 1 → 0 (ordered)
Use Case Stateless apps (anihpj Django) Databases, Kafka, Redis Cluster, ZooKeeper
postgresql-statefulset.yaml — anihpj Database 
# Headless Service (required for StatefulSet DNS)
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: postgres-headless
spec:
  clusterIP: None               # Headless — no cluster IP
  selector:
    app: postgres
  ports:
  - port: 5432
    name: postgres
---
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: StatefulSet
metadata:
  name: postgres
spec:
  serviceName: "postgres-headless"  # Required: links to headless service
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: postgres
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: postgres
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: postgres
        image: postgres:16
        ports:
        - containerPort: 5432
        env:
        - name: POSTGRES_DB
          value: "anihpj"
        - name: PGDATA
          value: "/var/lib/postgresql/data/pgdata"
        volumeMounts:
        - name: data
          mountPath: /var/lib/postgresql/data
        resources:
          requests:
            cpu: "500m"
            memory: "1Gi"
          limits:
            cpu: "1"
            memory: "2Gi"
  volumeClaimTemplates:            # Per-pod PVC template
  - metadata:
      name: data
    spec:
      accessModes: ["ReadWriteOnce"]
      storageClassName: standard
      resources:
        requests:
          storage: 10Gi
StatefulSet — Ordered Pods with Stable Identity & Per-Pod Storage
  StatefulSet: "postgres"    replicas: 3
  serviceName: "postgres-headless"

  Deployment Pods:             StatefulSet Pods:
  (random names)               (ordered, predictable)
  ┌──────────────┐             ┌──────────────┐
  │ app-x7k2z    │             │ postgres-0   │ ← Created FIRST (primary)
  ├──────────────┤             │  DNS: postgres-0.postgres-headless
  │ app-m9p4q    │             ├──────────────┤
  ├──────────────┤             │ postgres-1   │ ← Created SECOND (must wait for 0)
  │ app-j3r8w    │             │  DNS: postgres-1.postgres-headless
  └──────────────┘             ├──────────────┤
                               │ postgres-2   │ ← Created THIRD
                               │  DNS: postgres-2.postgres-headless
                               └──────────────┘

  Per-Pod Persistent Volumes (survive pod restarts!):
  ┌──────────┐     ┌──────────┐     ┌──────────┐
  │postgres-0│     │postgres-1│     │postgres-2│
  │┌────────┐│     │┌────────┐│     │┌────────┐│
  ││data-   ││     ││data-   ││     ││data-   ││
  ││postgres││     ││postgres││     ││postgres││
  ││-0      ││     ││-1      ││     ││-2      ││
  ││ 10Gi   ││     ││ 10Gi   ││     ││ 10Gi   ││
  │└───┬────┘│     │└───┬────┘│     │└───┬────┘│
  └────┼─────┘     └────┼─────┘     └────┼─────┘
       ▼                ▼                ▼
  ┌─────────┐     ┌─────────┐     ┌─────────┐
  │  PV-0   │     │  PV-1   │     │  PV-2   │
  │ (disk)  │     │ (disk)  │     │ (disk)  │
  └─────────┘     └─────────┘     └─────────┘

  Key guarantees:
  ✅ Stable network identity  — postgres-0.postgres-headless.default.svc
  ✅ Ordered deployment       — 0 → 1 → 2 (each must be Ready before next)
  ✅ Ordered deletion         — 2 → 1 → 0 (reverse order)
  ✅ Stable storage           — PVC survives pod restart (data preserved!)
⚠️ StatefulSet Requires a Headless Service

You must create a headless Service (clusterIP: None) for the StatefulSet. The serviceName field in the StatefulSet spec must match the Service name. This enables DNS records for each pod like pod-name.service-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local.

🚨 StatefulSet Deletion — PVCs Are NOT Deleted!

When you delete a StatefulSet (even with --cascade=foreground), the PersistentVolumeClaims are preserved. This is by design to prevent data loss. You must manually delete them:

  • kubectl delete statefulset postgres → Pods deleted, PVCs remain ✅
  • kubectl get pvc → data-postgres-0, data-postgres-1, data-postgres-2 still exist
  • kubectl delete pvc data-postgres-0 data-postgres-1 data-postgres-2 → Manually clean up

This is intentional — recreating the StatefulSet will automatically reattach to existing PVCs, preserving all data!

StatefulSet Scaling — Ordered Operations 
# Scale up — pods created in order (3, 4, 5...)
kubectl scale statefulset postgres --replicas=5
# postgres-3 created (waits until Ready)
# postgres-4 created (waits until Ready)

# Scale down — pods deleted in REVERSE order (4, 3)
kubectl scale statefulset postgres --replicas=3
# postgres-4 terminated first
# postgres-3 terminated second
# postgres-0, 1, 2 untouched ✅

# PVCs from scaled-down pods are PRESERVED
kubectl get pvc
# data-postgres-3 and data-postgres-4 still exist
# Re-scaling up will reattach to them!

Choosing the Right Workload Type

🚀
Deployment

Stateless apps that run forever.
anihpj Django web server, nginx, API services.

🗄️
StatefulSet

Apps needing stable identity & storage.
PostgreSQL, MySQL, Kafka, Redis Cluster.

📋
DaemonSet

One pod on every node.
Log collectors, monitoring agents, CNI plugins.

✅
Job / CronJob

Run-to-completion / scheduled tasks.
DB migrations, backups, batch processing.

Decision Tree: Which Workload Type Do I Need?
                        ┌──────────────────┐
                        │  New Workload?   │
                        └────────┬─────────┘
                                 │
                    ┌────────────┴────────────┐
                    │                         │
              Runs forever?              Runs once/scheduled?
                    │                         │
              ┌─────┴─────┐             ┌─────┴─────┐
              │           │             │           │
        All nodes?   Some pods?    On schedule?   One-time?
              │           │             │           │
         DaemonSet  ┌─────┴─────┐   CronJob      Job
                    │           │
              Needs stable   Stateless?
              identity/PVC?     │
                    │       Deployment
              StatefulSet

  ╔════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
  ║  anihpj Stack Mapping:                                ║
  ║                                                        ║
  ║  📦 anihpj Django      → Deployment (stateless)       ║
  ║  🗄️ PostgreSQL         → StatefulSet (needs storage)  ║
  ║  📋 Fluentd logging    → DaemonSet (every node)       ║
  ║  🔄 DB migration       → Job (run once)               ║
  ║  💾 Nightly backup     → CronJob (scheduled)          ║
  ╚════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝

🟡 Part 5: Services & Networking Chapters 17-20

Chapter 17: Kubernetes Networking Fundamentals

Networking is the backbone of Kubernetes. Every pod, service, and DNS lookup depends on a correctly configured network. In this chapter you'll master the 4 fundamental networking rules, understand how CNI plugins implement the network, trace packets between pods on the same and different nodes, explore how kube-proxy routes service traffic, and build a troubleshooting toolkit for common network issues — all using our anihpj/jobpost Django stack as a real-world example.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~2 hours 🌐 Networking 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ The 4 fundamental Kubernetes networking rules — why NAT-free networking matters
  • ✅ How CNI plugins (Calico, Cilium, Flannel) implement the network layer
  • ✅ Pod-to-Pod communication — same-node vs cross-node packet paths
  • ✅ How kube-proxy translates Service IPs into pod endpoints using iptables/IPVS
  • ✅ Network troubleshooting commands and strategies for the CKA exam
🎓 CKA Relevance
Services & Networking 20% Troubleshooting 30%
Networking Is 20% of the CKA Exam

Networking questions on the CKA range from creating Services and Ingress to troubleshooting DNS and pod connectivity. Key exam skills:

  • Know which CNI plugin is installed: ls /etc/cni/net.d/
  • Check kube-proxy mode: kubectl logs -n kube-system kube-proxy-xxxxx
  • Test connectivity: kubectl exec -it pod -- curl http://service-name
  • Debug DNS: kubectl exec -it pod -- nslookup service-name

17.1 The Kubernetes Networking Model

Kubernetes enforces a flat, NAT-free networking model. This is fundamentally different from Docker's default bridge networking, and understanding these rules is critical for every CKA topic that follows.

1️⃣
Every Pod Gets a Unique IP

No two pods share an IP address. Containers within the same pod share the pod's IP and communicate via localhost.

2️⃣
Pod-to-Pod Without NAT

Any pod can reach any other pod directly using its IP — even across nodes. No network address translation needed.

3️⃣
Node-to-Pod Without NAT

Nodes can communicate directly with any pod IP in the cluster without address translation.

4️⃣
Pod Sees Its Own Real IP

Inside a pod, the IP it sees for itself is the same IP that every other pod and node sees. No surprises.

The 4 Fundamental Kubernetes Networking Rules
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  KUBERNETES FLAT NETWORKING MODEL                                        │
  │                                                                          │
  │  Rule 1: Every Pod gets a unique IP                                     │
  │  ┌────────────────┐  ┌────────────────┐  ┌────────────────┐            │
  │  │  anihpj-abc    │  │  postgres-0    │  │  redis-xyz     │            │
  │  │  10.244.1.5    │  │  10.244.2.8    │  │  10.244.3.2    │            │
  │  │  (Node 1)      │  │  (Node 2)      │  │  (Node 1)      │            │
  │  └────────────────┘  └────────────────┘  └────────────────┘            │
  │                                                                          │
  │  Rule 2: Pod-to-Pod — direct, no NAT                                    │
  │  anihpj (10.244.1.5) ────────────────────→ postgres (10.244.2.8) ✅    │
  │                       no translation needed!                             │
  │                                                                          │
  │  Rule 3: Node-to-Pod — direct, no NAT                                  │
  │  Node 1 (192.168.1.10) ──────────────────→ postgres (10.244.2.8) ✅    │
  │                          on Node 2, still direct!                        │
  │                                                                          │
  │  Rule 4: Pod sees its own real IP                                       │
  │  Inside anihpj:     hostname -i  →  10.244.1.5                         │
  │  From postgres:     ping anihpj  →  10.244.1.5   ✅ Same IP!          │
  │                                                                          │
  │  ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════     │
  │  CONTRAST: Docker default networking (WITHOUT Kubernetes)                │
  │  Container A → NAT → Host IP → NAT → Container B   ❌ Complex!        │
  │  Container sees 172.17.0.2, outside sees host IP    ❌ Different!      │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
📖 Why This Model Matters

The flat networking model means you can take any application that communicates over TCP/IP and run it in Kubernetes without modifying the code. Your anihpj Django app connects to postgres:5432 exactly the same way whether running locally or in a Kubernetes cluster. No port mapping gymnastics, no NAT tables to debug.

IP Address Ranges in Kubernetes

Kubernetes Network CIDRs

🖥️ Node Network
Physical/VM network: 192.168.1.0/24 — each node gets an IP from your infrastructure
📦 Pod Network (CIDR)
10.244.0.0/16 (default for Flannel) or 192.168.0.0/16 (Calico). Each node gets a /24 subnet.
🔗 Service Network
10.96.0.0/12 (default). Virtual IPs — no real interface, handled by kube-proxy iptables rules.
Inspecting Cluster Network CIDRs 
# Pod CIDR — check kubeadm config or CNI
kubectl cluster-info dump | grep -m 1 cluster-cidr
# --cluster-cidr=10.244.0.0/16

# Service CIDR
kubectl cluster-info dump | grep -m 1 service-cluster-ip-range
# --service-cluster-ip-range=10.96.0.0/12

# Each node's pod subnet
kubectl get nodes -o jsonpath='{range .items[*]}{.metadata.name}{"\t"}{.spec.podCIDR}{"\n"}{end}'
# worker-1    10.244.1.0/24
# worker-2    10.244.2.0/24
# worker-3    10.244.3.0/24

# Pod IPs — verify flat networking
kubectl get pods -o wide
# NAME           READY   IP            NODE
# anihpj-abc     1/1     10.244.1.5    worker-1
# postgres-0     1/1     10.244.2.8    worker-2

17.2 CNI Plugins — How Networking Is Implemented

The Container Network Interface (CNI) is a specification that defines how networking is set up for containers. Kubernetes itself does NOT implement networking — it delegates to a CNI plugin. As a CKA admin, you must know how to identify, install, and troubleshoot the CNI.

CNI Plugin — The Missing Piece
  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  KUBERNETES says: "I need networking. Here's my spec."   │
  │                                                          │
  │  ┌──────────┐     ┌────────────────────────────────┐    │
  │  │ kubelet  │ ──→ │  CNI Plugin (your choice)       │    │
  │  │          │     │                                  │    │
  │  │ "Create  │     │  1. Assign pod IP               │    │
  │  │  network │     │  2. Set up veth pair             │    │
  │  │  for new │     │  3. Configure bridge/routing     │    │
  │  │  pod"    │     │  4. Enable cross-node traffic    │    │
  │  └──────────┘     └────────────────────────────────┘    │
  │                                                          │
  │  Without CNI → pods have NO networking → cluster broken  │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
CNI Plugin Networking Mode Network Policy Performance Best For
Calico BGP (Layer 3) / VXLAN overlay ✅ Full support ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ Production, CKA labs, enterprise
Cilium eBPF (kernel-level) ✅ Advanced (L7) ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ High-performance, observability
Flannel VXLAN overlay (Layer 2) ❌ None ⭐⭐⭐☆☆ Simple clusters, learning
Weave Net Mesh overlay ✅ Basic ⭐⭐⭐☆☆ Easy setup, small clusters
Canal Flannel networking + Calico policies ✅ Full support ⭐⭐⭐⭐☆ Flannel users needing NetworkPolicy
Identifying and Inspecting the CNI Plugin 
# Which CNI is installed? Check the config directory
ls /etc/cni/net.d/
# 10-calico.conflist    ← Calico is installed

# Check CNI pods running
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep -E "calico|cilium|flannel|weave"
# calico-node-abc12    1/1    Running
# calico-node-def34    1/1    Running

# View CNI binary directory
ls /opt/cni/bin/
# calico  calico-ipam  bandwidth  bridge  host-local  loopback  portmap

# Check pod CIDR assigned to each node
kubectl get nodes -o jsonpath='{range .items[*]}{.metadata.name}: {.spec.podCIDR}{"\n"}{end}'

# If CNI pods are crashing — cluster networking is broken!
kubectl describe pod calico-node-abc12 -n kube-system
⚠️ CKA Exam: CNI Must Be Installed

After kubeadm init, nodes show NotReady until you install a CNI. The exam may ask you to install one:

  • Calico: kubectl apply -f https://docs.projectcalico.org/manifests/calico.yaml
  • Flannel: kubectl apply -f https://github.com/flannel-io/flannel/releases/latest/download/kube-flannel.yml
  • If nodes are NotReady — first check if CNI pods exist in kube-system

17.3 Pod-to-Pod Communication

Understanding how packets travel between pods is essential for troubleshooting. The path differs depending on whether pods are on the same node or different nodes.

Same-Node Communication — Via Linux Bridge
  ┌──────────────────────── Node 1 ────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                             │
  │  ┌─────────────────┐          ┌─────────────────┐          │
  │  │ anihpj-abc      │          │ redis-xyz        │          │
  │  │ 10.244.1.5      │          │ 10.244.1.12      │          │
  │  │ eth0 ──────┐    │          │    ┌────── eth0  │          │
  │  └────────────┼────┘          └────┼─────────────┘          │
  │               │  veth pair         │  veth pair              │
  │          ┌────▼────────────────────▼────┐                   │
  │          │       cbr0 / cni0 (bridge)   │                   │
  │          │       10.244.1.1             │                   │
  │          └──────────────────────────────┘                   │
  │                                                             │
  │  Packet path: anihpj eth0 → veth → bridge → veth → redis  │
  │  ✅ Fast — stays entirely within the node kernel           │
  │  ✅ No encapsulation overhead                               │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
Cross-Node Communication — Via Overlay Network (VXLAN/BGP)
  ┌────────── Node 1 ──────────┐         ┌────────── Node 2 ──────────┐
  │                              │         │                              │
  │ ┌─────────────────┐         │         │         ┌─────────────────┐ │
  │ │ anihpj-abc      │         │         │         │ postgres-0      │ │
  │ │ 10.244.1.5      │         │         │         │ 10.244.2.8      │ │
  │ │ eth0            │         │         │         │        eth0     │ │
  │ └──────┬──────────┘         │         │         └────────▲────────┘ │
  │        │ veth                │         │                  │ veth     │
  │ ┌──────▼──────┐             │         │         ┌────────┴────┐    │
  │ │ cbr0 bridge │             │         │         │ cbr0 bridge │    │
  │ │ 10.244.1.1  │             │         │         │ 10.244.2.1  │    │
  │ └──────┬──────┘             │         │         └────────▲────┘    │
  │        │ routing             │         │                  │         │
  │ ┌──────▼──────┐             │         │         ┌────────┴────┐    │
  │ │  eth0 (NIC) │═════════════╪═════════╪═════════│  eth0 (NIC) │    │
  │ │ 192.168.1.10│   physical  │         │         │ 192.168.1.11│    │
  │ └─────────────┘   network   │         │         └─────────────┘    │
  └──────────────────────────────┘         └──────────────────────────────┘

  Packet journey:
  1. anihpj (10.244.1.5) → sends to postgres (10.244.2.8)
  2. Pod eth0 → veth → cbr0 bridge → node routing table
  3. CNI plugin encapsulates packet (VXLAN header or BGP route)
  4. Node 1 eth0 ═══ physical network ═══ Node 2 eth0
  5. Node 2 decapsulates → cbr0 → veth → postgres eth0
  6. postgres receives packet from 10.244.1.5 ✅
🌐
Overlay (VXLAN)

Pod packets wrapped in outer UDP packet. Works anywhere (cloud, bare metal). Slight overhead. Used by: Flannel, Calico (VXLAN mode), Cilium.

⚡
Routing (BGP)

Node routing tables updated to forward pod traffic directly. No encapsulation overhead. Requires L3 network. Used by: Calico (BGP mode).

Testing Pod-to-Pod Connectivity 
# Get pod IPs
kubectl get pods -o wide
# NAME           IP            NODE
# anihpj-abc     10.244.1.5    worker-1
# postgres-0     10.244.2.8    worker-2

# Test direct pod-to-pod (cross-node)
kubectl exec anihpj-abc -- curl -s --max-time 3 http://10.244.2.8:5432
# OR for TCP check
kubectl exec anihpj-abc -- nc -zv 10.244.2.8 5432
# 10.244.2.8 (10.244.2.8:5432) open

# Test same-node connectivity
kubectl exec anihpj-abc -- ping -c 2 10.244.1.12
# PING 10.244.1.12: 64 bytes, time=0.1ms  ← very fast (same node)

17.4 kube-proxy — Service Networking

kube-proxy runs on every node as a DaemonSet. Its job is to implement Service routing — when a pod sends traffic to a Service ClusterIP, kube-proxy's rules redirect that traffic to one of the Service's backend pods.

kube-proxy — How Service ClusterIP Routing Works
  anihpj pod calls: http://anihpj-svc:80   (ClusterIP: 10.96.45.2)

  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  Step 1: Pod sends packet to 10.96.45.2:80 (Service ClusterIP)  │
  │                                                                  │
  │  Step 2: Packet hits iptables/IPVS rules (set by kube-proxy)   │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
  │  │  kube-proxy rules on this node:                          │   │
  │  │                                                          │   │
  │  │  10.96.45.2:80 → DNAT to one of:                        │   │
  │  │    ├── 10.244.1.5:8000  (anihpj-abc on worker-1) 33%   │   │
  │  │    ├── 10.244.2.3:8000  (anihpj-def on worker-2) 33%   │   │
  │  │    └── 10.244.3.7:8000  (anihpj-ghi on worker-3) 33%   │   │
  │  │                                                          │   │
  │  │  Random selection (iptables) or round-robin (IPVS)      │   │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
  │                                                                  │
  │  Step 3: Packet destination rewritten to 10.244.2.3:8000        │
  │          → Normal pod-to-pod routing delivers it                │
  │                                                                  │
  │  Step 4: Response comes back, source NAT reverses               │
  │          Pod sees response from 10.96.45.2 (Service IP)         │
  │                                                                  │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐                    │
  │  │  Key insight: 10.96.45.2 is VIRTUAL     │                    │
  │  │  No network interface has this IP!       │                    │
  │  │  It only exists in iptables/IPVS rules  │                    │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────┘                    │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
kube-proxy Mode How It Works Performance When to Use
iptables Creates iptables NAT rules for each Service/Endpoint. Random backend selection. Good for < 1000 Services Default — most clusters
IPVS Uses Linux kernel IPVS module. Hash-based load balancing (round-robin, least connections, etc.). Excellent for 1000+ Services Large-scale production
nftables Uses nftables (successor to iptables). More efficient rule processing. Better than iptables Newer kernels (K8s 1.29+)
Inspecting kube-proxy 
# Check kube-proxy pods
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-proxy
# NAME              READY   STATUS    NODE
# kube-proxy-abc12  1/1     Running   worker-1
# kube-proxy-def34  1/1     Running   worker-2

# Check kube-proxy mode
kubectl logs -n kube-system kube-proxy-abc12 | grep "Using"
# I0401 Using iptables proxier    ← iptables mode

# View kube-proxy config
kubectl get configmap kube-proxy -n kube-system -o yaml | grep mode
# mode: ""   ← empty means iptables (default)

# View actual iptables rules for a Service (on a node)
# ssh worker-1
iptables -t nat -L KUBE-SERVICES | grep anihpj-svc
# KUBE-SVC-xxx  tcp  --  anywhere  10.96.45.2  tcp dpt:http
💡 CKA Tip: kube-proxy Crash = Services Stop Working

If kube-proxy pods are down, Services still resolve via DNS but traffic can't be routed to pods. Symptoms: DNS lookup works but curl http://service:port hangs. Always check kube-proxy if Service connectivity fails but DNS is fine.

17.5 Network Troubleshooting for Admins

The CKA exam frequently tests your ability to diagnose and fix networking issues. Here's your systematic troubleshooting playbook.

1
Check CNI

Are CNI pods running? kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep calico

2
Check kube-proxy

Is kube-proxy running? kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep proxy

3
Test Pod IP

Can you reach the pod directly? kubectl exec -- curl POD_IP:PORT

4
Test Service IP

Can you reach the Service? kubectl exec -- curl SVC_IP:PORT

5
Test DNS

Does name resolution work? kubectl exec -- nslookup svc-name

Symptom Likely Cause Diagnostic Command Fix
Nodes NotReady CNI not installed or crashing kubectl get pods -n kube-system Install/fix CNI plugin
Pod can't reach pod on other node CNI overlay broken, firewall blocking kubectl exec -- ping <pod-ip> Check CNI pods, node firewall rules
DNS works but Service unreachable kube-proxy down or misconfigured kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-proxy Restart kube-proxy, check ConfigMap
Service returns no endpoints Selector doesn't match pod labels kubectl get endpoints svc-name Fix selector labels to match pods
nslookup fails CoreDNS pods down kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns Check CoreDNS pods and logs
Traffic blocked between pods NetworkPolicy denying traffic kubectl get networkpolicies -A Review/update NetworkPolicy rules
Network Troubleshooting Toolkit 
# 1. Quick connectivity test from a debug pod
kubectl run nettest --image=nicolaka/netshoot --rm -it -- bash

# Inside netshoot pod:
curl -s http://anihpj-svc:80          # Test Service by name
curl -s http://10.96.45.2:80          # Test Service by IP
ping -c 2 10.244.2.8                  # Test pod IP directly
nslookup anihpj-svc                   # Test DNS resolution
nslookup anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local  # Full FQDN
traceroute 10.244.2.8                 # Trace packet path

# 2. Check endpoints (are pods registered with the Service?)
kubectl get endpoints anihpj-svc
# NAME          ENDPOINTS                                    AGE
# anihpj-svc   10.244.1.5:8000,10.244.2.3:8000,10.244.3.7:8000   5m
# If ENDPOINTS is empty → selector doesn't match any pods!

# 3. Verify Service selector matches pod labels
kubectl get svc anihpj-svc -o jsonpath='{.spec.selector}'
# {"app":"anihpj"}
kubectl get pods -l app=anihpj
# Should show your pods — if empty, labels don't match!

# 4. Check if NetworkPolicies are blocking traffic
kubectl get networkpolicies -A
kubectl describe networkpolicy -n anihpj-prod
