Chapter 18: Services Deep Dive

Services are the stable networking abstraction that makes Kubernetes applications accessible. While pods come and go with dynamic IPs, a Service provides a fixed endpoint — a virtual IP and DNS name — that automatically routes traffic to healthy pods. In this chapter you'll master every Service type, understand what happens under the hood, and learn how pods discover each other using our anihpj/jobpost Django stack throughout.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~2 hours 🔗 Services 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ How Services work under the hood — Endpoints, selectors, and kube-proxy integration
  • ✅ ClusterIP — internal-only access (the default and most common type)
  • ✅ NodePort — expose a Service on every node's IP at a static port
  • ✅ LoadBalancer — cloud-provider external load balancers
  • ✅ ExternalName — CNAME-based redirection to external services
  • ✅ Headless Services — direct pod DNS without proxy
  • ✅ Service Discovery — DNS-based and environment-variable methods
🎓 CKA Relevance
Services & Networking 20%
Services Are Heavily Tested on the CKA

Expect 2-3 questions creating/debugging Services. Must-know imperative commands:

  • kubectl expose deployment anihpj --port=80 --target-port=8000 --type=ClusterIP
  • kubectl expose deployment anihpj --port=80 --target-port=8000 --type=NodePort
  • kubectl get svc and kubectl get endpoints — always check both
  • Debug: kubectl describe svc anihpj-svc — verify selector, endpoints, ports

18.1 How Services Work — Under the Hood

A Service is an API object that defines a logical set of pods (via a label selector) and a policy for accessing them. Here's what happens when you create a Service:

Service Creation — The Full Chain
  kubectl apply -f anihpj-svc.yaml
           │
           ▼
  ┌─────────────────────┐
  │    API Server        │  Stores Service object in etcd
  └─────────┬───────────┘
            │
            ▼
  ┌─────────────────────┐
  │  Endpoint Controller │  Watches pods matching selector
  │                      │  Creates Endpoints object:
  │                      │  anihpj-svc → [10.244.1.5:8000,
  │                      │                10.244.2.3:8000]
  └─────────┬───────────┘
            │
            ▼
  ┌─────────────────────┐
  │    kube-proxy        │  Watches Service + Endpoints
  │    (on every node)   │  Creates iptables/IPVS rules:
  │                      │  10.96.45.2:80 → DNAT → pod IPs
  └─────────┬───────────┘
            │
            ▼
  ┌─────────────────────┐
  │      CoreDNS         │  Creates DNS record:
  │                      │  anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local
  │                      │  → 10.96.45.2
  └─────────────────────┘

Anatomy of a Service Object

🏷️ spec.selector
Label selector — determines which pods receive traffic. app: anihpj
📡 spec.ports
port = Service port (what clients use), targetPort = pod port (where app listens)
🔢 spec.clusterIP
Virtual IP assigned from Service CIDR (10.96.0.0/12). Immutable once created.
📋 Endpoints
Auto-populated list of pod IPs matching the selector. Updated on pod add/remove.
🌐 spec.type
ClusterIP (default), NodePort, LoadBalancer, or ExternalName
📖 Key Concept: Port vs TargetPort

port: 80 is what clients connect to on the Service IP. targetPort: 8000 is the container port where your app actually listens. These can be different! Your anihpj Django app listens on 8000, but clients access http://anihpj-svc:80.

18.2 ClusterIP Services

ClusterIP is the default and most common Service type. It assigns a virtual IP only reachable from within the cluster — pods and nodes can access it, but external clients cannot.

✅
When to Use
  • Internal microservice communication
  • Database access (postgres-svc)
  • Cache layer (redis-svc)
  • Backend APIs not exposed externally
❌
Limitations
  • Not accessible from outside the cluster
  • Cannot be reached from your laptop
  • Need NodePort/LoadBalancer/Ingress for external access
anihpj-clusterip-svc.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: anihpj-svc
  namespace: default
  labels:
    app: anihpj
    tier: web
spec:
  type: ClusterIP          # Default — can be omitted
  selector:
    app: anihpj             # Must match pod labels!
  ports:
    - name: http
      protocol: TCP
      port: 80              # Service port (clients connect here)
      targetPort: 8000      # Container port (Django listens here)
    - name: admin
      protocol: TCP
      port: 8080            # Second port — Django admin
      targetPort: 8000
Imperative ClusterIP Creation & Verification 
# Create ClusterIP service (imperative — fast for CKA)
kubectl expose deployment anihpj --name=anihpj-svc \
  --port=80 --target-port=8000 --type=ClusterIP

# Verify Service created
kubectl get svc anihpj-svc
# NAME         TYPE        CLUSTER-IP    EXTERNAL-IP   PORT(S)   AGE
# anihpj-svc  ClusterIP   10.96.45.2    <none>        80/TCP    5s

# Check endpoints — are pods registered?
kubectl get endpoints anihpj-svc
# NAME         ENDPOINTS                        AGE
# anihpj-svc  10.244.1.5:8000,10.244.2.3:8000  5s

# Test from another pod
kubectl exec -it debug-pod -- curl http://anihpj-svc:80
# HTTP 200 — Django responds!

18.3 NodePort Services

NodePort exposes the Service on a static port on every node's IP in the cluster. External clients can access the Service via <NodeIP>:<NodePort>.

NodePort — How External Traffic Reaches Pods
  External client: http://192.168.1.10:30080

  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                 │
  │  External Request → ANY node at port 30080                     │
  │                                                                 │
  │  ┌── Node 1 ──────────────┐    ┌── Node 2 ──────────────┐     │
  │  │  192.168.1.10:30080    │    │  192.168.1.11:30080    │     │
  │  │         │              │    │         │              │     │
  │  │         ▼ kube-proxy   │    │         ▼ kube-proxy   │     │
  │  │  ┌─────────────────┐   │    │  ┌─────────────────┐   │     │
  │  │  │ iptables/IPVS   │   │    │  │ iptables/IPVS   │   │     │
  │  │  │ DNAT to pod     │   │    │  │ DNAT to pod     │   │     │
  │  │  └────────┬────────┘   │    │  └────────┬────────┘   │     │
  │  │           │            │    │           │            │     │
  │  │  ┌────────▼─────────┐  │    │  ┌────────▼─────────┐  │     │
  │  │  │ anihpj-pod-abc   │  │    │  │ anihpj-pod-def   │  │     │
  │  │  │ 10.244.1.5:8000  │  │    │  │ 10.244.2.3:8000  │  │     │
  │  │  └──────────────────┘  │    │  └──────────────────┘  │     │
  │  └────────────────────────┘    └────────────────────────┘     │
  │                                                                 │
  │  Note: Request to Node 1:30080 can be routed to pod on Node 2  │
  │  NodePort range: 30000 - 32767 (configurable in API server)    │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
anihpj-nodeport-svc.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: anihpj-nodeport
spec:
  type: NodePort
  selector:
    app: anihpj
  ports:
    - name: http
      protocol: TCP
      port: 80              # ClusterIP port (internal)
      targetPort: 8000      # Container port
      nodePort: 30080       # External port on every node
                            # Range: 30000-32767
                            # Omit to auto-assign
Working with NodePort Services 
# Create NodePort (imperative)
kubectl expose deployment anihpj --name=anihpj-nodeport \
  --port=80 --target-port=8000 --type=NodePort

# Service gets BOTH a ClusterIP AND a NodePort
kubectl get svc anihpj-nodeport
# NAME              TYPE       CLUSTER-IP    PORT(S)        AGE
# anihpj-nodeport  NodePort   10.96.80.12   80:31245/TCP   5s
#                                            ↑    ↑
#                                            |    └ NodePort (auto-assigned)
#                                            └ ClusterIP port

# Access from outside the cluster
curl http://192.168.1.10:31245     # via Node 1
curl http://192.168.1.11:31245     # via Node 2 — same result!
⚠️ NodePort Limitations
  • Only 1 Service per port (limited range 30000-32767)
  • No TLS termination — clients use HTTP
  • Clients must know node IPs — no load balancing across nodes
  • Not suitable for production — use LoadBalancer or Ingress instead

18.4 LoadBalancer Services

LoadBalancer is the standard way to expose Services to the internet in cloud environments (AWS, GCP, Azure). It provisions an actual external load balancer that distributes traffic across your nodes.

LoadBalancer Service — Cloud Integration
  Internet user: http://203.0.113.50:80

  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                  │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐                    │
  │  │  Cloud Load Balancer (AWS ELB / GCP LB) │                    │
  │  │  External IP: 203.0.113.50              │                    │
  │  │  Provisioned automatically by K8s       │                    │
  │  └────────────┬────────────────┬───────────┘                    │
  │               │                │                                 │
  │     ┌─────────▼────┐    ┌─────▼────────┐                       │
  │     │ Node 1:30080 │    │ Node 2:30080 │   (NodePort used      │
  │     │ (kube-proxy) │    │ (kube-proxy) │    under the hood)    │
  │     └──────┬───────┘    └────┬─────────┘                       │
  │            │                 │                                   │
  │     ┌──────▼──────────┐  ┌──▼──────────────┐                   │
  │     │ anihpj pod-abc  │  │ anihpj pod-def  │                   │
  │     │ 10.244.1.5:8000 │  │ 10.244.2.3:8000 │                   │
  │     └─────────────────┘  └─────────────────┘                   │
  │                                                                  │
  │  LoadBalancer = ClusterIP + NodePort + External LB              │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
anihpj-loadbalancer-svc.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: anihpj-lb
  annotations:
    # Cloud-specific annotations
    service.beta.kubernetes.io/aws-load-balancer-type: nlb   # AWS NLB
spec:
  type: LoadBalancer
  selector:
    app: anihpj
  ports:
    - name: http
      protocol: TCP
      port: 80
      targetPort: 8000
  # Optional: restrict external access by IP
  loadBalancerSourceRanges:
    - 10.0.0.0/8
    - 203.0.113.0/24
Working with LoadBalancer Services 
# Create LoadBalancer service
kubectl apply -f anihpj-loadbalancer-svc.yaml

# Check — EXTERNAL-IP may show <pending> initially
kubectl get svc anihpj-lb -w
# NAME        TYPE           CLUSTER-IP    EXTERNAL-IP     PORT(S)        AGE
# anihpj-lb  LoadBalancer   10.96.90.1    <pending>       80:31890/TCP   5s
# anihpj-lb  LoadBalancer   10.96.90.1    203.0.113.50    80:31890/TCP   30s

# Access from the internet
curl http://203.0.113.50:80
# Django anihpj responds!

# On bare metal without cloud LB — use MetalLB
# EXTERNAL-IP stays <pending> forever without a LB controller
💡 Bare Metal: Use MetalLB

On-premises clusters have no cloud LB provider. MetalLB fills this gap by assigning real external IPs from a configured pool. Often used in CKA lab environments and homelab setups.

18.5 ExternalName Services

ExternalName is a special Service type that maps a Service name to an external DNS name via a CNAME record. No proxying, no ClusterIP — just DNS redirection.

🔀
How It Works

When a pod resolves external-db, CoreDNS returns a CNAME pointing to the external hostname. Traffic goes directly to the external endpoint — kube-proxy is not involved.

📋
Use Cases
  • Point to an external database (RDS, Cloud SQL)
  • Migrate from external to internal service gradually
  • Abstract external dependencies behind K8s DNS
external-db-svc.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: external-db
  namespace: default
spec:
  type: ExternalName
  externalName: mydb.example.com    # External DNS name
  # No selector!
  # No ClusterIP!
  # No ports!
Using ExternalName Services 
# From inside a pod:
nslookup external-db
# external-db.default.svc.cluster.local → CNAME → mydb.example.com

# Django settings.py can just use:
# DATABASES = { 'HOST': 'external-db' }
# If you later migrate to an internal postgres pod, just
# delete the ExternalName service and create a ClusterIP service
# with the same name — zero code changes!

18.6 Headless Services

A Headless Service is a ClusterIP Service with clusterIP: None. Instead of a single virtual IP, DNS resolution returns the individual pod IPs directly. This is essential for StatefulSets where clients need to reach specific pods.

Headless vs Normal Service — DNS Comparison
  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                    │
  │  NORMAL Service (ClusterIP: 10.96.45.2)                          │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐    │
  │  │  nslookup anihpj-svc                                     │    │
  │  │  → 10.96.45.2  (single ClusterIP — kube-proxy routes)   │    │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘    │
  │                                                                    │
  │  HEADLESS Service (clusterIP: None)                               │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐    │
  │  │  nslookup postgres-headless                               │    │
  │  │  → 10.244.1.15  (postgres-0)                             │    │
  │  │  → 10.244.2.18  (postgres-1)                             │    │
  │  │  → 10.244.3.22  (postgres-2)                             │    │
  │  │                                                           │    │
  │  │  PLUS individual pod DNS:                                 │    │
  │  │  postgres-0.postgres-headless.default.svc.cluster.local  │    │
  │  │  → 10.244.1.15                                           │    │
  │  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘    │
  │                                                                    │
  │  Use headless when you need to reach SPECIFIC pods               │
  │  (StatefulSets, database replicas, leader election)              │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
postgres-headless-svc.yaml 
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: postgres-headless
spec:
  clusterIP: None            # ← This makes it headless!
  selector:
    app: postgres
  ports:
    - name: postgres
      port: 5432
      targetPort: 5432
---
# Used with StatefulSet
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: StatefulSet
metadata:
  name: postgres
spec:
  serviceName: postgres-headless    # ← Must reference headless service
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: postgres
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: postgres
    spec:
      containers:
        - name: postgres
          image: postgres:15
          ports:
            - containerPort: 5432
Using Headless Service DNS 
# DNS resolves to ALL pod IPs
kubectl exec debug-pod -- nslookup postgres-headless
# Name: postgres-headless.default.svc.cluster.local
# Address: 10.244.1.15
# Address: 10.244.2.18
# Address: 10.244.3.22

# Access specific pod by ordinal name
kubectl exec debug-pod -- nslookup postgres-0.postgres-headless
# Address: 10.244.1.15    ← always the primary!

# Django connects to primary:
# DATABASES = { 'HOST': 'postgres-0.postgres-headless' }

18.7 Service Discovery — How Pods Find Services

Kubernetes provides two built-in methods for pods to discover Services. DNS is the primary method used in practice.

Method How It Works Pros Cons
DNS (Primary) CoreDNS creates records: svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local Always current, discoverable by name, works across namespaces Requires CoreDNS running
Environment Variables kubelet injects SVC_NAME_SERVICE_HOST and SVC_NAME_SERVICE_PORT into pods Works without DNS, simple Only services created BEFORE the pod; ordering-dependent
Service Discovery in Action 
# Method 1: DNS — the recommended way
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- nslookup anihpj-svc
# Name:   anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local
# Address: 10.96.45.2

# Short names work within the same namespace:
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- curl http://anihpj-svc:80       # ✅ same namespace
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- curl http://anihpj-svc.prod:80  # ✅ "prod" namespace
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- curl http://anihpj-svc.prod.svc.cluster.local:80  # ✅ FQDN

# Method 2: Environment variables
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- env | grep ANIHPJ_SVC
# ANIHPJ_SVC_SERVICE_HOST=10.96.45.2
# ANIHPJ_SVC_SERVICE_PORT=80
# ANIHPJ_SVC_SERVICE_PORT_HTTP=80
DNS Name Resolution Hierarchy
  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │  DNS Resolution for Services                                     │
  │                                                                   │
  │  Short name:     anihpj-svc                                      │
  │                  ↓ (search domain appended)                      │
  │  Within NS:      anihpj-svc.default                              │
  │                  ↓                                                │
  │  Cluster FQDN:   anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local           │
  │                  ↓                                                │
  │  Resolves to:    10.96.45.2 (ClusterIP)                          │
  │                                                                   │
  │  ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════          │
  │                                                                   │
  │  Cross-namespace: anihpj-svc.prod                                │
  │                   ↓                                               │
  │  Cluster FQDN:    anihpj-svc.prod.svc.cluster.local             │
  │                   ↓                                               │
  │  Resolves to:     10.96.88.5 (prod namespace Service)            │
  │                                                                   │
  │  ═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════          │
  │                                                                   │
  │  Pod DNS resolv.conf (auto-configured):                          │
  │  nameserver 10.96.0.10  (CoreDNS ClusterIP)                     │
  │  search default.svc.cluster.local svc.cluster.local cluster.local│
  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

🔑 Chapter 18 Quick Reference

Service Type ClusterIP External Access Use Case
ClusterIP✅ Virtual IP❌ Internal onlyPod-to-pod communication
NodePort✅ Virtual IP✅ via NodeIP:PortDev/test, simple external access
LoadBalancer✅ Virtual IP✅ via cloud LBProduction external access
ExternalName❌ NoneN/A (CNAME only)External service aliasing
Headless❌ None❌ Internal onlyStatefulSets, direct pod access

Chapter 19: DNS and CoreDNS

DNS is the service discovery backbone of Kubernetes. Every time your anihpj pod connects to postgres-svc, a DNS query resolves that name to a ClusterIP. CoreDNS is the cluster DNS server that handles all of this — and knowing how to configure and troubleshoot it is a core CKA skill.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~1 hour 🔍 DNS 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ How Kubernetes DNS works end-to-end — from pod query to CoreDNS response
  • ✅ CoreDNS architecture, Corefile configuration, and plugins
  • ✅ Every type of DNS record Kubernetes creates (A, AAAA, SRV, CNAME)
  • ✅ Systematic DNS troubleshooting — the #1 exam debugging skill
🎓 CKA Relevance
Services & Networking 20% Troubleshooting 30%
DNS Questions Appear on Nearly Every CKA Exam

CoreDNS troubleshooting is one of the most tested topics. Memorize these:

  • CoreDNS pods: kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns
  • CoreDNS ConfigMap: kubectl get configmap coredns -n kube-system -o yaml
  • Test DNS: kubectl exec POD -- nslookup SERVICE_NAME
  • Full FQDN format: svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local

19.1 How Kubernetes DNS Works

Every pod in Kubernetes is automatically configured to use the cluster DNS server (CoreDNS). Here's the complete resolution flow:

DNS Resolution Flow — End to End
  anihpj pod runs: curl http://postgres-svc:5432

  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                      │
  │  Step 1: Pod checks /etc/resolv.conf (auto-configured by kubelet)   │
  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │  nameserver 10.96.0.10                                        │ │
  │  │  search default.svc.cluster.local svc.cluster.local           │ │
  │  │         cluster.local                                          │ │
  │  │  ndots:5                                                       │ │
  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
  │                                                                      │
  │  Step 2: "postgres-svc" has fewer than 5 dots → search path used   │
  │          → Queries: postgres-svc.default.svc.cluster.local          │
  │                                                                      │
  │  Step 3: Query sent to 10.96.0.10 (CoreDNS ClusterIP)             │
  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────┐                    │
  │  │  CoreDNS (kube-system namespace)           │                    │
  │  │  Pod: coredns-abc-123                      │                    │
  │  │  ClusterIP: 10.96.0.10 (kube-dns Service) │                    │
  │  │                                            │                    │
  │  │  Looks up Service "postgres-svc" in        │                    │
  │  │  namespace "default"                        │                    │
  │  │  → Found!  ClusterIP: 10.96.88.5          │                    │
  │  └──────────────────┬─────────────────────────┘                    │
  │                     │                                                │
  │  Step 4: Returns A record: 10.96.88.5                              │
  │                                                                      │
  │  Step 5: Pod connects to 10.96.88.5:5432                           │
  │          → kube-proxy DNAT → actual postgres pod IP                │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

Pod's /etc/resolv.conf — Explained

🔢 nameserver
10.96.0.10 — The ClusterIP of the kube-dns Service in kube-system (points to CoreDNS pods)
🔍 search domains
default.svc.cluster.local — Allows short names like postgres-svc to resolve within the namespace
🔢 ndots:5
If the name has fewer than 5 dots, append search domains first. postgres-svc (0 dots) → search path used. api.example.com (2 dots) → also search path first!
📖 ndots:5 Performance Consideration

The default ndots:5 means external domains like api.example.com generate 4-5 DNS queries before the real one succeeds. For performance-sensitive apps, you can override this in the pod spec with dnsConfig: { options: [{ name: ndots, value: "2" }] }.

19.2 CoreDNS Configuration

CoreDNS is deployed as a Deployment in the kube-system namespace, typically with 2 replicas for high availability. Its configuration lives in a ConfigMap called coredns.

CoreDNS Architecture in Kubernetes
  ┌──────────────────── kube-system namespace ────────────────────┐
  │                                                                │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │  Deployment: coredns (replicas: 2)                       │ │
  │  │  ┌─────────────────┐   ┌─────────────────┐              │ │
  │  │  │ coredns-abc-111 │   │ coredns-abc-222 │              │ │
  │  │  │ (Pod 1)         │   │ (Pod 2)         │              │ │
  │  │  └────────┬────────┘   └────────┬────────┘              │ │
  │  └───────────┼─────────────────────┼────────────────────────┘ │
  │              │                     │                           │
  │  ┌───────────▼─────────────────────▼────────────────────────┐ │
  │  │  Service: kube-dns (ClusterIP: 10.96.0.10)              │ │
  │  │  Ports: 53/UDP, 53/TCP, 9153/TCP (metrics)              │ │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
  │                                                                │
  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
  │  │  ConfigMap: coredns                                      │  │
  │  │  Contains: Corefile (plugin configuration)               │  │
  │  │  Mounted at: /etc/coredns/Corefile                       │  │
  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
The Default Corefile — Annotated 
# kubectl get configmap coredns -n kube-system -o yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: ConfigMap
metadata:
  name: coredns
  namespace: kube-system
data:
  Corefile: |
    .:53 {                          # Listen on port 53 for all zones
        errors                       # Log errors to stdout
        health {                     # Health check endpoint on :8080
            lameduck 5s              # Wait 5s before shutting down
        }
        ready                        # Readiness probe on :8181
        kubernetes cluster.local in-addr.arpa ip6.arpa {
            pods insecure            # Enable pod DNS records
            fallthrough in-addr.arpa ip6.arpa
            ttl 30                   # Cache TTL: 30 seconds
        }
        prometheus :9153             # Metrics endpoint for monitoring
        forward . /etc/resolv.conf {  # Forward external queries to
            max_concurrent 1000       # upstream DNS (node's resolv.conf)
        }
        cache 30                     # Cache all responses for 30s
        loop                         # Detect and stop DNS loops
        reload                       # Auto-reload Corefile on change
        loadbalance                  # Round-robin A/AAAA records
    }
Common CoreDNS Customizations 
# Add a custom DNS entry (stub domain)
# Edit the ConfigMap to forward *.example.com to a specific DNS server
kubectl edit configmap coredns -n kube-system

# Add this block inside the Corefile:
#   example.com:53 {
#       forward . 10.0.0.1
#   }

# Add a static host entry (hosts plugin)
# Add inside the main server block, before kubernetes:
#   hosts {
#       10.10.10.50 mydb.internal
#       fallthrough
#   }

# After editing — CoreDNS auto-reloads (reload plugin)
# Verify: check CoreDNS logs
kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns --tail=20
⚠️ CKA Exam: Never Delete the CoreDNS ConfigMap

If you're asked to modify DNS configuration, use kubectl edit configmap coredns -n kube-system. The reload plugin means changes take effect automatically within 30 seconds. Do NOT restart CoreDNS pods unless explicitly required — they auto-reload.

19.3 DNS Records in Kubernetes

Kubernetes automatically creates DNS records for Services and (optionally) Pods. Understanding these record formats is essential for cross-namespace communication.

Record Type DNS Name Format Resolves To Example
A Record (Service) svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local ClusterIP anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local → 10.96.45.2
A Record (Headless) svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local All Pod IPs postgres-headless.default.svc.cluster.local → 10.244.1.15, 10.244.2.18
A Record (Pod via Headless) pod-name.svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local Specific Pod IP postgres-0.postgres-headless.default.svc.cluster.local → 10.244.1.15
SRV Record _port-name._protocol.svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local Port + hostname _http._tcp.anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local → 0 10 80 ...
CNAME (ExternalName) svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local External hostname external-db.default.svc.cluster.local → mydb.example.com
A Record (Pod direct) pod-ip-dashed.namespace.pod.cluster.local Pod IP 10-244-1-5.default.pod.cluster.local → 10.244.1.5
Exploring All DNS Record Types 
# Launch a DNS debug pod
kubectl run dnstest --image=busybox:1.36 --rm -it -- sh

# A record — Service ClusterIP
nslookup anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local
# Address: 10.96.45.2

# A records — Headless Service (returns all pod IPs)
nslookup postgres-headless.default.svc.cluster.local
# Address: 10.244.1.15
# Address: 10.244.2.18

# A record — specific pod via headless service
nslookup postgres-0.postgres-headless.default.svc.cluster.local
# Address: 10.244.1.15

# SRV record — discover port and protocol
nslookup -type=SRV _http._tcp.anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local
# service = 0 10 80 anihpj-svc.default.svc.cluster.local

# Pod DNS (IP dashes)
nslookup 10-244-1-5.default.pod.cluster.local
# Address: 10.244.1.5

# Cross-namespace resolution
nslookup anihpj-svc.prod.svc.cluster.local
# Address: 10.96.88.5  (Service in "prod" namespace)
💡 Short Names vs FQDN

Within the same namespace, use short names: anihpj-svc or anihpj-svc:80. Across namespaces, include the namespace: anihpj-svc.prod. Full FQDNs are rarely needed in practice but may appear on the CKA exam.

19.4 Troubleshooting DNS Issues

DNS failures are one of the most common Kubernetes problems — and one of the most tested on the CKA. Here's a systematic approach to diagnose and fix DNS issues.

1
Is CoreDNS Running?

kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns — both pods should be Running

2
Is the kube-dns Service Up?

kubectl get svc kube-dns -n kube-system — should have ClusterIP 10.96.0.10

3
Can Pods Reach CoreDNS?

kubectl exec POD -- nslookup kubernetes.default — tests basic DNS connectivity

4
Check CoreDNS Logs

kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns — look for errors

5
Check Corefile Config

kubectl get cm coredns -n kube-system -o yaml — verify configuration is correct

DNS Symptom Likely Cause Diagnostic Fix
nslookup times out CoreDNS pods not running kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns Check CoreDNS deployment, restart pods
SERVFAIL response Corefile misconfiguration kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns Fix Corefile syntax in ConfigMap
NXDOMAIN — name not found Service doesn't exist or wrong namespace kubectl get svc -A | grep svc-name Create the Service or use correct namespace prefix
External domains fail forward plugin pointing to wrong upstream Check forward . /etc/resolv.conf in Corefile Fix upstream DNS (e.g., forward . 8.8.8.8)
DNS works in some pods, not others Pod's dnsPolicy is set to Default kubectl get pod POD -o yaml | grep dnsPolicy Change to dnsPolicy: ClusterFirst (the default)
DNS loop detected Node's /etc/resolv.conf points to a local DNS that forwards back to CoreDNS CoreDNS logs: "Loop ... detected" Change forward . /etc/resolv.conf to forward . 8.8.8.8 8.8.4.4
DNS Troubleshooting Playbook 
# Step 1: Check CoreDNS pods
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns
# NAME                     READY   STATUS    RESTARTS   AGE
# coredns-abc-111          1/1     Running   0          5d
# coredns-abc-222          1/1     Running   0          5d

# Step 2: Check kube-dns Service
kubectl get svc kube-dns -n kube-system
# NAME       TYPE        CLUSTER-IP   PORT(S)
# kube-dns   ClusterIP   10.96.0.10   53/UDP,53/TCP,9153/TCP

# Step 3: Test DNS from a debug pod
kubectl run dnsdiag --image=busybox:1.36 --rm -it -- sh
nslookup kubernetes.default     # Should resolve to API server ClusterIP
nslookup anihpj-svc             # Should resolve to Service ClusterIP
nslookup google.com             # Should resolve (tests external forwarding)

# Step 4: Check CoreDNS logs for errors
kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns --tail=50
# Look for: "SERVFAIL", "i/o timeout", "Loop detected"

# Step 5: Verify pod resolv.conf
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- cat /etc/resolv.conf
# nameserver 10.96.0.10
# search default.svc.cluster.local svc.cluster.local cluster.local

# Step 6: Check the Corefile
kubectl get configmap coredns -n kube-system -o yaml

🔑 Chapter 19 Quick Reference

Action Command
Check CoreDNS podskubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns
Check kube-dns Servicekubectl get svc kube-dns -n kube-system
Test DNS resolutionkubectl exec POD -- nslookup SERVICE
View CoreDNS configkubectl get cm coredns -n kube-system -o yaml
Edit CoreDNS configkubectl edit cm coredns -n kube-system
CoreDNS logskubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns
Pod DNS configkubectl exec POD -- cat /etc/resolv.conf
Service FQDN formatsvc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local
Pod FQDN (headless)pod-name.svc-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local

Chapter 20: Ingress and Network Policies

While Services handle internal routing, Ingress manages external HTTP/HTTPS traffic with path-based routing, TLS termination, and virtual hosts. Network Policies act as a firewall for pod-to-pod traffic — controlling which pods can talk to each other. Together, these are the most heavily tested networking topics on the CKA exam.

🟡 Intermediate ⏱️ ~2 hours 🛡️ Network Security 📝 CKA EXAM

🎯 What You'll Learn

  • ✅ Ingress — HTTP/HTTPS routing with path and host rules
  • ✅ Setting up an Ingress Controller (NGINX) in a cluster
  • ✅ Network Policies — the pod-level firewall for zero-trust networking
  • ✅ Policy types: Ingress (incoming), Egress (outgoing), and combined
  • ✅ Real-world YAML patterns for common Network Policy scenarios
  • ✅ Testing and verifying Network Policy enforcement
🎓 CKA Relevance
Services & Networking 20%
Ingress + NetworkPolicy = High-Value CKA Points

These are among the highest-weighted questions on the CKA. Tips:

  • Know the Ingress YAML by heart — the exam won't have auto-complete for all fields
  • NetworkPolicy: remember that empty podSelector {} selects ALL pods, and an empty ingress: [{}] allows ALL traffic
  • If asked to "deny all traffic" — apply a NetworkPolicy with podSelector: {} and policyTypes: [Ingress, Egress] with empty rules
  • An Ingress Controller must be running for Ingress resources to work

20.1 Ingress — HTTP/HTTPS Routing

An Ingress is an API object that manages external HTTP/HTTPS access to Services. It provides path-based routing, host-based routing, and TLS termination — all features that NodePort/LoadBalancer Services lack.

Ingress — How External HTTP Traffic Is Routed
  Internet user: https://anihpj.example.com/api/jobs/
                 https://anihpj.example.com/admin/

  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │                                                                  │
  │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐       │
  │  │  Ingress Controller (NGINX / Traefik / HAProxy)      │       │
  │  │  Deployed as a pod, exposed via LoadBalancer/NodePort │       │
  │  │  Reads Ingress rules from API server                 │       │
  │  └───────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────┘       │
  │                      │                                           │
  │          ┌───────────┼────────────┐                             │
  │          │           │            │                              │
  │   path: /api/*  path: /admin/*  path: /static/*                │
  │          │           │            │                              │
  │   ┌──────▼─────┐ ┌──▼────────┐ ┌─▼───────────┐                │
  │   │ anihpj-svc │ │ admin-svc │ │  static-svc │                │
  │   │ port: 80   │ │ port: 80  │ │  port: 80   │                │
  │   └──────┬─────┘ └──┬────────┘ └─┬───────────┘                │
  │          │           │            │                              │
  │   ┌──────▼─────┐ ┌──▼────────┐ ┌─▼───────────┐                │
  │   │ anihpj     │ │ anihpj    │ │  nginx      │                │
  │   │ pods       │ │ pods      │ │  pods       │                │
  │   └────────────┘ └───────────┘ └─────────────┘                │
  │                                                                  │
  │  Benefits over NodePort/LoadBalancer:                           │
  │  ✅ Single external IP for multiple services                   │
  │  ✅ Path-based and host-based routing                          │
  │  ✅ TLS termination at the edge                                │
  │  ✅ Centralized SSL certificate management                     │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
anihpj-ingress.yaml 
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: anihpj-ingress
  namespace: default
  annotations:
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rewrite-target: /
spec:
  ingressClassName: nginx         # Which Ingress Controller to use
  tls:                             # TLS termination
    - hosts:
        - anihpj.example.com
      secretName: anihpj-tls       # TLS cert stored in a Secret
  rules:
    - host: anihpj.example.com     # Host-based routing
      http:
        paths:
          - path: /api              # Path-based routing
            pathType: Prefix        # Prefix or Exact
            backend:
              service:
                name: anihpj-svc   # Route to this Service
                port:
                  number: 80
          - path: /admin
            pathType: Prefix
            backend:
              service:
                name: anihpj-svc
                port:
                  number: 80
    - host: docs.example.com       # Different host → different Service
      http:
        paths:
          - path: /
            pathType: Prefix
            backend:
              service:
                name: docs-svc
                port:
                  number: 80
pathType Match Behavior Example
Prefix Matches URL path prefix by / segments /api matches /api, /api/, /api/jobs
Exact Exact URL path match (case-sensitive) /api matches only /api, NOT /api/
ImplementationSpecific Depends on the IngressClass / controller Behavior varies — avoid on exam
Working with Ingress Resources 
# Apply the Ingress
kubectl apply -f anihpj-ingress.yaml

# Check Ingress — look for ADDRESS (external IP)
kubectl get ingress
# NAME             CLASS   HOSTS                  ADDRESS        PORTS     AGE
# anihpj-ingress  nginx   anihpj.example.com     203.0.113.50   80, 443   5s

# Describe for detailed routing info
kubectl describe ingress anihpj-ingress
# Rules:
#   Host                  Path  Backends
#   ----                  ----  --------
#   anihpj.example.com
#                         /api    anihpj-svc:80 (10.244.1.5:8000,10.244.2.3:8000)
#                         /admin  anihpj-svc:80 (10.244.1.5:8000,10.244.2.3:8000)

# Create TLS Secret for HTTPS
kubectl create secret tls anihpj-tls \
  --cert=tls.crt --key=tls.key -n default

20.2 Ingress Controller Setup

An Ingress resource is just configuration — it does nothing without an Ingress Controller. The controller is the actual reverse proxy (NGINX, Traefik, HAProxy) that reads Ingress rules and routes traffic.

📦
Ingress Resource

The rules — path routing, TLS config, host rules. Just a YAML API object. Does nothing by itself.

⚙️
Ingress Controller

The implementation — NGINX/Traefik pod that reads rules and configures the reverse proxy. Must be installed separately.

Ingress Controller Provider Features Best For
NGINX Ingress Kubernetes community Path/host routing, TLS, rate limiting, auth CKA exam, most clusters
Traefik Traefik Labs Auto-discovery, Let's Encrypt, middleware Dynamic environments
HAProxy HAProxy Technologies High performance, TCP/HTTP load balancing High-traffic production
AWS ALB AWS AWS Application Load Balancer integration EKS clusters
Installing NGINX Ingress Controller 
# Install NGINX Ingress Controller (bare metal / kubeadm)
kubectl apply -f https://raw.githubusercontent.com/kubernetes/ingress-nginx/controller-v1.10.0/deploy/static/provider/baremetal/deploy.yaml

# Verify the controller is running
kubectl get pods -n ingress-nginx
# NAME                                       READY   STATUS
# ingress-nginx-controller-abc12-xyz         1/1     Running

# Check the IngressClass
kubectl get ingressclass
# NAME    CONTROLLER                           PARAMETERS   AGE
# nginx   k8s.io/ingress-nginx                 <none>       30s

# Verify the controller Service
kubectl get svc -n ingress-nginx
# NAME                        TYPE       EXTERNAL-IP   PORT(S)
# ingress-nginx-controller    NodePort   <none>        80:31080/TCP,443:31443/TCP
⚠️ CKA Exam: Ingress Controller Must Be Running

If the exam asks you to create an Ingress resource and it's not working, first check if an Ingress Controller is installed: kubectl get pods -n ingress-nginx. An Ingress resource without a controller does nothing — the ADDRESS field will be empty.

20.3 Network Policies — Firewall for Pods

By default, Kubernetes allows all pods to communicate with all other pods — no restrictions. Network Policies let you define fine-grained rules controlling which pods can send traffic to which other pods, implementing a zero-trust network.

Default Behavior vs Network Policy Applied
  ┌──────────────────────── DEFAULT (No NetworkPolicy) ──────────────┐
  │                                                                   │
  │  anihpj ─────────→ postgres  ✅                                  │
  │  anihpj ─────────→ redis     ✅                                  │
  │  random-pod ─────→ postgres  ✅  ← anyone can access DB!        │
  │  random-pod ─────→ anihpj   ✅  ← no isolation at all          │
  │                                                                   │
  │  EVERYTHING is allowed. Any pod talks to any pod.                │
  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ┌──────────────── WITH NetworkPolicy on postgres ──────────────────┐
  │                                                                   │
  │  NetworkPolicy: "only pods with app=anihpj can reach postgres"  │
  │                                                                   │
  │  anihpj ─────────→ postgres  ✅  (label matches)                │
  │  random-pod ─────→ postgres  ❌  BLOCKED                        │
  │  redis ──────────→ postgres  ❌  BLOCKED                        │
  │                                                                   │
  │  anihpj ─────────→ redis     ✅  (no policy on redis)           │
  │  random-pod ─────→ anihpj   ✅  (no policy on anihpj)          │
  │                                                                   │
  │  ⚠️ Policy only affects the TARGET pod (podSelector)            │
  │  ⚠️ Pods with NO policy → still fully open                     │
  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
🛡️
Key Concepts
  • Policies are additive — multiple policies are OR'd together
  • Applying ANY policy to a pod makes it deny by default
  • No policy = all traffic allowed (default open)
  • Requires CNI with NetworkPolicy support (Calico, Cilium)
⚠️
Critical Requirement

Flannel does NOT support NetworkPolicies. If you apply a NetworkPolicy with Flannel, it's silently ignored. The CKA exam uses Calico or Cilium — both support NetworkPolicy.

20.4 Network Policy Types

Network Policies have two directions: Ingress (incoming traffic TO the pods) and Egress (outgoing traffic FROM the pods). You can control both independently.

Policy Type Direction Controls Example
Ingress Incoming → to selected pods Who can SEND traffic to these pods Only anihpj pods can reach postgres on port 5432
Egress Outgoing ← from selected pods Where these pods can SEND traffic anihpj pods can only reach postgres and DNS
Both Bidirectional Controls both directions Fully locked-down pods — explicit allow required

Network Policy Selectors — Who Can Talk?

📦 podSelector
Match pods by labels in the same namespace. matchLabels: { app: anihpj }
📁 namespaceSelector
Match all pods in namespaces with specific labels. matchLabels: { env: prod }
🌐 ipBlock
Match traffic from/to specific IP ranges. cidr: 10.0.0.0/8, except: [10.0.1.0/24]

20.5 Network Policy YAML Patterns

Here are the most common Network Policy patterns you'll encounter on the CKA exam, using our anihpj stack as context.

Pattern 1: Deny All Ingress (Default Deny)

deny-all-ingress.yaml 
# Deny ALL incoming traffic to ALL pods in "default" namespace
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: deny-all-ingress
  namespace: default
spec:
  podSelector: {}          # Empty = selects ALL pods in namespace
  policyTypes:
    - Ingress              # Only control incoming traffic
  # ingress: []            # Omitted = no traffic allowed in
                           # (there are no "allow" rules)

Pattern 2: Allow Specific Pod Access

allow-anihpj-to-postgres.yaml 
# Only anihpj pods can reach postgres on port 5432
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: allow-anihpj-to-postgres
  namespace: default
spec:
  podSelector:              # Target: postgres pods
    matchLabels:
      app: postgres
  policyTypes:
    - Ingress
  ingress:
    - from:                  # Allow FROM:
        - podSelector:       # Pods with these labels
            matchLabels:
              app: anihpj
      ports:                 # On these ports only
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 5432

Pattern 3: Allow from Specific Namespace

allow-from-monitoring-ns.yaml 
# Allow monitoring namespace to scrape all pods in default namespace
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: allow-monitoring
  namespace: default
spec:
  podSelector: {}           # All pods in default
  policyTypes:
    - Ingress
  ingress:
    - from:
        - namespaceSelector:
            matchLabels:
              name: monitoring    # Namespace must have this label!

Pattern 4: Egress — Restrict Outgoing Traffic

anihpj-egress-policy.yaml 
# anihpj pods can only reach postgres and DNS
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: anihpj-egress
  namespace: default
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: anihpj
  policyTypes:
    - Egress
  egress:
    - to:                    # Allow TO postgres
        - podSelector:
            matchLabels:
              app: postgres
      ports:
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 5432
    - to: []                 # Allow DNS (required!)
      ports:
        - protocol: UDP
          port: 53
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 53

Pattern 5: Deny All Ingress + Egress (Total Lockdown)

deny-all-traffic.yaml 
# Complete isolation — no traffic in or out
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: deny-all
  namespace: default
spec:
  podSelector: {}           # All pods
  policyTypes:
    - Ingress
    - Egress
  # No ingress or egress rules = deny everything
⚠️ CKA Gotcha: AND vs OR Logic in Network Policies

Within a single from block, multiple selectors are ANDed. Separate - from: entries are ORed.

AND vs OR — Critical Difference 
# AND — pod must match BOTH selectors
ingress:
  - from:
      - namespaceSelector:          # AND
          matchLabels: { env: prod }
        podSelector:                # AND (same list item!)
          matchLabels: { app: anihpj }

# OR — pod matches EITHER selector
ingress:
  - from:
      - namespaceSelector:          # OR
          matchLabels: { env: prod }
      - podSelector:                # OR (separate list item!)
          matchLabels: { app: anihpj }

20.6 Testing Network Policies

Always verify your Network Policies work as expected. Here's a systematic testing approach using our anihpj stack.

Testing Network Policy Enforcement 
# Step 1: Apply the "allow-anihpj-to-postgres" policy
kubectl apply -f allow-anihpj-to-postgres.yaml

# Step 2: Verify policy is created
kubectl get networkpolicies
# NAME                        POD-SELECTOR     AGE
# allow-anihpj-to-postgres   app=postgres     5s

# Step 3: Describe the policy for details
kubectl describe networkpolicy allow-anihpj-to-postgres
# Allowing ingress traffic:
#   To Port: 5432/TCP
#   From:
#     PodSelector: app=anihpj

# Step 4: Test ALLOWED traffic (anihpj → postgres)
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- nc -zv postgres-svc 5432
# postgres-svc (10.96.88.5:5432) open   ✅ ALLOWED

# Step 5: Test BLOCKED traffic (random pod → postgres)
kubectl run test-blocked --image=busybox --rm -it -- nc -zv postgres-svc 5432
# → hangs/timeout  ❌ BLOCKED!   (This pod has no "app: anihpj" label)

# Step 6: Test that non-policy pods are still open
kubectl exec anihpj-pod -- curl -s http://redis-svc:6379
# ✅ Works — redis has no NetworkPolicy, so it's still fully open
1
List Policies

kubectl get networkpolicies -A

2
Check Target Pods

kubectl describe netpol POLICY — verify podSelector matches

3
Test Allowed Path

kubectl exec ALLOWED-POD -- nc -zv TARGET PORT

4
Test Blocked Path

kubectl run test -- nc -zv TARGET PORT — should timeout

5
Verify CNI Support

If nothing is blocked — CNI may not support policies (Flannel)
